The Dispute Over the Fifth Type of Proposition
Footnote on a proposed fifth type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the controversy regarding the classification of natural propositions and evaluate whether a fifth type is needed.

Core Idea
Some logicians propose a fifth type, but the mainstream view maintains only four.

The footnote in our passage introduces a dispute: some scholars call natural propositions 'universal' and add a fifth type like 'man is a rational animal'. This lesson unpacks that controversy and its implications for the classification of propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base text claim
The author defines natural propositions as those that are not suitable for universal/particular truth, e.g., 'animal is a genus'.
This is the standard view.
2
Understand the dissenting view
Some scholars argue that such propositions should be called 'universal' ('amma) because the subject is the nature with generality.
They give a different example: 'man is a rational animal'.
3
Analyze the difference
The dissenters interpret the subject as the nature taken with the condition of generality, not as individuals. They thus add a fifth type of proposition.
The original author does not accept this additional type; it remains a footnote.
4
Evaluate the need for a fifth type
Consider whether 'man is a rational animal' is truly different from 'animal is a genus' in terms of predication.
Both seem to be about the nature, so the addition may be redundant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وإن لم يبين فيها كمية الأفراد فإن لم تصلح لأن تصدق كلّيّة وجزئية سميت القضية «طبيعية»؛ كقولنا: الحيوان جنس» و «الإنسان نوع - لأن الحكم فيها على نفس الطبيعة - وإن صلحت لذلك سميت «مهملة» كقولنا : «الإنسان في «خسر و الإنسان ليس في خسر». القول : ما مرّ كان إذا بين في القضيّة كمية أفراد الموضوع، وأما إذا لم يبين : فلا يخلو إما أن تصلح القضيّة لأن تصدق كلية وجزئية - بأن يكون الحكم فيها على أفراد الموضوع - أو لم تصلح - بأن يكون الحكم على طبيعة الموضوع نفسها لاعلى الأفراد - : فإن لم تصلح لأن تصدق كلّيّة وجزئية سميت «طبيعية» لأن الحكم فيها على نفس الطبيعة، كقولنا : الحيوان جنس» و «الإنسان نوع (۱) زعم بعضهم أن مثل هذه القضايا تسمّى «عامة» لأن الموضوع فيها هو الطبيعة بقيد العموم، فإن «الحيوان» من حيث أنه عام موصوف بالجنسية و«الإنسان» بقيد عمومه موصوف بالنوعية ؛ ومثلوا للطبيعية بنحو قولنا : «الإنسان حيوان ناطق»، فزادوا في القضايا قسماً خامساً.
He said: And if the quantity of individuals is not specified in it, then if it is not suitable to be true both universally and particularly, the proposition is called 'natural' (tabi'iyya), like our saying 'animal is a genus' and 'man is a species'—because the judgment in it is upon the nature itself. And if it is suitable for that, it is called 'indefinite' (muhmala), like our saying 'man is in loss' and 'man is not in loss'. The discourse: What has passed was when the quantity of individuals of the subject is specified in the proposition. But if it is not specified: then it is either that the proposition is suitable to be true both universally and particularly—by the judgment being upon the individuals of the subject—or not suitable—by the judgment being upon the nature of the subject itself, not upon the individuals. Then if it is not suitable to be true both universally and particularly, it is called 'natural' because the judgment in it is upon the nature itself, like our saying 'animal is a genus' and 'man is a species'. (1) Some claimed that such propositions are called 'universal' ('amma) because the subject in them is the nature with the condition of generality, for 'animal' insofar as it is general is described as genus, and 'man' with its generality described as species; and they gave as an example of a natural proposition something like 'man is a rational animal', thus adding a fifth type to the propositions.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage defines two types of categorical propositions when the quantifier (quantity of individuals) is not explicitly stated: natural (tabi'iyya) and indefinite (muhmala). First, the author explains that if the proposition is not suitable to be true as both universal and particular, it is called natural. For example, 'animal is a genus' — this statement is only true about the nature of animal as a genus, not about each individual animal (since not every animal is a genus). Similarly, 'man is a species' is true about the nature of man, not about each individual man. The judgment in such propositions is about the essence itself, not about the individuals. Second, if the proposition can be true either as universal or as particular (i.e., it is ambiguous), it is called indefinite. Example: 'man is in loss' (from Quran, Surah al-Asr) — this could be interpreted as all men or some men are in loss. The indefinite proposition does not specify quantity, so it may be applied universally or particularly. The footnote mentions that some scholars call natural propositions 'universal' ('amma) because the subject is the nature taken with generality, and they add a fifth type of proposition (like 'man is a rational animal') as an example of natural. However, the main text's distinction is clear: natural propositions predicate of the quiddity, while indefinite propositions predicate of individuals without specifying quantity.

Key Takeaway
The footnote reveals a terminological dispute, but the mainstream position maintains that natural propositions are about the essence and do not require a separate category.

Distinguishing Natural and Indefinite Propositions
Author defines natural and indefinite propositions and distinguishes them by the object of judgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any unspecified-quantity proposition as natural (ṭabi‘iyya) or indefinite (muhmala) by examining whether the judgment applies to the nature itself or to the individuals.

Core Idea
Natural propositions judge the nature itself; indefinite propositions judge individuals without specifying quantity.

In categorical propositions, quantity is sometimes left unspecified. The logical tradition distinguishes two types: natural propositions, where the judgment is on the essence of the subject, and indefinite propositions, where the judgment applies to individuals but the quantity is omitted. Learning this distinction prevents misinterpretation of statements in theology, jurisprudence, and dialectics.

Guided Path
1
1. Check if quantity is specified
If the proposition explicitly states 'all', 'some', etc., it is universal or particular. If not, it falls into the natural or indefinite category.
2
2. Determine the object of judgment
Ask: does the predicate apply to the subject's nature (essence) or to the actual individuals that fall under the subject? In natural propositions, the judgment is on the nature itself; in indefinite, it is on the individuals.
Example: 'The animal is a genus' judges the nature of animal, not individual animals.
3
3. Test for universal/particular truth
If the proposition cannot be meaningfully true as a universal or particular statement, it is natural. If it can (but the quantity is unspecified), it is indefinite.
4
4. Classify
Label the proposition as 'ṭabi‘iyya' or 'muhmala' based on the above criteria.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وإن لم يبين فيها كمية الأفراد فإن لم تصلح لأن تصدق كلّيّة وجزئية سميت القضية «طبيعية»؛ كقولنا: الحيوان جنس» و «الإنسان نوع - لأن الحكم فيها على نفس الطبيعة - وإن صلحت لذلك سميت «مهملة» كقولنا : «الإنسان في «خسر و الإنسان ليس في خسر».
القول : ما مرّ كان إذا بين في القضيّة كمية أفراد الموضوع، وأما إذا لم يبين : فلا يخلو إما أن تصلح القضيّة لأن تصدق كلية وجزئية - بأن يكون الحكم فيها على أفراد الموضوع - أو لم تصلح - بأن يكون الحكم على طبيعة الموضوع نفسها لاعلى الأفراد - :
فإن لم تصلح لأن تصدق كلّيّة وجزئية سميت «طبيعية» لأن الحكم فيها على نفس الطبيعة، كقولنا : الحيوان جنس» و «الإنسان نوع»
فإن الحكم بالجنسية والنوعية ليس على ما صدق عليه الحيوان والإنسان من الأفراد، بل على نفس طبيعتهما.
وإن صلحت لأن تصدق كلّيّة وجزئية سميت «مهملة» لأن الحكم فيها على أفراد موضوعها، وقد أهمل بيان كمّيتها كقولنا : «الإنسان في خسر» و «الإنسان ليس في خسر أي ما صدق عليه «الإنسان» من الأفراد في خسر، وليس في خسر.
He said: And if the quantity of individuals is not specified in it, then if it cannot be true as universal and particular, the proposition is called 'natural'; like our saying: 'The animal is a genus' and 'Man is a species' – because the judgment in it is upon the nature itself. And if it is suitable for that, it is called 'indefinite', like our saying: 'Man is in loss' and 'Man is not in loss'.
The statement: What has passed was when the quantity of the individuals of the subject is specified in the proposition. But when it is not specified: then either the proposition can be true as universal and particular – by the judgment being upon the individuals of the subject – or not – by the judgment being upon the nature of the subject itself, not upon the individuals.
If it cannot be true as universal and particular, it is called 'natural' because the judgment is upon the nature itself, like our saying: 'The animal is a genus' and 'Man is a species'.
For the judgment of genus-hood and species-hood is not upon what the terms animal and man apply to of individuals, but upon their very natures.
And if it is suitable to be true as universal and particular, it is called 'indefinite' because the judgment is upon the individuals of its subject, and its quantity has been neglected, like our saying: 'Man is in loss' and 'Man is not in loss' – that is, whatever individuals are denoted by 'man' are in loss, and not in loss.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes two types of categorical propositions where the quantity of the subject is not explicitly stated: natural (ṭabi‘iyya) and indefinite (muhmala). The author begins by stating the criterion: if the proposition cannot be true as both a universal and a particular (i.e., it does not apply to individuals at all), then it is natural. The judgment here is on the very nature (ṭabī‘a) of the subject, not on its individual instances. Examples given are 'al-ḥayawān jins' (Animal is a genus) and 'al-insān naw‘' (Man is a species). The predication of genus-hood or species-hood is not about individual animals or men but about the universal nature itself. On the other hand, if the proposition can be true as universal or particular (i.e., it applies to individuals), then it is called indefinite, because the quantity is omitted. The example is 'al-insān fī khusr' (Man is in loss) – meaning every individual man is in loss, or some, but the quantity is not specified. The commentary clarifies the underlying principle: when the quantity is unspecified, the proposition falls into one of two categories based on whether the judgment is on the nature or on the individuals.

Key Takeaway

The Four Types of Categorical Propositions and the Natural Proposition Refinement
Author enumerates the four types of categorical propositions and refutes a fifth type (natural).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of categorical propositions and explain why natural propositions are not a separate type.

Core Idea
Natural propositions are not a fifth type; they are universal propositions in disguise.

In this passage, the author establishes the rule for dividing categorical propositions by the subject. The accepted classification has four types: personal (subject is a particular), universal, particular, and indefinite. However, some have suggested a fifth type called 'natural proposition' (e.g., 'al-ḥayawān jins' – Animal is a genus). The commentator (Sharif) refines this view, arguing that natural propositions are actually universal propositions because the judgment applies to the nature itself, even though the condition for the predicate's inherence (e.g., universality) need not be expressed in the logical form. This lesson will help you understand the correct classification and the reasoning behind rejecting a separate natural type.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four-fold division
The passage states that categorical propositions, by consideration of the subject, are restricted to four divisions: if the subject is particular it is personal; if universal it is further divided into quantified (universal or particular) and unquantified (indefinite). Confirm this by recalling the standard classification.
The four are: personal, universal, particular, indefinite.
2
Examine the challenge of natural propositions
Some logicians propose a fifth type: natural propositions, where the subject is the nature itself (e.g., 'al-ḥayawān jins' – Animal is a genus). The commentator argues these are not a separate type.
The example given is 'al-insān ḍāḥik' (Man is laughing), which is judged on the nature of man.
3
Understand the commentator's argument
The commentator says that in a natural proposition, the predicate (e.g., genus) is attributed to the nature of the subject (e.g., animal), but in reality this attribution holds because of a condition (e.g., universality). However, that condition does not need to be stated in the judgment itself. If we insisted on including such conditions, the number of proposition types would be unlimited.
Thus, natural propositions are actually universal propositions considered under a special aspect.
4
Apply the rule to Islamic science examples
Take a universal proposition like 'Kullu muskirin ḥarām' (Every intoxicant is forbidden). This is clearly universal. Now consider the phrase 'al-insān ḍāḥik' (Man is laughing). This is often mistaken for a natural proposition, but it is actually a universal proposition because the predicate 'laughing' is attributed to the nature of man, even though the reason (being a wonderer) is not stated.
Similarly, 'al-ḥayawān jins' is universal, not natural.
5
Confirm the verdict
The passage concludes: 'So the truth is that the proposition is confined to the four divisions.' This resolves the controversy and affirms the standard classification.
The commentator also notes that the division given in the commentary (the one just explained) is superior to that in the original text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضابطة تقسيم القضية باعتبار الموضوع] فقد بان أن الحملية باعتبار الموضوع منحصرة في أربعة أقسام، ولك أن تقول في التقسيم : G موضوع الحمليّة إما جزئي أو كلّيّ : فإن كان جزئياً فهي شخصية. والحق أن تلك القضايا أيضاً طبيعية، لأن المحكوم عليه بالجنسية هو طبيعة الحيوان ،وحدها، وكيف لا والمحكوم عليه هاهنا مايفهم من لفظ «الحيوان» وهو الطبيعة وحدها، وإن كان ثبوت الجنسية لها في نفس الأمر باعتبار كليتها، كما أن المحكوم عليه بالضحك في قولنا : الإنسان ضاحك هو طبيعة الإنسان، وإن كان ثبوت الضحك لها في نفس الأمر باعتبار كونها متعجبة؛ فإن القيد المعتبر في ثبوت المحكوم به للمحكوم عليه في نفس الأمر لا يجب أن يلاحظ في الحكم ثبوته له، وإن لوحظ لم تنحصر القضية في خمسة ولا في سنّة، لأن القيود المعتبرة حينئذ غير محصورة في عدد؛ فالحق انحصار القضية في الأقسام الأربعة، والتقسيم المذكور في الشرح أحسن تماهو في المتن (شريف)
Rule for the division of the proposition by subject] It has become clear that the categorical [proposition] by consideration of the subject is restricted to four divisions. And you may say in the division: G The subject of the categorical is either particular or universal: if it is particular, then it is personal. But the truth is that those propositions [i.e., natural ones] are also [really] universal, because the thing judged with genus-hood is the nature of animal alone; and how could it not be, for the thing judged here is what is understood from the word 'animal', which is the nature alone, even though the attribution of genus-hood to it in reality is by virtue of its universality. Similarly, the thing judged with laughter in our saying 'Man is laughing' is the nature of man, even though the attribution of laughter to it in reality is by virtue of his being a wonderer. For the condition considered in the attribution of the predicate to the subject in reality need not be observed in the judgment of its attribution to it. And if it were observed, the proposition would not be confined to five nor to six, because the conditions considered then would be unlimited in number. So the truth is that the proposition is confined to the four divisions, and the division mentioned in the commentary is better than what is in the text. (Sharif)
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents the rule for dividing categorical propositions based on the subject. It asserts that categorical propositions are restricted to four types: personal (shakhṣiyya) when the subject is a particular; and when the subject is universal, it is further divided into quantified (universal or particular) and indefinite (muhmala). However, a controversy arises: some consider natural propositions (e.g., 'al-ḥayawān jins') as a fifth type. The commentator (Sharif) argues that natural propositions are actually universal propositions, because the judgment (e.g., genus-hood) attaches to the nature of the subject in reality by virtue of its universality, even though the judgment itself is made on the nature without explicit quantification. He illustrates with the example 'al-insān ḍāḥik' (Man is laughing): the subject is the nature of man, but laughter is attributed to man in reality because of a specific cause (being a wonderer). The key point is that the condition for the predicate's inherence in reality need not be reflected in the logical form of the proposition. If one insisted on incorporating such conditions, the number of proposition types would multiply indefinitely. Therefore, the correct view is that there are only four types, and the division given in the commentary (i.e., the one explained here) is superior to that in the original text (matn).

Key Takeaway

10 min
The Four Divisions of Categorical Propositions Based on Subject
Author classifies categorical propositions into four types and defends against a critique from Ibn Sina's classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any categorical proposition into one of four types (personal, natural, quantified, indefinite) and explain why Ibn Sina's three-part division suffices for scientific propositions.

Core Idea
Categorical propositions divide into four types by subject, but only three matter in science.

In this lesson, we delve into the classification of categorical propositions (al-hamliyya) according to their subject. The author presents a fourfold division: personal, natural, quantified, and indefinite. However, he defends Ibn Sina's three-part division (omitting natural) on the grounds that natural propositions are not used in the sciences. Understanding this classification helps you identify the logical structure of statements in Islamic legal and theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject type
Determine whether the subject of the proposition is a particular individual (juz'i) or a universal (kulli). For example, 'Zayd is standing' has a particular subject; 'Every man is mortal' has a universal subject.
2
Apply the division rule
If the subject is particular, the proposition is personal (shakhsiyya). If universal, check if the judgment is on the nature itself or on its individuals. If on the nature, it's natural (tabi'iyya); if on individuals, proceed to step 3.
3
Distinguish quantified from indefinite
If the judgment is on individuals and the quantity of individuals is specified (e.g., 'all' or 'some'), it is quantified (mahsoura). If unspecified, it is indefinite (muhmala). Example: 'All humans are mortal' (quantified) vs. 'Humans are mortal' (indefinite, but interpreted as about individuals).
4
Evaluate the Ibn Sina controversy
Ibn Sina divided only into personal, quantified, and indefinite, omitting natural. Later scholars criticized this. The author defends Ibn Sina by arguing that natural propositions are not used in sciences because sciences study individuals, not natures. Personal propositions are still useful within quantified ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضابطة تقسيم القضية باعتبار الموضوع
فقد بان أن الحملية باعتبار الموضوع منحصرة في أربعة أقسام، ولك أن تقول في التقسيم:
موضوع الحمليّة إما جزئي أو كلّيّ:
فإن كان جزئياً فهي شخصية.
والحق أن تلك القضايا أيضاً طبيعية، لأن المحكوم عليه بالجنسية هو طبيعة الحيوان، وحدها، وكيف لا والمحكوم عليه هاهنا ما يفهم من لفظ "الحيوان" وهو الطبيعة وحدها، وإن كان ثبوت الجنسية لها في نفس الأمر باعتبار كليتها، كما أن المحكوم عليه بالضحك في قولنا: الإنسان ضاحك هو طبيعة الإنسان، وإن كان ثبوت الضحك لها في نفس الأمر باعتبار كونها متعجبة؛ فإن القيد المعتبر في ثبوت المحكوم به للمحكوم عليه في نفس الأمر لا يجب أن يلاحظ في الحكم ثبوته له، وإن لوحظ لم تنحصر القضية في خمسة ولا في ستة، لأن القيود المعتبرة حينئذ غير محصورة في عدد؛ فالحق انحصار القضية في الأقسام الأربعة، والتقسيم المذكور في الشرح أحسن مما هو في المتن.
وإن كان كلّيّاً: فإما أن يكون الحكم فيها على نفس طبيعة الكلّي أو على ما صدق عليه من الأفراد:
فإن كان الحكم على نفس الطبيعة فهي طبيعية.
وإن كان على ما صدق عليه من الأفراد:
فإما أن يبين فيها كمية الأفراد وهي المحصورة.
أو لا، وهي المهملة.
والشيخ في الشفاء ثلث القسمة فقال: "الموضوع إن كان جزئياً الشخصية، وإن كان كلّيّاً فإن بين فيها كمية الأفراد فهي المحصورة، وإلا فهي المهملة".
وشنع عليه المتأخرون بعدم الانحصار فيها، لخروج الطبيعية.
والجواب أن الكلام في القضية المعتبرة في العلوم، والطبيعيات لا اعتبار لها في العلوم، لأن الحكم في القضايا على ما صدق عليه.
وذلك لأن الموجودات المتأصلة هي الأفراد، والطبيعة إنما توجد في ضمنها، والمقصود من العلوم معرفة أحوال الموجودات المتأصلة. فإن قلت: الشخصية أيضاً ليست معتبرة في العلوم، إذ لا يبحث فيها عن الأشخاص. قلت: هي معتبرة في ضمن المحصورات، بخلاف الطبيعية، فإنها ليست بمعتبرة - لا في ذاتها ولا في ضمن المحصورات - لأن الحكم فيها على الأفراد – لا على الطبائع.
Rule for the division of the proposition according to the subject: It has become clear that the categorical proposition, according to the subject, is confined to four divisions. You may say in the division: The subject of the categorical is either particular or universal. If it is particular, then it is personal. And the truth is that those propositions are also natural, because the thing judged as genus is the nature of animal alone, and how not? Since that which is judged here is what is understood from the word 'animal', which is the nature alone, even though the attribution of genus to it in reality is by virtue of its universality, just as the thing judged as laughing in our statement 'Man is laughing' is the nature of man, even though the attribution of laughing to it in reality is by virtue of its being a wonder; for the condition considered in the attribution of the predicate to the subject in reality need not be observed in the judgment of its attribution to it, and if it were observed, the proposition would not be confined to five nor to six, because the conditions considered would then be uncountable in number. So the truth is the confinement of the proposition to four divisions, and the division mentioned in the commentary is better than what is in the text. And if it is universal: then either the judgment is upon the nature of the universal itself, or upon its individuals: if the judgment is upon the nature itself, then it is natural. And if it is upon its individuals: then either the quantity of the individuals is specified in it, and it is the quantified; or not, and it is the indefinite. And the Master (Ibn Sina) in al-Shifa divided it into three, saying: 'If the subject is particular, it is personal; if it is universal, then if the quantity of individuals is specified in it, it is quantified; otherwise it is indefinite.' And the later scholars criticized him for not restricting it therein, because of the exclusion of the natural. And the answer is that the discussion is about the proposition considered in the sciences, and the natural propositions have no consideration in the sciences, because the judgment in propositions is upon what it applies to. And that is because the fundamental existents are the individuals, and nature only exists within them, and the purpose of the sciences is to know the states of the fundamental existents. If you say: The personal also is not considered in the sciences, since one does not investigate individuals in them. I say: It is considered within the quantified, unlike the natural, for it is not considered—neither in itself nor within the quantified—because the judgment in it is upon the individuals, not upon the natures.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion of the classification of categorical propositions (al-hamliyya) according to their subject (al-mawdu'). The author begins by stating the rule that such propositions are confined to four divisions. The division proceeds: the subject is either particular (juz'i) or universal (kulli). If particular, the proposition is personal (shakhsiyya). However, the author immediately qualifies that these personal propositions are also natural (tabi'iyya) in a sense, because the judgment concerning genus is actually on the nature itself. He illustrates with the example of 'Animal is a genus'—the judged entity is the nature of animal alone, even though the attribution of genus to it is due to its universality. Similarly, in 'Man is laughing', the subject is the nature of man, though the laughter is attributed because of wonder. The author argues that the conditions of attribution do not need to be explicitly noted in the judgment; otherwise, the number of divisions would be unlimited. So the true division remains four. Then he continues the classification: if the subject is universal, either the judgment is on the nature itself (natural proposition) or on its individuals. If on individuals, either the quantity is specified (quantified, mahsoura) or not (indefinite, muhmala). The author then reports a controversy: Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh) in al-Shifa divided only into three (personal, quantified, indefinite), omitting the natural. The later scholars (al-muta'akhkhirun) criticized him for this omission. The response is that the focus is on propositions used in the sciences, and natural propositions are not considered in sciences because their judgment is on the nature, not on individuals. Sciences deal with fundamental existents which are individuals; nature exists only within individuals. Personal propositions, though not directly about individuals, are considered within quantified propositions, whereas natural propositions are not considered at all, either alone or within quantified ones. Thus, Ibn Sina's three-part division suffices for scientific purposes.

شخصية
طبيعية

Key Takeaway
Categorical propositions divide into four types by subject, but for scientific discourse, natural propositions are set aside because sciences investigate individuals, not natures.

The Two Interpretations of 'Every J is B': Haqiqa and Kharij
Author explains two interpretations of 'كل ج ب' and reasons for using variables.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the haqiqa and kharij interpretations of the universal affirmative proposition and apply each to an Islamic science example.

Core Idea
The universal proposition can range over possible existents (haqiqa) or only actual existents (kharij).

In Islamic logic, the statement 'Every J is B' is not univocal. The author explains two foundational interpretations: one based on reality (haqiqa) and one based on external existence (kharij). Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating propositions about non-existent entities, such as in theological arguments about the possible world.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations from the Arabic
Locate in the Arabic where the author distinguishes between 'بحسب الحقيقة' and 'بحسب الخارج'. Read the definitions carefully.
The Arabic uses 'تارة بحسب الحقيقة... وتارة بحسب الخارج'.
2
Explain the haqiqa interpretation in your own words
The haqiqa interpretation means that the proposition covers all possible individuals that would be J if they existed. It is a conditional claim about the implication between being J and being B.
Think of it as: 'If anything were a J, it would be a B'.
3
Explain the kharij interpretation
The kharij interpretation only covers Js that actually exist in the external world, regardless of time. It is an existential claim about actual individuals.
It is the everyday reading: 'All actual Js are B'.
4
Compare truth conditions
Consider a proposition like 'Every non-existent thing is impossible'. Under haqiqa, it is true if any possible non-existent would be impossible. Under kharij, since there are no non-existent things, the proposition might be vacuously true or false depending on convention.
This distinction is crucial when discussing possible worlds in Kalam.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Take the proposition 'Every prophet is infallible'. Under kharij, it applies only to actual prophets. Under haqiqa, it applies even to hypothetical prophets if they were to exist. Which interpretation do theologians use?
Most theological arguments rely on the haqiqa interpretation for universal claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثاني في تحقيق المحصورات الأربع قولنا: كلّ ج ب » يستعمل تارة بحسب الحقيقة، ومعناه أن كل ما لووجد كان (ج) من الأفراد الممكنة فهو بحيث لو وجد كان (ب)؛ أي «كل ما هو ملزوم (ج) هو ملزوم (ب)». وتارة بحسب الخارج، ومعناه: كلّ (ج) في الخارج - سواء كان حال الحكم أو قبله أو بعده - فهو (ب) في الخارج. القول : قد عرفت أن للحملية طرفين : أحدهما - وهو المحكوم عليه - يسمّى موضوعاً، وثانيهما - وهو المحكوم به - يسمّى محمولا. فاعلم أنّ عادة القوم في تحقيق المحصورات قد جرت بأنهم يعبرون عن الموضوع بـ «ج» وعن المحمول بـ«ب» حتى أنهم إذا قالوا : «كلّ ج ب» فكأنهم قالوا : «كل موضوع محمول». وإنما فعلوا ذلك لفائدتين : إحداهما الاختصار، فإن قولنا : «كلّ ج ب أخصر من قولنا : كلّ إنسان حيوان» مثلاً - وهو ظاهر. (1 وثانيهما ).
The second investigation: on the realization of the four quantified propositions. Our statement 'Every J is B' is used sometimes according to reality (haqiqa), and its meaning is: everything which, if it existed, would be (J) among possible individuals, is such that if it existed it would be (B); i.e., 'everything that is implied by (J) is implied by (B)'. And sometimes according to external existence (kharij), and its meaning is: every (J) in external existence—whether at the time of judgment, before it, or after it—is (B) in external existence. The statement: You have known that the categorical proposition has two sides: one of them—that which is judged upon—is called the subject, and the second—that which is judged with—is called the predicate. Know then that the customary practice of the scholars in the investigation of the quantified propositions is that they express the subject by (J) and the predicate by (B), such that when they say 'Every J is B', it is as if they said 'Every subject is a predicate'. They did this for two benefits: one of them is brevity, for our saying 'Every J is B' is more concise than our saying 'Every human is an animal', for example—and that is obvious. And the second benefit is [incomplete].
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya begins a new section on the four quantified propositions (المحصورات الأربع). The author first explains the two interpretations of the universal affirmative 'Every J is B'. According to the interpretation 'according to reality' (بحسب الحقيقة), it means that everything that would be a J if it existed (i.e., possible individuals) is such that it would be B. In other words, the implication: anything that entails J-ness also entails B-ness. According to the interpretation 'according to external existence' (بحسب الخارج), it means that every J that actually exists (at any time) is B. This distinction is important for handling propositions about non-existent subjects. Next, the author explains the convention of using the letters ج (J) for the subject and ب (B) for the predicate. This is done for two benefits: first, brevity—'Every J is B' is shorter than 'Every human is an animal'. The second benefit is cut off in the text, but from the context it likely concerns avoiding the misconception that the proposition is about specific terms, thus allowing general logical analysis.

بحسب الخارج

The universal affirmative proposition has two distinct interpretations: one covering possible beings (haqiqa) and one covering actual existents (kharij).

Why Logicians Use Variables: Brevity and Generality
Author explains why logicians use variable notation for subject and predicate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why logicians use variables like J and B instead of specific terms, and identify the benefits of this convention.

Core Idea
Variables prevent the confusion of logic with specific subject matters and ensure brevity.

You have learned that categorical propositions consist of subject and predicate. But why do logic texts use letters like ج and ب? The author explains that this convention serves two purposes: brevity and generality. This lesson unpacks these benefits and shows how variable notation is a powerful tool for logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Recall the structure of categorical propositions
A categorical proposition has a subject and a predicate. The subject is what is being judged upon, the predicate is what is being judged.
This is established earlier in the text.
2
Find where the author discusses the variable convention
In the Arabic, after explaining the subject and predicate, the author says: 'فاعلم أنّ عادة القوم... يعبرون عن الموضوع بـ ج وعن المحمول بـ ب'. Identify this section.
The text says they use ج for subject and ب for predicate.
3
Identify the first benefit: brevity
The author states that 'Every J is B' is shorter than 'Every human is animal'. This saves time and ink.
Brevity is especially useful in long logical derivations.
4
Infer the second benefit from context
Although the text cuts off, the second benefit is likely that using variables prevents the reader from thinking the logical form is tied to specific subject matters. It emphasizes the form over content.
This is a common pedagogical reason for formal notation.
5
Reflect on the application in Islamic legal theory
In usul al-fiqh, when analyzing the form of arguments, using variables helps to isolate the logical structure, as in the maxim 'Every ruling based on 'illa is general'.
Variables allow the mujtahid to focus on the logical connection without distraction.

Why Logicians Use Variables in Quantified Propositions
Author explains why logicians use variables in quantified propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two benefits (brevity and avoidance of confinement) of using variables ج and ب in quantified propositions and distinguish the two interpretations (by reality and by existence).

Core Idea
Variables make logical rules universal, not tied to a single example.

This lesson explores why logicians prefer using abstract symbols like ج and ب instead of concrete terms like 'human' and 'animal'. Understanding this practice is key to grasping how Islamic logic achieves generality and avoids the trap of thinking that logical rules apply only to specific cases.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two interpretations
The author explains that 'every ج is ب' can mean either (1) all possible individuals that would be ج are ب (by reality), or (2) all actual external individuals that are ج are ب (by existence). Note these two senses and how they differ.
This dual meaning is important when applying logic to real vs. hypothetical cases.
2
Recall the parts of a categorical proposition
Every categorical proposition has a subject (محمول عليه) and a predicate (محمول به). In variable notation, ج represents the subject and ب the predicate.
3
Identify the first benefit: brevity
Saying 'every ج is ب' is shorter than 'every human is animal'. The author notes this is obvious. Variables make logical statements concise.
Brevity allows focus on logical form rather than content.
4
Identify the second benefit: avoiding confinement
If logicians always used a concrete example like 'every human is animal', one might think the logical rules apply only to that example. Variables show that the rules are universal and apply to all subject matters.
This is similar to how the five universals are discussed without reference to specific materials.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The second investigation: on the verification of the four quantified propositions. Our statement 'every ج is ب' is used sometimes according to reality, meaning that every possible individual that would be ج is such that it would be ب; i.e., 'everything that entails ج entails ب'. And sometimes according to existence, meaning: every ج in existence - whether at the time of judgment, before it, or after it - is ب in existence. The statement: You have known that the categorical proposition has two sides: one - that which is judged upon - is called the subject, and the second - that which is judged by - is called the predicate. Know that the practice of the [logician] people in verifying the quantified propositions has been that they express the subject by 'ج' and the predicate by 'ب', so that when they say 'every ج is ب', it is as if they said 'every subject is predicate'. They did this for two benefits: one is brevity, for our statement 'every ج is ب' is more concise than our statement 'every human is an animal', for example - and that is obvious. The second is to repel the illusion of confinement, for if they had posited for the universal, for example, our statement 'every human is an animal' and applied the rulings to it, the mind might go to the idea that those rulings are only in this matter and not in other universal affirmative [propositions]. So they conceived the concept of the proposition and stripped it of matters, and expressed its two sides by 'ج' and 'ب', as a notification that the rulings applied to it encompass all its particulars, not confined to some without others; just as in the section on concepts they took the concepts of the five universals without reference to any matter, and investigated their states with an investigation covering all natures of things, and for this reason the investigations of this art became universal laws applicable to all particulars. So when we say 'every ج is ب', there are two things: one is the concept of ج and its reality, and the other is what is true of ج among individuals; so its meaning is not that the concept of ج is
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage explains the rationale behind the logicians' use of variables 'ج' and 'ب' in quantified propositions. The author first distinguishes two interpretations of 'every ج is ب': one based on reality (considering possible individuals) and one based on existence (actual external individuals). He then reminds us that a categorical proposition has a subject and a predicate. The logicians adopted the convention of using 'ج' for the subject and 'ب' for the predicate to achieve two purposes: brevity and avoiding the illusion that the ruling is confined to a specific subject matter. For example, if they always used 'every human is an animal' as the example, one might think the logical rules apply only to that specific case. By using variables, they emphasize that the rules are universal and apply to all subject matters. The author compares this to the approach in the section on concepts, where the five universals are discussed without reference to specific natures. Finally, he clarifies that when we say 'every ج is ب', we are referring to the individuals that fall under the concept of ج, not the concept itself.

The True Meaning of 'Every J is B'
Author clarifies the extension-based meaning of universal affirmative propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct interpretation of a universal affirmative proposition by distinguishing between intension and extension.

Core Idea
A universal proposition does not equate concepts but predicates a concept of individuals.

When a logician says 'Every J is B', what exactly is being asserted? Is it about the meaning of J and B, or about the things that J and B refer to? This passage from al-Qutbi clarifies the vital distinction between intension (mafhum) and extension (ma sadaqa 'alayhi). Understanding this is essential before you can evaluate any syllogism or definition in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two aspects of a term
Each universal term (like 'human') has two aspects: its concept (mafhum) and the individuals it applies to (ma sadaqa 'alayhi). The passage lists these for both J and B. Recognize this duality.
This mirrors the discussion of the five universals in tasawwurat.
2
Eliminate the false interpretations
The author refutes two wrong readings: (1) that the concept of J equals the concept of B (synonymy, no real predication), and (2) that the extension of J equals the extension of B (makes proposition tautological). Consider why each fails.
3
Extract the correct interpretation
The correct meaning is: every individual that falls under J has the concept B predicated of it. In other words, the extension of J is a subset of the extension of B. This is what the universal affirmative proposition asserts.
This avoids both conceptual identity and extensional identity.
4
Apply the quantifier 'كل'
The word 'كل' (every) reinforces that the predication covers all individuals of J. The full meaning: 'Every individual that is true of J is true of B.'
This standard interpretation is used throughout Islamic logic.
5
Test your understanding
Now consider a real Islamic example: 'Every believer is righteous.' Which interpretation is correct? It does not mean the concept of believer is the concept of righteous, nor that the set of believers equals the set of righteous. It means every individual who is a believer has the attribute of righteousness (in the sense of iman).
This semantic precision is crucial in usul al-fiqh when analyzing Qur'anic verses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
«كل إنسان حيوان وأجروا عليه الأحكام، أمكن أن يذهب الوهم إلى أنّ تلك الأحكام إنما هي في هذه المادة دون الموجبات الكليات الأخر، فتصوروا مفهوم القضية وجردوها عن المواد ، وعبروا عن طرفيها بـ«ج» و «ب» تنبيهاً على أن الأحكام الجارية عليها شاملة لجميع جزئياتها، غير مقصورة على البعض دون البعض ؛ كما أنهم في قسم التصورات أخذوا مفهومات الكليات الخمس من غير إشارة إلى مادة من المواد، وبحثوا عن أحوالها بحثاً متناولاً الجميع طبائع الأشياء، ولهذا صارت مباحث هذا الفن قوانين كلية منطبقة على جميع الجزئيات.
فإذا قلنا : «كلّ ج ب » فهناك أمران : أحدهما مفهوم (ج) وحقيقته، والآخر ما صدق عليه (ج) من الأفراد، فليس معناه أن مفهوم (ج) هو مفهوم (ب) وإلا لكان (ج) و (ب) لفظين مترادفين، فلايكون حمل في المعنى بل في اللفظ - بل معناه أن كل ما صدق عليه (ج) من الأفراد فهو (ب).
فإن قلت : كما أن لـ«ج» اعتبارين كذلك لـ«ب» اعتباران : مفهوم وأفراد، فالأولى أن يقال : إذا قلنا : «ج ب» فلا نعني به أن مفهوم (ج) مفهوم (ب) وإلا لم يكن هناك حمل بحسب المعنى - بل بحسب اللفظ – ولا نعني به أيضاً أن مفهوم (ج) ما يصدق عليه مفهوم (ب) - وإلا لكانت قضية طبيعية غير معتبرة في العلوم - بل نعني به أن ما صدق عليه (ج) من الأفراد يصدق عليه (ب)؛ وإذا قرن (ج) بلفظ «كل» كان المعنى «كل ما يصدق عليه (ج) من الأفراد يصدق عليه (ب)».
(۱) قد عرفت أنّ كلّ كلّي له مفهوم وما صدق عليه من الأفراد، فلكل واحد من (ج) و (ب) مفهوم وما صدق عليه من الأفراد؛ فيتصور هناك معان أربعة : الأول أن مفهوم (ج) مفهوم (ب) وقد عرفت بطلاته. والثاني أن ما صدق عليه (ج) من الأفراد ثبت له مفهوم (ب) وهو المراد. والثالث أن ما صدق عليه (ج) من الأفراد هو ماصدق عليه (ب)؛ وهو أيضاً باطل، لأن ما صدق عليه الموضوع هو بعينه ما صدق عليه المحمول سواء انحصر ما صدق عليه المحمول فيما صدق عليه الموضوع أولم ينحصر، وإذا اتحد ما صدقا عليه كان مفهوم القضية ثبوت الشيء لنفسه، فيكون صدقاً ضرورياً، فتنحصر القضايا في الضرورية.
فإن قلت : على تقدير إرادة الأفراد منهما معاً ينبغي أن لايكون في القضية حمل بحسب المعنى، لاتحاد الموضوع والمحمول حينئذ في الحقيقة، ولذلك قال : «ضرورة ثبوت الشيء لنفسه». قلت: هما وإن اتحدا حقيقة، لكنهما اختلفا من جهة أن الأفراد اعتبرت
If we say: 'Every human is an animal' and apply rulings to it, the imagination could go to that those rulings are only in this particular matter without the other universal implications. So they conceived the concept of the proposition and stripped it of matters, and expressed its two extremes by 'J' and 'B' as a reminder that the rulings applied to them encompass all their particulars, not confined to some without others. Just as in the section on concepts they took the concepts of the five universals without reference to any matter, and investigated their conditions in an investigation encompassing all natures of things. For this reason, the topics of this art became universal laws applicable to all particulars. So if we say: 'Every J is B', then there are two matters: one is the concept of J and its reality, and the other is what the individuals are true of J. So its meaning is not that the concept of J is the concept of B, otherwise J and B would be synonymous words, and there would be no predication in meaning but rather in wording. Rather, its meaning is that every individual that is true of J is B. If you say: Just as J has two considerations, so too B has two considerations: concept and individuals. So it is more appropriate to say: When we say 'J is B', we do not mean that the concept of J is the concept of B, otherwise there would be no predication in meaning — but rather in wording — and we also do not mean that the concept of J is what is true of the concept of B — otherwise it would be a natural proposition not considered in the sciences — but we mean that what is true of J of individuals is true of B; and if J is accompanied by the word 'every', the meaning is 'Every individual that is true of J is true of B'. (1) You have known that every universal has a concept and what is true of it of individuals. So for each of J and B there is a concept and what is true of it of individuals. So four meanings are conceived: First: the concept of J is the concept of B, and you have known its falsity. Second: that what is true of J of individuals has the concept B proven for it, and that is the intended meaning. Third: that what is true of J of individuals is what is true of B; that is also false, because what is true of the subject is exactly what is true of the predicate, whether what is true of the predicate is confined to what is true of the subject or not. And if what they are true of are identical, then the meaning of the proposition becomes the affirmation of a thing for itself, so it would be necessarily true, and propositions would be confined to necessary ones. If you say: On the assumption that individuals are intended from both, there should be no predication in meaning due to the identity of subject and predicate in reality, and therefore he said: 'Necessarily affirmation of the thing for itself'. I say: Although they are identical in reality, they differ in that the individuals were considered...
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining why logicians abstract propositions from particular matters and use variables like J and B. It begins by noting that if we say 'Every human is an animal' and apply rulings, one might mistakenly think those rulings are limited to that specific matter. To avoid this, logicians conceive the concept of the proposition stripped of specific matters and denote the two extremes by J and B, signaling that the rulings apply to all particulars. This is analogous to how in the theory of concepts (tasawwurat) they took the five universals abstractly. Consequently, the discussions of this art become universal laws applicable to all particulars. The passage then examines the meaning of 'كل ج ب' (Every J is B). It distinguishes two aspects: the concept (mafhum) of J and its reality, and what the individuals are true of (ma sadaqa 'alayhi) J. The author clarifies that 'كل ج ب' does not mean that the concept of J is the concept of B (which would make them synonymous and yield no real predication), but rather that every individual falling under J is B. An objection is raised: since both J and B have a concept and individuals, we should consider four possible interpretations. The author refutes the first (concept-concept identity) and the third (identity of extensions), and asserts that the correct meaning is the second: individuals of J have the concept B predicated of them. The commentary then elaborates on the four possible meanings, showing that the third would lead to all propositions being necessarily true, which is absurd. Finally, a further objection about the identity of subject and predicate when both are taken extensionally is answered by noting a difference in consideration. The passage is a careful semantic analysis of the universal affirmative proposition, a foundational topic in logic.

Key Takeaway
A universal affirmative proposition predicates the concept of the predicate of every individual belonging to the subject's extension, not of the subject's concept itself.

Interpreting 'Every J is B': Four Possibilities and the Correct One
Author analyzes four possible meanings of 'كل ج ب' and selects the correct one for scientific propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct interpretation of a universal affirmative proposition in scientific discourse and distinguish it from incorrect interpretations.

Core Idea
The meaning of 'Every J is B' is not about concepts or identical extensions, but about individuals of J having the concept of B.

In this passage, the author dissects the universal affirmative proposition 'كل ج ب', revealing four possible meanings. Most are flawed, leading to tautologies or necessary truths. The correct interpretation is crucial for meaningful predication in the sciences, such as in kalam arguments about miracles or in fiqh rulings about intoxicants.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two components of a universal
Every universal has a concept (مفهوم) and individuals that it applies to (ما صدق عليه). Understand this distinction for both subject and predicate.
2
List the four combinations
The author enumerates four combinations of concept and individuals for subject (ج) and predicate (ب). Write them down: 1) concept of J = concept of B; 2) individuals of J have concept of B; 3) individuals of J = individuals of B; 4) concept of J = what B applies to.
3
Evaluate the first and third interpretations
The first (concept = concept) is false because it reduces predication to synonymy. The third (individuals = individuals) makes the proposition necessarily true (affirmation of a thing of itself), restricting all scientific propositions to necessary truths.
The author argues this would be catastrophic for science, as only necessary truths would exist.
4
Analyze the second interpretation (the correct one)
The second interpretation means that the individuals that J applies to have the concept of B. This allows meaningful predication: you are attributing a property to individuals. The author defends this against an objection about identity.
Despite the individuals being identical in reality, the difference in relation (as subject vs predicate) suffices for valid predication.
5
Understand the rejection of the fourth interpretation
The fourth (concept of J is what B applies to) judges on the nature, not individuals. Scientific propositions aim to apply judgments to essences (individuals), so this interpretation is not considered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد عرفت أن كل كلّي له مفهوم وما صدق عليه من الأفراد، فلكل واحد من (ج) و (ب) مفهوم وما صدق عليه من الأفراد؛ فيتصور هناك معان أربعة : الأول أن مفهوم (ج) مفهوم (ب) وقد عرفت بطلاته. والثاني أن ما صدق عليه (ج) من الأفراد ثبت له مفهوم (ب) وهو المراد. والثالث أن ما صدق عليه (ج) من الأفراد هو ما صدق عليه (ب)؛ وهو أيضاً باطل، لأن ما صدق عليه الموضوع هو بعينه ما صدق عليه المحمول سواء انحصر ما صدق عليه المحمول فيما صدق عليه الموضوع أولم ينحصر، وإذا اتحد ما صدقا عليه كان مفهوم القضية ثبوت الشيء لنفسه، فيكون صدقاً ضرورياً، فتنحصر القضايا في الضرورية. وإن قلت: على تقدير إرادة الأفراد منهما معاً ينبغي أن لايكون في القضية حمل بحسب المعنى، لاتحاد الموضوع والمحمول حينئذ في الحقيقة، ولذلك قال: «ضرورة ثبوت الشيء لنفسه». قلت: هما وإن اتحدا حقيقة، لكنهما اختلفا من جهة أن الأفراد اعتبرت في جانب الموضوع من حيث أنها يصدق عليها (ج)، وفي المحمول من حيث أنها يصدق عليها (ب)؛ وهذا المقدار من الاختلاف والتغاير كاف في صحة الحمل بحسب المعنى. وأما اعتبار التغاير في مفهوم واحد باعتبار الدلالة عليه بلفظين فغير ملتفت إليه؛ فلذلك قال هناك بعدم الحمل - دون انحصار القضايا في الضرورية. الرابع أن مفهوم (ج) ما صدق عليه (ب) ، وهو أيضاً ليس من القضايا المعتبرة، لما عرفت من أن الحكم على الأفراد دون الطبيعة. والحاصل أن المعتبر في جانب الموضوع هو الأفراد، وفي جانب المحمول هو المفهوم. هذا في القضايا المعتبرة في العلوم، إذ المقصود منها كما عرفت إجراء الأحكام على الذوات المتأصلة في الوجود بأحوالها، والذوات المتأصلة الأفراد، والأحوال هي المفهومات (شريف).
You have known that every universal has a concept and individuals that it applies to. So for each of (J) and (B) there is a concept and individuals that it applies to. Four meanings are conceivable: First, the concept of (J) is the concept of (B) – and you have known its falsity. Second, that the individuals that (J) applies to have the concept of (B) – and that is intended. Third, that the individuals that (J) applies to are exactly the individuals that (B) applies to – and that is also false, because what the subject applies to is exactly what the predicate applies to, whether the application of the predicate is restricted to what the subject applies to or not; if they are identical in application, the meaning of the proposition becomes the affirmation of a thing of itself, so it would be necessarily true, and all propositions would be restricted to necessary ones. If you object: On the assumption that both refer to individuals, there should be no predication in meaning because subject and predicate are then identical in reality, and that is why he said 'necessary affirmation of a thing of itself'. I respond: Even though they are identical in reality, they differ in that the individuals are considered on the side of the subject insofar as (J) applies to them, and on the side of the predicate insofar as (B) applies to them; this degree of difference is sufficient for the validity of predication in meaning. As for considering a difference within one concept by denoting it with two words, that is not paid attention to; that is why he said there that there is no predication – without restricting propositions to necessary ones. Fourth, that the concept of (J) is what (B) applies to – and that is also not among the considered propositions, because you have known that judgment pertains to individuals, not the nature. The conclusion is that on the side of the subject, what is considered is the individuals, and on the side of the predicate, the concept. This applies to propositions considered in the sciences, since their purpose, as you have known, is to apply judgments to the essences that subsist in existence along with their states, and the subsisting essences are the individuals, and the states are the concepts.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is a definitional analysis of the meaning of a universal affirmative proposition, specifically the form 'كل ج ب' (Every J is B). The author, building on the fact that each universal has both a concept (مفهوم) and individuals that it applies to (ما صدق عليه من الأفراد), lays out four possible interpretations. He states the first interpretation (concept of J = concept of B) is false, presumably because it reduces predication to mere synonymy. The second interpretation (individuals of J have the concept of B) is identified as the intended meaning for scientific propositions. The third interpretation (individuals of J = individuals of B) is criticized because it would make the proposition necessarily true (affirmation of a thing of itself), thus restricting all propositions to necessary ones. He then addresses an objection: if both subject and predicate refer to individuals, predication seems impossible due to identity. His response is that while the individuals are identical in reality, they differ in the relations (J-ness vs. B-ness), and that suffices for meaningful predication. The fourth interpretation (concept of J is what B applies to) is rejected because judgment applies to individuals, not natures. The final conclusion is that in scientific propositions the subject term stands for individuals (الذوات المتأصلة) and the predicate term stands for a concept or state (حال). This ensures that predication conveys new information about the individual's attribute, e.g., 'Every wine is forbidden' means each individual wine has the attribute of being forbidden.

Key Terms
مفهوم
ما صدق عليه
قضية طبيعية
ضرورية

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct interpretation of 'كل خمر حرام' (Every wine is forbidden) in scientific discourse?
A) The concept of wine is the concept of forbidden.
B) The individuals that wine applies to have the concept of forbidden.
C) The individuals that wine applies to are exactly the individuals that forbidden applies to.
D) The concept of wine is what forbidden applies to.

Key Takeaway
In scientific propositions, 'كل ج ب' means that every individual that J applies to has the concept of B, not that the concepts are identical or that the extensions are the same.

Understanding Predication: Intension and Extension
Defining predication as concept applied to individuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between the subject as extension and the predicate as intension in categorical propositions, and explain why the predicate cannot be taken as extension.

Core Idea
Predication applies a concept to individuals, not individuals to individuals.

In this passage, the author clarifies the correct meaning of predication (al-haml) in universal affirmative propositions. He argues that if the predicate were also taken as the individuals to which it applies, all propositions would be necessary, which is absurd. Instead, the predicate must be a concept that describes the individuals falling under the subject.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate roles
Notice that in a proposition like 'Every J is B', the subject term 'J' refers to individuals (its extension), while the predicate term 'B' refers to a concept (its intension). The author asks why the predicate cannot also be taken as individuals.
This is the key asymmetry in predication.
2
Understand the consequence of treating predicate as extension
If 'B' in the predicate were also taken as individuals, then the subject and predicate would refer to the same set of individuals, making the proposition necessarily true by identity. This would render all propositions necessary and eliminate possible propositions, which is false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Extract the correct definition of predication
The author states that the meaning of 'Every J is B' is: every individual to which the concept of J applies is described by the concept of B. The subject side deals with individuals (extension), the predicate side with concepts (intension).
This definition preserves contingency and avoids the absurdity.
4
Apply the definition to an Islamic example
Consider the proposition 'Every human is a rational animal'. The subject 'human' refers to individual humans (Zayd, Amr, etc.), while the predicate 'rational animal' is a concept that describes those individuals. The proposition is true because each individual human is indeed a rational animal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلِمَ لا يجوز أن يكون المحمول ماصدق عليه (ب) من الأفراد لامفهومه ؟ كما أن الموضوع كذلك ؟ فنقول: ما صدق عليه الموضوع هو بعينه ما صدق عليه المحمول، فلوكان المحمول ما صدق عليه (ب)، لكان المحمول ضروري الثبوت للموضوع، ضرورة ثبوت الشيء لنفسه، فتنحصر القضايا في الضرورية، ولم تصدق ممكنة خاصة أصلاً. فقد ظهر أن معنى القضية «كلّ ماصدق عليه مفهوم ج من الأفراد فهو مفهوم ب لا «ماصدق عليه ب». لا يقال : إذا قلنا كلّ ج ب» فإما أن يكون مفهوم (ج) عين مفهوم (ب) أو غيره، فإن كان عينه : يلزم ما ذكرتم من أن الحمل لا يكون في جانب الموضوع من حيث أنها يصدق عليها (ج)، وفي المحمول من حيث أنها يصدق عليها (ب)؛ وهذا المقدار من الاختلاف والتغاير كاف في صحة الحمل بحسب المعنى. وأما اعتبار التغاير في مفهوم واحد باعتبار الدلالة عليه بلفظين فغير ملتفت إليه؛ فلذلك قال هناك بعدم الحمل - دون انحصار القضايا في الضرورية. الرابع أن مفهوم (ج) ماصدق عليه (ب)، وهو أيضاً ليس من القضايا المعتبرة، لما عرفت من أن الحكم على الأفراد دون الطبيعة. والحاصل أن المعتبر في جانب الموضوع هو الأفراد، وفي جانب المحمول هو المفهوم. هذا في القضايا المعتبرة في العلوم، إذ المقصود منها كما عرفت إجراء الأحكام على الذوات المتأصلة في الوجود بأحوالها، والذوات المتأصلة الأفراد، والأحوال هي المفهومات. مفيداً، وإن كان غيره : امتنع أن يقال : «أحدهما هو الآخر» لاستحالة أن يكون الشيء نفس ماليس هو هو. يجاب عنه بأن قولكم الحمل محال» يشتمل على الحمل، فيكون إبطالا للشيء بنفسه - وأنه محال. هذا الجواب معارضة لتلك الشبهة؛ تقريرها أن مدعاكم - وهو قولكم : «الحمل محال باطل، لأنه مشتمل على صحة الحمل، إذ قد حمل فيه المحال على الحمل، فيكون مدعاكم مبطلاً لنفسه، وماكان مبطلاً لنفسه كان باطلاً، إذ لو كان حقاً لكان حقاً وباطلاً معاً - وهو محال. ورد الشارح هذا الجواب بأنه إنما يصح إذا كان مدعى الخصم موجبة، وأما إذا كان مدعاه سالبة فلا يصح هذا الجواب قطعاً، بل يجب أن يقال : مفهوما (ج) و (ب) متغايران، ولا نعني بحمل (ب) على (ج) أن مفهوم (ج) هو عين مفهوم (ب) - فيلزم الحكم باتحاد المتغايرين - بل نعني كما تقدم أن «ماصدق عليه مفهوم (ج) من الأفراد يصدق عليه مفهوم (ب)» وصدق الأمور المتغايرة في المفهوم على ذات واحدة جائز، كصدق الإنسان والضاحك والماشي - و غير ذلك من المفهومات المتغايرة – على زيد. وللخصم أن يقول : قد حملت مفهوم (ب) بهو هو على ما صدق عليه (ج). فنقول : ما صدق عليه (ج) إما أن يكون عين مفهوم (ب)، فلاحمل بحسب المعنى أو غيره، فيلزم الحكم بأن أحد المتغايرين هو الآخر – وهو باطل – بل نقول صدق مفهوم (ج) - على ما فرضت صدقه عليه أيضاً - باطل، لأنهما إن اتحدا فلا صدق بحسب المعنى، وإن تغايرا لم يصح أن يقال أحدهما هو الآخر - لا تقييداً ولا إخباراً - فقد تضاعفت الشبهة بذلك الجواب الحق، ولا تنحسم مادتها إلا بتحقيق معنى الصدق والحمل.
Why is it not permissible that the predicate is what (B) applies to of individuals, not its concept? Just as the subject is likewise? Then we say: What the subject applies to is exactly what the predicate applies to. So if the predicate were what (B) applies to, then the predicate would be necessarily affirmed of the subject due to the necessity of a thing being itself, so all propositions would be necessary, and no particular possible proposition would be true at all. Thus it has become clear that the meaning of the proposition 'Every J is B' is 'Every individual to which the concept of J applies is the concept of B', not 'what (B) applies to'. It may not be said: When we say 'Every J is B', either the concept of J is the same as the concept of B or other. If it is the same, then what you mentioned follows: that the predication is not on the side of the subject in terms of its being described by J, and on the side of the predicate in terms of its being described by B; and this amount of difference and diversity is sufficient for the validity of predication in meaning. As for considering diversity in one concept in terms of indicating it with two words, that is not taken into account. Therefore it was said there that there is no predication – without limiting propositions to necessary ones. Fourth: the concept of J is what (B) applies to. This also is not among the considered propositions, for you know that the judgment is about individuals, not about the nature. The summary is that the considered in the subject side is the individuals, and in the predicate side is the concept. This is in the propositions considered in the sciences, since the purpose from them, as you know, is to apply judgments to the essences that are rooted in existence by their states, and the rooted essences are the individuals, and the states are the concepts. If it is useful, and if it is other, then it is impossible to say 'one of them is the other' because it is impossible for a thing to be what it is not. The response is that your statement 'predication is impossible' includes predication, and thus it is an invalidation of the thing by itself – and that is impossible. This response is a counterargument to that sophism; its formulation is that your claim – namely your statement 'predication is impossible' – is false because it includes the validity of predication, since it has predicated impossibility on predication, so your claim invalidates itself, and whatever invalidates itself is false, for if it were true it would be both true and false – which is impossible. The commentator refuted this response by saying that it is valid only if the opponent's claim is affirmative; but if his claim is negative, then this response is not valid at all. Rather, one must say: The concepts of J and B are diverse, and we do not mean by predicating B of J that the concept of J is the same as the concept of B – so that the judgment of unification of diverse things would follow – but we mean, as previously, that 'individuals to which the concept of J applies have the concept of B apply to them', and the truth of diverse concepts on a single essence is permissible, like the truth of human, laughing, walking – and other diverse concepts – on Zayd. And the opponent may say: You have predicated the concept of B as 'is' on what J applies to. So we say: What J applies to is either the same as the concept of B, then there is no predication in meaning, or other, then it necessitates the judgment that one of two diverse things is the other – which is false. Rather we say: The truth of the concept of J – on what you assumed is also true of it – is false, because if they are unified, there is no truth in meaning, and if they are diverse, it is not correct to say that one of them is the other – neither as a qualification nor as information. Thus the sophism is doubled by that true response, and its material is not resolved except by realizing the meaning of truth and predication.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is a commentary that first clarifies the correct meaning of predication (al-haml) in categorical propositions and then responds to a sophistical objection (shubha) that attempts to prove predication is impossible. The commentator (al-Sharif al-Jurjani) speaks on behalf of the author of the original text. The discussion begins with a question: why can't the predicate be taken as the individuals to which (B) applies, just as the subject is taken as the individuals to which (J) applies? The answer is that if predicate were also taken as individuals, then the predicate would be necessarily true of the subject due to identity, making all propositions necessary and eliminating possible propositions—an absurd result. Therefore, the correct interpretation of 'Every J is B' is that every individual falling under the concept of J is described by the concept of B, not that the individuals are B's. The text then anticipates an objection: when we say 'Every J is B', either the concept of J is the same as the concept of B or different. If identical, the earlier objection about necessity arises; or if they are different, the opponent claims it's impossible for one thing to be another. The answer is that the difference between the subject (as individuals) and the predicate (as concept) is enough for valid predication; conceptual diversity is sufficient. The text then mentions a 'fourth' point: that the concept of J is what B applies to, but this is not the intended meaning because judgments are about individuals, not natures. The overall conclusion is that in scientific propositions, the subject refers to individuals (the essences rooted in existence) and the predicate refers to concepts (states of those individuals). A second sophism follows: the opponent argues that predication is impossible because either the subject and predicate concepts are identical (so no real predication) or different (so you claim one thing is another, which is false). The response is that the opponent's own statement 'predication is impossible' itself involves a predication (impossibility on predication), thus it is self-refuting. However, the commentator (the Sharh) refutes this meta-response by noting it only works if the opponent's claim is affirmative; if it's negative (i.e., 'predication does not occur'), the self-refutation doesn't apply. The commentator then gives the correct resolution: we mean by predication that individuals under J are described by B, not that the concepts are one. Diverse concepts can apply to the same individual, e.g., 'human', 'laughing', 'walking' all apply to Zayd. The passage ends by noting that the sophism persists unless one fully understands the meaning of truth (sidq) and predication.

Key Terms
الحمل
ما صدق عليه
المفهوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would happen if the predicate were taken as the individuals to which it applies?
A) All propositions would be possible.
B) All propositions would be necessary.
C) All propositions would be impossible.
D) Propositions would remain as they are.

Key Takeaway
Predication requires the subject to supply individuals (extension) and the predicate to supply a concept (intension).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the proposition 'Every intoxicant is forbidden (haram)'. Identify what the subject refers to (individual intoxicants like wine, beer) and what the predicate is (the concept of forbiddenness). Explain why the predicate cannot be taken as the individuals to which 'forbidden' applies.
?
Construct a similar reductio: Would the proposition 'Zayd is a man' become necessarily true if 'man' were taken as the individuals that 'man' applies to? Why not?

Refuting the Sophism of the Impossibility of Predication
Refuting the sophism that predication is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the sophism claiming predication is impossible, evaluate the self-refutation response, and articulate the correct resolution based on subject-predicate asymmetry.

Core Idea
The sophism is self-refuting only if the opponent's claim is affirmative; the true resolution is the asymmetry of extension and intension.

The passage now addresses a sophistical objection that seeks to prove predication is impossible: either the subject and predicate concepts are identical (so no real predication) or different (so you assert one thing is another, which is false). The author and commentator analyze the strengths and limitations of various responses, culminating in the correct understanding.

Guided Path
1
State the sophism
The opponent argues: In 'J is B', either concept J equals concept B, making the predication trivial (no real predication), or they are different, in which case claiming they are the same would be false. Thus predication is impossible.
This is a false dichotomy: it ignores the subject-predicate asymmetry.
2
Analyze the first response: self-refutation
One might respond that the opponent's own statement 'predication is impossible' itself involves a predication (impossibility is predicated on predication), so it is self-defeating. This response works only if the opponent's claim is affirmative (e.g., 'predication is false'), not if it is negative (e.g., 'predication does not occur').
The commentator al-Sharif al-Jurjani points out this limitation.
3
Understand the correct resolution
The correct answer is that the subject and predicate are not on the same level: the subject provides individuals, the predicate provides a concept. The same individual can have multiple diverse concepts true of it (e.g., Zayd is human, laughing, walking). Thus predication is the application of a concept to individuals, not the identity of concepts.
This dissolves the sophism because conceptual diversity does not prevent predication.
4
Test the resolution on an Islamic example
Consider 'The prayer is obligatory'. The subject 'prayer' refers to the individual acts of prayer (extension), the predicate 'obligatory' is a concept that describes each such act. The concepts 'prayer' and 'obligatory' are diverse, yet they apply to the same individuals, making the proposition true.

Source Text

فلِمَ لا يجوز أن يكون المحمول ماصدق عليه (ب) من الأفراد لامفهومه ؟ كما أن الموضوع كذلك ؟ فنقول: ما صدق عليه الموضوع هو بعينه ما صدق عليه المحمول، فلوكان المحمول ما صدق عليه (ب)، لكان المحمول ضروري الثبوت للموضوع، ضرورة ثبوت الشيء لنفسه، فتنحصر القضايا في الضرورية، ولم تصدق ممكنة خاصة أصلاً. فقد ظهر أن معنى القضية «كلّ ماصدق عليه مفهوم ج من الأفراد فهو مفهوم ب لا «ماصدق عليه ب». لا يقال : إذا قلنا كلّ ج ب» فإما أن يكون مفهوم (ج) عين مفهوم (ب) أو غيره، فإن كان عينه : يلزم ما ذكرتم من أن الحمل لا يكون في جانب الموضوع من حيث أنها يصدق عليها (ج)، وفي المحمول من حيث أنها يصدق عليها (ب)؛ وهذا المقدار من الاختلاف والتغاير كاف في صحة الحمل بحسب المعنى. وأما اعتبار التغاير في مفهوم واحد باعتبار الدلالة عليه بلفظين فغير ملتفت إليه؛ فلذلك قال هناك بعدم الحمل - دون انحصار القضايا في الضرورية. الرابع أن مفهوم (ج) ماصدق عليه (ب)، وهو أيضاً ليس من القضايا المعتبرة، لما عرفت من أن الحكم على الأفراد دون الطبيعة. والحاصل أن المعتبر في جانب الموضوع هو الأفراد، وفي جانب المحمول هو المفهوم. هذا في القضايا المعتبرة في العلوم، إذ المقصود منها كما عرفت إجراء الأحكام على الذوات المتأصلة في الوجود بأحوالها، والذوات المتأصلة الأفراد، والأحوال هي المفهومات. مفيداً، وإن كان غيره : امتنع أن يقال : «أحدهما هو الآخر» لاستحالة أن يكون الشيء نفس ماليس هو هو. يجاب عنه بأن قولكم الحمل محال» يشتمل على الحمل، فيكون إبطالا للشيء بنفسه - وأنه محال. هذا الجواب معارضة لتلك الشبهة؛ تقريرها أن مدعاكم - وهو قولكم : «الحمل محال باطل، لأنه مشتمل على صحة الحمل، إذ قد حمل فيه المحال على الحمل، فيكون مدعاكم مبطلاً لنفسه، وماكان مبطلاً لنفسه كان باطلاً، إذ لو كان حقاً لكان حقاً وباطلاً معاً - وهو محال. ورد الشارح هذا الجواب بأنه إنما يصح إذا كان مدعى الخصم موجبة، وأما إذا كان مدعاه سالبة فلا يصح هذا الجواب قطعاً، بل يجب أن يقال : مفهوما (ج) و (ب) متغايران، ولا نعني بحمل (ب) على (ج) أن مفهوم (ج) هو عين مفهوم (ب) - فيلزم الحكم باتحاد المتغايرين - بل نعني كما تقدم أن «ماصدق عليه مفهوم (ج) من الأفراد يصدق عليه مفهوم (ب)» وصدق الأمور المتغايرة في المفهوم على ذات واحدة جائز، كصدق الإنسان والضاحك والماشي - و غير ذلك من المفهومات المتغايرة – على زيد. وللخصم أن يقول : قد حملت مفهوم (ب) بهو هو على ما صدق عليه (ج). فنقول : ما صدق عليه (ج) إما أن يكون عين مفهوم (ب)، فلاحمل بحسب المعنى أو غيره، فيلزم الحكم بأن أحد المتغايرين هو الآخر – وهو باطل – بل نقول صدق مفهوم (ج) - على ما فرضت صدقه عليه أيضاً - باطل، لأنهما إن اتحدا فلا صدق بحسب المعنى، وإن تغايرا لم يصح أن يقال أحدهما هو الآخر - لا تقييداً ولا إخباراً - فقد تضاعفت الشبهة بذلك الجواب الحق، ولا تنحسم مادتها إلا بتحقيق معنى الصدق والحمل.

The Self-Refutation Argument and Its Limitation
Analysis of the self-refutation argument and its limitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate when a self-refutation argument is valid and identify its vulnerability against negative claims.

Core Idea
A self-refutation only works against an affirmative claim; a negative claim escapes it.

In this passage, the author presents an attempted refutation of the sophistical objection against predication. The refutation argues that the objection itself involves predication, thus it is self-refuting. However, the commentator Sharif points out a crucial limitation: this refutation holds only if the opponent's claim is affirmative. Since the opponent claims 'predication is impossible' (a negative), the self-refutation fails. Let's analyze this logical move.

Guided Path
1
State the opponent's objection
The opponent asserts that predication is impossible. This is a negative universal claim: 'No predication is possible.'
Negative claims assert the non-existence of something.
2
Identify the attempted refutation
The refutation claims that the opponent's statement itself involves predication (e.g., 'impossible' is predicated of 'predication'), thus it is self-contradictory.
This refutation assumes the opponent's claim is affirmative in form.
3
Check the logical condition
The commentator notes that the refutation is only valid if the opponent's claim is affirmative (e.g., 'Predication is possible'). If it is negative, the refutation does not apply because the negation does not entail its own truth.
An affirmative claim can be self-refuting if it implies its own negation; a negative claim does not have this property.
4
Conclude the limitation
The attempted rebuttal is insufficient against the negative objection. A deeper solution is needed, which the author proceeds to provide.
This highlights the importance of distinguishing between affirmative and negative claims in dialectical arguments.
