مفيداً ، وإن كان غيره : امتنع أن يقال : «أحدهما هو الآخر» لاستحالة أن يكون الشيء نفس ماليس هو هو.
(۲) لاله يجاب عنه بأن قولكم الحمل محال» يشتمل على الحمل، فيكون إيطالا للشيء بنفسه - وأنه محال.
(۱) إذ لاحمل بحسب المعنى، بل بحسب اللفظ فقط (شريف).
(۲ هذا الجواب معارضة لتلك الشبهة ؛ تقريرها أن مدعاكم - وهو قولكم : «الحمل محال باطل، لأنه مشتمل على صحة الحمل، إذ قد حمل فيه المحال على الحمل، فيكون مدعاكم مبطلاً لنفسه، وماكان مبطلاً لنفسه كان باطلاً، إذ لو كان حقاً لكان حقاً وباطلاً معاً - وهو محال.
ورد الشارح هذا الجواب بأنه إنما يصح إذا كان مدعى الخصم موجبة، وأما إذا كان مدعاه سالبة فلا يصح هذا الجواب قطعاً، بل يجب أن يقال : مفهوما (ج) و (ب) متغایران، ولا نعني بحمل (ب) على (ج) أن مفهوم (ج) هو عين مفهوم (ب) - فيلزم الحكم باتحاد المتغايرين - بل نعني كما تقدم أن «ماصدق عليه مفهوم (ج) من الأفراد يصدق عليه مفهوم (ب)» وصدق الأمور المتغايرة في المفهوم على ذات واحدة جائز، كصدق الإنسان والضاحك والماشي - و غير ذلك من المفهومات المتغايرة – على زيد.
وللخصم أن يقول : قد حملت مفهوم (ب) بهو هو على ما صدق عليه (ج). فنقول : ما صدق عليه (ج) إما أن يكون عين مفهوم (ب)، فلاحمل بحسب المعنى أو غيره، فيلزم الحكم بأن أحد المتغايرين هو الآخر – وهو باطل – بل نقول صدق مفهوم (ج) - على ما فرضت صدقه عليه أيضاً - باطل، لأنهما إن اتحدا فلا صدق بحسب المعنى، وإن تغايرا لم يصح أن يقال أحدهما هو الآخر - لا تقييداً ولا إخباراً - فقد تضاعفت الشبهة بذلك الجواب الحق،
ولا تنحسم مادتها إلا بتحقيق معنى الصدق والحمل.

وللسائل أن يعود ويقول : لاندعي الإيجاب، بل ندعي إما أن الحمل ليس بمفيد، أو أنه ليس بممكن وصدق السالبة لا ينافي كذب سائر الموجبات.
فالحق في الجواب أنا نختار أن مفهوم (ب) غير مفهوم (ج) وقوله : استحالة حمل (ب) على (ج) هو هو ». قلنا : لانسلم، وإنما يكون حمله عليه محالاً لو كان المراد به أن (ج) نفس (ب)؛ وليس كذلك، لما تبين أن المراد ماصدق عليه (ج) يصدق عليه (ب) ويجوز صدق الأمور المتغايرة بحسب المفهوم على ذات واحدة، فما صدق عليه (ج) يسمى ذات الموضوع ، ومفهوم (ج) يسمّى وصف الموضوع وعنوانه، لأنه يعرف بـه ذات (ج) الذي هو المحكوم عليه حقيقة كما يعرف الكتاب بعنوانه. والعنوان قد يكون عين الذات ، كقولنا : «كل إنسان حيوان» فإن حقيقة الإنسان عين ماهية زيد وعمرو وبكر وغيرهم من أفراده ؛ وقد يكون جزء لها، كقولنا : كلّ حيوان حسّاس» فإن الحكم فيه أيضاً على
فنقول : لابد في الحمل من تغاير طرفيه ذهناً، وإلا لم يتصور بينهما حمل أصلاً؛ ولابد أيضاً أن يتحدا وجوداً بحسب الخارج سواء كان محققاً أو موهوماً، لأن المتغايرين في الوجود الخارجي - المحقق أو الموهوم - يستحيل أن يحمل أحدهما على الآخر بهو هو بديهة، سواء فرض بينهما اتصال آخر أو لا؛ فمعنى الحمل اتحاد المتغايرين ذهناً في الوجود الخارجى محققاً أو موهوماً- كما حقق في موضعه (شريف). (۱) وذلك لأن العنوان كلّي، فإذا نسب إلى ماهية ماصدق عليه من أفراده، فلابد أن يكون أحد الأقسام الثلاثة - كما مرّ في الكليات الخمس (شريف).
Useful; and if it were otherwise, it would be prevented from saying: 'One of them is the other' due to the impossibility of a thing being the very thing it is not. (2) It is answered that your statement 'predication is impossible' includes predication, so it is an invalidation of the thing by itself—and that is impossible. (1) [Because there is no predication according to meaning, but only according to wording (Sharif).] (2) This answer is a rebuttal of that sophism; its formulation is that your claim—which is your statement 'predication is impossible'—is false because it includes the validity of predication, since in it the impossible is predicated of predication, so your claim invalidates itself, and whatever invalidates itself is false, because if it were true, it would be true and false together—and that is impossible. The commentator replied that this answer is only valid if the opponent's claim is affirmative; but if his claim is negative, this answer is definitely not valid. Rather, one must say: the concepts of (j) and (b) are different, and we do not mean by predicating (b) of (j) that the concept of (j) is identical to the concept of (b)—for that would entail the judgment of union of distinct things—but we mean, as previously stated, that 'what the concept of (j) applies to of individuals, the concept of (b) applies to it,' and the truth of concepts different in concept being true of one essence is permissible, like the truth of 'human,' 'laughing,' and 'walking'—and other different concepts—concerning Zayd. The opponent may say: You have predicated the concept of (b) as identical to what (j) applies to. We say: What (j) applies to is either identical to the concept of (b)—so there is no predication according to meaning—or other, which entails the judgment that one of the two distinct things is the other—and that is false. Rather, we say: the truth of the concept of (j) concerning what you assumed it is true of is also false, because if they are identical, there is no truth according to meaning; and if they are different, it is not correct to say that one of them is the other—neither restrictively nor informatively. Thus the sophism is compounded by that correct answer, and its substance is not cut off except by verifying the meaning of truth and predication. [PAGE_250] The questioner may return and say: We do not claim affirmation; rather, we claim either that predication is not useful or that it is not possible, and the truth of the negative does not contradict the falsehood of other affirmatives. The truth in the answer is that we choose that the concept of (b) is not the concept of (j), and his statement: 'the impossibility of predicating (b) of (j) as identical' we say: We do not accept that. Rather, it would be impossible to predicate it if what is meant were that (j) is identical to (b); but it is not so, because it has been made clear that what is meant is that what (j) applies to, (b) applies to it, and it is permissible for things different in concept to be true of one essence. So what (j) applies to is called the essence of the subject, and the concept of (j) is called the description of the subject and its title, because by it the essence of (j)—which is truly the subject of judgment—is known, just as a book is known by its title. The title may be identical to the essence, as in our saying: 'Every human is an animal,' for the reality of human is identical to the quiddity of Zayd, 'Amr, Bakr, and other individuals; or it may be a part of it, as in our saying: 'Every animal is sentient,' for the judgment in that is also upon... So we say: In predication, there must be a mental difference between its two sides, otherwise no predication could be conceived between them at all; and there must also be unity in existence with respect to the external world, whether it be real or imaginary, because two things distinct in external existence—real or imaginary—cannot possibly have one predicated of the other as identical intuitively, whether there is another connection between them or not. So the meaning of predication is the union of what is mentally distinct in external existence, real or imaginary—as verified in its place (Sharif). (1) That is because the title is universal, so when it is related to a quiddity of what it applies to of its individuals, it must be one of the three divisions—as mentioned in the five universals (Sharif).
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange defending the possibility of predication against a sceptical objection. The objection argues that predication is impossible because if the subject and predicate are identical in concept, the statement is tautological; if they are different, it asserts identity between distinct things, which is false. The author presents and critiques an initial answer—that the objection is self-refuting because it itself involves predication—pointing out that this only works against an affirmative claim, not a negative one. The correct solution is then given: predication does not require conceptual identity; it requires only that the predicate applies to the same individuals as the subject. The concepts may differ (e.g., 'human' and 'laughing' are different concepts but both apply to Zayd). The author further refines this by distinguishing between the 'essence of the subject' (the actual entity) and the 'title' (the concept used to refer to it). For predication to be meaningful, the two terms must be mentally distinct but existentially united in the external world (real or imaginary). The example of 'Every human is an animal' illustrates a case where the title is identical to the essence of the individuals, while 'Every animal is sentient' shows a case where the title is a part. This analysis upholds the logical validity of predication in all sciences, including fiqh (e.g., 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' where 'intoxicant' and 'forbidden' are different concepts but apply to the same substances).

A self-refutation argument against a claim is only valid if the claim is affirmative; negative claims are immune to this type of rebuttal.

The Correct Solution: Conceptual Difference and Existential Unity
Presentation of the correct solution for predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the distinction between concept and instantiation to validate predication in Islamic logical and legal reasoning.

Core Idea
Predication requires mental difference but existential unity of the referent.

After dismissing the flawed self-refutation, the author provides the correct solution to the sophistical objection against predication. The key insight: predication does not require the concepts of subject and predicate to be identical; it only requires that they apply to the same external individuals. This is the distinction between concept (mafhūm) and instance (miṣdāq). Let's unpack this solution step by step.

Guided Path
1
Separate concept from instance
Understand that the concepts of subject (j) and predicate (b) can be different in meaning (e.g., 'human' and 'laughing') while still applying to the same individual (e.g., Zayd).
This is the core distinction: mental difference vs. existential unity.
2
Define predication properly
Predication means that what the subject concept applies to (the individuals) is also what the predicate concept applies to. It does not mean that the concepts themselves are identical.
Thus, 'Zayd is laughing' is valid even though 'Zayd' and 'laughing' have different concepts.
3
Identify the opponent's mistake
The opponent falsely assumes that predication requires conceptual identity. By showing that conceptual difference is allowed as long as the referents coincide, the objection collapses.
The opponent's argument that predication is impossible because it would equate distinct concepts is based on a misunderstanding.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the fiqh ruling: 'Every intoxicant (khamr) is forbidden (haram).' The concept 'intoxicant' is different from 'forbidden', but both apply to the same substances (wine, etc.). The predication is valid and informative.
This shows how the distinction is used in legal reasoning.

The Title-Essence Distinction and Types of Predication
Refinement of predication with the title-essence distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify predications based on whether the title (ʿunwān) is identical to the essence, a part of it, or something else, and apply this to Islamic definitions.

Core Idea
The conceptual title may be identical to, a part of, or wholly different from the essence of the subject.

In the final part of the passage, the author introduces a further refinement: the subject of predication can be considered as an essence (dhāt) and the predicate as a title (ʿunwān). The title may relate to the essence in three ways, as seen in the five universals. This explains apparently different types of predication like 'Every human is an animal' (title identical to essence) vs. 'Every animal is sentient' (title part of essence). Let's examine this classification.

Guided Path
1
Understand the terminology
The 'essence of the subject' (dhāt al-mawḍūʿ) is the actual entity being referred to. The 'title' (ʿunwān) is the conceptual description used to refer to that entity.
E.g., in 'Zayd is laughing', Zayd is the essence, and 'laughing' is a title.
2
Identify the three relations
The title can be: (1) identical to the essence (e.g., 'human' is identical to the quiddity of Zayd), (2) a part of the essence (e.g., 'animal' is a part of the quiddity of a human), or (3) external to the essence (e.g., 'laughing' is an accident).
These correspond to the categories of essential (dhātī), part of essential (juzʾ dhātī), and accidental (ʿaraḍī).
3
Apply to example predications
'Every human is an animal': the title 'animal' is identical to the essence of humans (since animal is part of the quiddity of human? Actually, here the author says the title is identical to the essence? Wait: the passage says 'العنوان قد يكون عين الذات، كقولنا: كل إنسان حيوان' – but 'animal' is not the same concept as 'human'. Perhaps they mean that the essence of the individuals (e.g., Zayd) is the same as the essence that the title points to? This is a bit nuanced. In the five universals, 'human' is a species under 'animal' as genus, so the reality of human includes animality. The passage might mean that the title 'animal' is identical to the essence of the individuals in the sense that animality is part of the quiddity. Actually, the passage later says 'وقد يكون جزء لها، كقولنا: كل حيوان حساس' so 'animal' is part of the quiddity of sentient? Confusing. We interpret as per Sharif's explanation: the title may be the same as the essence (for essential predications where the predicate is the genus or differentia), or a part (e.g., genus), etc. For the sake of this lesson, we follow the text.
The author uses two examples: 'Every human is an animal' (title identical to essence) and 'Every animal is sentient' (title part of essence). This classification helps distinguish different types of predication.
4
Verify the necessity of mental difference
In all cases, the subject and predicate concepts are mentally distinct, but they must be existentially united. Even when the title is identical to the essence (as in the first example), there is still mental difference because the title is a concept while the essence is the external entity.
This reinforces the earlier solution: predication always requires mental difference and existential unity.

(۲) لاله يجاب عنه بأن قولكم الحمل محال» يشتمل على الحمل، فيكون إيطالا للشيء بنفسه - وأنه محال.
(۱) إذ لاحمل بحسب المعنى، بل بحسب اللفظ فقط (شريف).
(۲ هذا الجواب معارضة لتلك الشبهة ؛ تقريرها أن مدعاكم - وهو قولكم : «الحمل محال باطل، لأنه مشتمل على صحة الحمل، إذ قد حمل فيه المحال على الحمل، فيكون مدعاكم مبطلاً لنفسه، وماكان مبطلاً لنفسه كان باطلاً، إذ لو كان حقاً لكان حقاً وباطلاً معاً - وهو محال.
ورد الشارح هذا الجواب بأنه إنما يصح إذا كان مدعى الخصم موجبة، وأما إذا كان مدعاه سالبة فلا يصح هذا الجواب قطعاً، بل يجب أن يقال : مفهوما (ج) و (ب) متغایران، ولا نعني بحمل (ب) على (ج) أن مفهوم (ج) هو عين مفهوم (ب) - فيلزم الحكم باتحاد المتغايرين - بل نعني كما تقدم أن «ماصدق عليه مفهوم (ج) من الأفراد يصدق عليه مفهوم (ب)» وصدق الأمور المتغايرة في المفهوم على ذات واحدة جائز، كصدق الإنسان والضاحك والماشي - و غير ذلك من المفهومات المتغايرة – على زيد.
وللخصم أن يقول : قد حملت مفهوم (ب) بهو هو على ما صدق عليه (ج). فنقول : ما صدق عليه (ج) إما أن يكون عين مفهوم (ب)، فلاحمل بحسب المعنى أو غيره، فيلزم الحكم بأن أحد المتغايرين هو الآخر – وهو باطل – بل نقول صدق مفهوم (ج) - على ما فرضت صدقه عليه أيضاً - باطل، لأنهما إن اتحدا فلا صدق بحسب المعنى، وإن تغايرا لم يصح أن يقال أحدهما هو الآخر - لا تقييداً ولا إخباراً - فقد تضاعفت الشبهة بذلك الجواب الحق،
ولا تنحسم مادتها إلا بتحقيق معنى الصدق والحمل.

وللسائل أن يعود ويقول : لاندعي الإيجاب، بل ندعي إما أن الحمل ليس بمفيد، أو أنه ليس بممكن وصدق السالبة لا ينافي كذب سائر الموجبات.
فالحق في الجواب أنا نختار أن مفهوم (ب) غير مفهوم (ج) وقوله : استحالة حمل (ب) على (ج) هو هو ». قلنا : لانسلم، وإنما يكون حمله عليه محالاً لو كان المراد به أن (ج) نفس (ب)؛ وليس كذلك، لما تبين أن المراد ماصدق عليه (ج) يصدق عليه (ب) ويجوز صدق الأمور المتغايرة بحسب المفهوم على ذات واحدة، فما صدق عليه (ج) يسمى ذات الموضوع ، ومفهوم (ج) يسمّى وصف الموضوع وعنوانه، لأنه يعرف بـه ذات (ج) الذي هو المحكوم عليه حقيقة كما يعرف الكتاب بعنوانه. والعنوان قد يكون عين الذات ، كقولنا : «كل إنسان حيوان» فإن حقيقة الإنسان عين ماهية زيد وعمرو وبكر وغيرهم من أفراده ؛ وقد يكون جزء لها، كقولنا : كلّ حيوان حسّاس» فإن الحكم فيه أيضاً على
فنقول : لابد في الحمل من تغاير طرفيه ذهناً، وإلا لم يتصور بينهما حمل أصلاً؛ ولابد أيضاً أن يتحدا وجوداً بحسب الخارج سواء كان محققاً أو موهوماً، لأن المتغايرين في الوجود الخارجي - المحقق أو الموهوم - يستحيل أن يحمل أحدهما على الآخر بهو هو بديهة، سواء فرض بينهما اتصال آخر أو لا؛ فمعنى الحمل اتحاد المتغايرين ذهناً في الوجود الخارجى محققاً أو موهوماً- كما حقق في موضعه (شريف). (۱) وذلك لأن العنوان كلّي، فإذا نسب إلى ماهية ماصدق عليه من أفراده، فلابد أن يكون أحد الأقسام الثلاثة - كما مرّ في الكليات الخمس (شريف).
Useful; and if it were otherwise, it would be prevented from saying: 'One of them is the other' due to the impossibility of a thing being the very thing it is not. (2) It is answered that your statement 'predication is impossible' includes predication, so it is an invalidation of the thing by itself—and that is impossible. (1) [Because there is no predication according to meaning, but only according to wording (Sharif).] (2) This answer is a rebuttal of that sophism; its formulation is that your claim—which is your statement 'predication is impossible'—is false because it includes the validity of predication, since in it the impossible is predicated of predication, so your claim invalidates itself, and whatever invalidates itself is false, because if it were true, it would be true and false together—and that is impossible. The commentator replied that this answer is only valid if the opponent's claim is affirmative; but if his claim is negative, this answer is definitely not valid. Rather, one must say: the concepts of (j) and (b) are different, and we do not mean by predicating (b) of (j) that the concept of (j) is identical to the concept of (b)—for that would entail the judgment of union of distinct things—but we mean, as previously stated, that 'what the concept of (j) applies to of individuals, the concept of (b) applies to it,' and the truth of concepts different in concept being true of one essence is permissible, like the truth of 'human,' 'laughing,' and 'walking'—and other different concepts—concerning Zayd. The opponent may say: You have predicated the concept of (b) as identical to what (j) applies to. We say: What (j) applies to is either identical to the concept of (b)—so there is no predication according to meaning—or other, which entails the judgment that one of the two distinct things is the other—and that is false. Rather, we say: the truth of the concept of (j) concerning what you assumed it is true of is also false, because if they are identical, there is no truth according to meaning; and if they are different, it is not correct to say that one of them is the other—neither restrictively nor informatively. Thus the sophism is compounded by that correct answer, and its substance is not cut off except by verifying the meaning of truth and predication. [PAGE_250] The questioner may return and say: We do not claim affirmation; rather, we claim either that predication is not useful or that it is not possible, and the truth of the negative does not contradict the falsehood of other affirmatives. The truth in the answer is that we choose that the concept of (b) is not the concept of (j), and his statement: 'the impossibility of predicating (b) of (j) as identical' we say: We do not accept that. Rather, it would be impossible to predicate it if what is meant were that (j) is identical to (b); but it is not so, because it has been made clear that what is meant is that what (j) applies to, (b) applies to it, and it is permissible for things different in concept to be true of one essence. So what (j) applies to is called the essence of the subject, and the concept of (j) is called the description of the subject and its title, because by it the essence of (j)—which is truly the subject of judgment—is known, just as a book is known by its title. The title may be identical to the essence, as in our saying: 'Every human is an animal,' for the reality of human is identical to the quiddity of Zayd, 'Amr, Bakr, and other individuals; or it may be a part of it, as in our saying: 'Every animal is sentient,' for the judgment in that is also upon... So we say: In predication, there must be a mental difference between its two sides, otherwise no predication could be conceived between them at all; and there must also be unity in existence with respect to the external world, whether it be real or imaginary, because two things distinct in external existence—real or imaginary—cannot possibly have one predicated of the other as identical intuitively, whether there is another connection between them or not. So the meaning of predication is the union of what is mentally distinct in external existence, real or imaginary—as verified in its place (Sharif). (1) That is because the title is universal, so when it is related to a quiddity of what it applies to of its individuals, it must be one of the three divisions—as mentioned in the five universals (Sharif).
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange defending the possibility of predication against a sceptical objection. The objection argues that predication is impossible because if the subject and predicate are identical in concept, the statement is tautological; if they are different, it asserts identity between distinct things, which is false. The author presents and critiques an initial answer—that the objection is self-refuting because it itself involves predication—pointing out that this only works against an affirmative claim, not a negative one. The correct solution is then given: predication does not require conceptual identity; it requires only that the predicate applies to the same individuals as the subject. The concepts may differ (e.g., 'human' and 'laughing' are different concepts but both apply to Zayd). The author further refines this by distinguishing between the 'essence of the subject' (the actual entity) and the 'title' (the concept used to refer to it). For predication to be meaningful, the two terms must be mentally distinct but existentially united in the external world (real or imaginary). The example of 'Every human is an animal' illustrates a case where the title is identical to the essence of the individuals, while 'Every animal is sentient' shows a case where the title is a part. This analysis upholds the logical validity of predication in all sciences, including fiqh (e.g., 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' where 'intoxicant' and 'forbidden' are different concepts but apply to the same substances).

Key Terms
الحمل
المصداق
المفهوم
الذات
العنوان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is an example where the title is part of the essence?
A) 'Every human is an animal'
B) 'Every animal is sentient'
C) 'Every human is laughing'
D) 'Every book is readable'

Key Takeaway
The title (ʿunwān) in a predication may be identical to the essence of the subject, a part of it, or accidental, which corresponds to the classification of universals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following predications according to the title-essence relation: (a) 'Every human is rational' (human = species, rational = differentia). (b) 'Every substance is corporeal' (substance = genus, corporeal = property). Use the five universals.

The Objection and Reply on Predication's Possibility
Reply to the first objection about predication's possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the questioner's objection about predication and explain the author's reply using the distinction between concept and essence.

Core Idea
Predication requires conceptual difference but existential unity, not conceptual identity.

In this lesson, we analyze a dialectical exchange. A questioner argues that predication is either useless or impossible. The author responds by clarifying that predication does not require the subject concept to be identical to the predicate concept; it only requires that the referent of both is the same thing in external existence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The questioner claims that predication is either not beneficial (if concepts are the same) or not possible (if concepts differ), and also that the truth of a negative proposition does not require the falsehood of all affirmatives.
2
Find the author's initial move
The author says 'We choose that the concept of (b) is other than the concept of (j)' – asserting that the concepts differ, which is necessary for predication.
3
Understand the refutation
The author explains that predication only becomes impossible if one requires the subject to be identical to the predicate in concept, but predication means the subject's referent (essence) has the predicate's referent, not that the concepts are identical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللسائل أن يعود ويقول : لاندعي الإيجاب، بل ندعي إما أن الحمل ليس بمفيد، أو أنه ليس بممكن وصدق السالبة لا ينافي كذب سائر الموجبات.
فالحق في الجواب أنا نختار أن مفهوم (ب) غير مفهوم (ج) وقوله : استحالة حمل (ب) على (ج) هو هو ». قلنا : لانسلم، وإنما يكون حمله عليه محالاً لو كان المراد به أن (ج) نفس (ب)؛ وليس كذلك، لما تبين أن المراد ماصدق عليه (ج) يصدق عليه (ب) ويجوز صدق الأمور المتغايرة بحسب المفهوم على ذات واحدة، فما صدق عليه (ج) يسمى ذات الموضوع ، ومفهوم (ج) يسمّى وصف الموضوع وعنوانه، لأنه يعرف بـه ذات (ج) الذي هو المحكوم عليه حقيقة كما يعرف الكتاب بعنوانه.
فنقول : لابد في الحمل من تغاير طرفيه ذهناً، وإلا لم يتصور بينهما حمل أصلاً؛ ولابد أيضاً أن يتحدا وجوداً بحسب الخارج سواء كان محققاً أو موهوماً، لأن المتغايرين في الوجود الخارجي - المحقق أو الموهوم - يستحيل أن يحمل أحدهما على الآخر بهو هو بديهة، سواء فرض بينهما اتصال آخر أو لا؛ فمعنى الحمل اتحاد المتغايرين ذهناً في الوجود الخارجى محققاً أو موهوماً- كما حقق في موضعه (شريف). وذلك لأن العنوان كلّي، فإذا نسب إلى ماهية ماصدق عليه من أفراده، فلابد أن يكون أحد الأقسام الثلاثة - كما مرّ في الكليات الخمس (شريف). والعنوان قد يكون عين الذات ، كقولنا : «كل إنسان حيوان» فإن حقيقة الإنسان عين ماهية زيد وعمرو وبكر وغيرهم من أفراده ؛ وقد يكون جزء لها، كقولنا : كلّ حيوان حسّاس» فإن الحكم فيه أيضاً على زيد و عمرو وغيرهما من الأفراد، وحقيقة «الحيوان» إنما هي جزء لها ؛ وقد يكون خارجا عنها كقولنا كل ماش حيوان، فإن الحكم فيه أيضاً على زيد وعمرو وغيرهما من أفراده، ومفهوم «الماشي» خارج عن ماهياتها.
And the questioner may return and say: We do not claim affirmation, rather we claim either that predication is not beneficial, or that it is not possible, and the truth of the negative does not contradict the falsehood of the rest of the affirmatives. The correct answer is: We choose that the concept of (b) is other than the concept of (j), and his saying: 'The impossibility of bearing (b) upon (j) is the same [as the impossibility of predication]' We say: We do not accept. It would only be impossible for it to be borne upon it if by it is meant that (j) is the same as (b); and it is not so, because it is clear that what is intended is that whatever (j) is true of, (b) is true of, and it is permissible for things that differ in concept to be true of a single essence. So what (j) is true of is called the subject's essence, and the concept of (j) is called the description of the subject and its title, because by it the essence of (j) which is the true subject of judgment is known, just as a book is known by its title. We say: In predication there must be a mental difference between its two terms, otherwise no predication between them would be conceivable; and also they must be united in existence according to external reality, whether actual or hypothetical, because two things that differ in external existence - actual or hypothetical - it is impossible for one to be predicated of the other as identical, necessarily, whether there is another connection between them or not. So the meaning of predication is the union of two mentally distinct things in external existence, actual or hypothetical - as has been verified in its proper place. And that is because the title is a universal, so when it is related to the quiddity of the individuals to which it applies, it must be one of three divisions - as has passed in the five universals. The title may be identical to the essence, as in our saying 'Every human is an animal': for the reality of human is the same as the quiddity of Zayd and Amr and Bakr and other individuals of it; or it may be part of it, as in our saying 'Every animal is sensitive': for the judgment therein is also upon Zayd and Amr and others of its individuals, and the reality of 'animal' is only part of them; or it may be external to it, as in our saying 'Every walker is an animal': for the judgment therein is also upon Zayd and Amr and others of its individuals, and the concept of 'walker' is external to their quiddities.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange where the author answers a questioner's objection about predication. The objection claims that predication is either useless or impossible, and that the truth of a negative proposition does not require the falsehood of all affirmatives. The author responds by clarifying the nature of predication. First, he insists that the concepts of subject and predicate are different; the impossibility would only arise if we required the subject to be literally identical to the predicate, but predication only requires that the subject's referent also be the referent of the predicate. The author then lays down the general conditions for predication: the two terms must be different in the mind (otherwise no predication would be conceivable) but they must be united in external existence (actual or hypothetical). This is because things distinct in existence cannot be predicated as identical. Thus predication is the mental union of two distinct concepts in external reality. After establishing this, the author explains that since the title (the concept of the subject term) is a universal, when it is related to the quiddity of its individuals, it must fall into one of three categories: it may be identical to the essence, as in the case of 'human' when we say 'every human is an animal' (the concept 'human' is exactly the essence of its individuals); it may be part of the essence, as in 'animal' when we say 'every animal is sensitive' (the concept 'animal' is a part of the quiddity of individuals); or it may be external to the essence, as in 'walker' when we say 'every walker is an animal' (the concept 'walker' is not part of the quiddity). This threefold division is referenced to the earlier discussion of the five universals. Thus the author successfully refutes the questioner's doubt by showing that predication is always meaningful when these conditions are met.

المفهوم
الذات

C) Because the subject and predicate must be different in existence
D) Because the negative proposition is always false

Key Takeaway
Predication is valid when the subject and predicate differ in concept but unite in external existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Conditions of Valid Predication
Deriving the two conditions of predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for predication (mental difference and existential unity) and explain why both are necessary.

Core Idea
Predication unites two mentally distinct terms in a single external existence.

After refuting the objection, the author lays down the necessary conditions for any predication. If the terms are mentally identical, no predication is possible; if they are existentially distinct, predication is also impossible. Only when they differ in concept but share the same external referent can we predicate.

Guided Path
1
Find the first condition
The author says 'There must be a mental difference between its two terms' – without that, no predication is conceivable.
2
Find the second condition
They must be 'united in existence according to external reality, whether actual or hypothetical' – otherwise predication as identical is impossible.
3
Understand the justification
Things distinct in existence cannot be predicated as identical by necessity.
4
Restate the definition
Predication is the union of two mentally distinct things in external existence (actual or hypothetical).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the questioner may return and say: We do not claim affirmation, rather we claim either that predication is not beneficial, or that it is not possible, and the truth of the negative does not contradict the falsehood of the rest of the affirmatives. The correct answer is: We choose that the concept of (b) is other than the concept of (j), and his saying: 'The impossibility of bearing (b) upon (j) is the same [as the impossibility of predication]' We say: We do not accept. The objection claims that predication is either useless or impossible, and that the truth of a negative proposition does not require the falsehood of all affirmatives. The author responds by clarifying the nature of predication. First, he insists that the concepts of subject and predicate are different; the impossibility would only arise if we required the subject to be literally identical to the predicate, but predication only requires that the subject's referent also be the referent of the predicate. The author then lays down the general conditions for predication: the two terms must be different in the mind (otherwise no predication would be conceivable) but they must be united in external existence (actual or hypothetical). This is because things distinct in existence cannot be predicated as identical. Thus predication is the mental union of two distinct concepts in external reality. After establishing this, the author explains that since the title (the concept of the subject term) is a universal, when it is related to the quiddity of its individuals, it must fall into one of three categories: it may be identical to the essence, as in the case of 'human' when we say 'every human is an animal' (the concept 'human' is exactly the essence of its individuals); it may be part of the essence, as in 'animal' when we say 'every animal is sensitive' (the concept 'animal' is a part of the quiddity of individuals); or it may be external to the essence, as in 'walker' when we say 'every walker is an animal' (the concept 'walker' is not part of the quiddity). This threefold division is referenced to the earlier discussion of the five universals. Thus the author successfully refutes the questioner's doubt by showing that predication is always meaningful when these conditions are met.

Predication is valid only if the two terms differ in mental conception but refer to the same external reality.

The Three Relations of Subject Concept to Essence
Explaining the threefold relation between subject concept and essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three ways the concept (title) of the subject relates to its essence: identical, part, or external.

Core Idea
The subject concept may be the essence itself, a part of it, or completely external to it.

After establishing the general nature of predication, the author explains that since the subject term is a universal, when it is predicated of individuals, its concept can relate to the individuals' quiddity in one of three ways. This classification is crucial for understanding the semantics of propositions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the role of the title
The title (al-'unwan) is the concept of the subject term; it is a universal that applies to individuals.
2
First category: identity
Example: 'Every human is an animal' – the concept 'human' is the same as the essence of the individuals (Zayd, Amr, etc.).
3
Second category: part
Example: 'Every animal is sensitive' – the concept 'animal' is part of the quiddity of the individuals.
4
Third category: external
Example: 'Every walker is an animal' – the concept 'walker' is external to the quiddity of the individuals.
5
Connect to five universals
This division was previously mentioned in the discussion of the five universals, indicating that the title may correspond to a genus, differentia, or accident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللسائل أن يعود ويقول : لاندعي الإيجاب، بل ندعي إما أن الحمل ليس بمفيد، أو أنه ليس بممكن وصدق السالبة لا ينافي كذب سائر الموجبات.
فالحق في الجواب أنا نختار أن مفهوم (ب) غير مفهوم (ج) وقوله : استحالة حمل (ب) على (ج) هو هو ». قلنا : لانسلم، وإنما يكون حمله عليه محالاً لو كان المراد به أن (ج) نفس (ب)؛ وليس كذلك، لما تبين أن المراد ماصدق عليه (ج) يصدق عليه (ب) ويجوز صدق الأمور المتغايرة بحسب المفهوم على ذات واحدة، فما صدق عليه (ج) يسمى ذات الموضوع ، ومفهوم (ج) يسمّى وصف الموضوع وعنوانه، لأنه يعرف بـه ذات (ج) الذي هو المحكوم عليه حقيقة كما يعرف الكتاب بعنوانه.
فنقول : لابد في الحمل من تغاير طرفيه ذهناً، وإلا لم يتصور بينهما حمل أصلاً؛ ولابد أيضاً أن يتحدا وجوداً بحسب الخارج سواء كان محققاً أو موهوماً، لأن المتغايرين في الوجود الخارجي - المحقق أو الموهوم - يستحيل أن يحمل أحدهما على الآخر بهو هو بديهة، سواء فرض بينهما اتصال آخر أو لا؛ فمعنى الحمل اتحاد المتغايرين ذهناً في الوجود الخارجى محققاً أو موهوماً- كما حقق في موضعه (شريف). وذلك لأن العنوان كلّي، فإذا نسب إلى ماهية ماصدق عليه من أفراده، فلابد أن يكون أحد الأقسام الثلاثة - كما مرّ في الكليات الخمس (شريف). والعنوان قد يكون عين الذات ، كقولنا : «كل إنسان حيوان» فإن حقيقة الإنسان عين ماهية زيد وعمرو وبكر وغيرهم من أفراده ؛ وقد يكون جزء لها، كقولنا : كلّ حيوان حسّاس» فإن الحكم فيه أيضاً على زيد و عمرو وغيرهما من الأفراد، وحقيقة «الحيوان» إنما هي جزء لها ؛ وقد يكون خارجا عنها كقولنا كل ماش حيوان، فإن الحكم فيه أيضاً على زيد وعمرو وغيرهما من أفراده، ومفهوم «الماشي» خارج عن ماهياتها.
And the questioner may return and say: We do not claim affirmation, rather we claim either that predication is not beneficial, or that it is not possible, and the truth of the negative does not contradict the falsehood of the rest of the affirmatives. The correct answer is: We choose that the concept of (b) is other than the concept of (j), and his saying: 'The impossibility of bearing (b) upon (j) is the same [as the impossibility of predication]' We say: We do not accept. It would only be impossible for it to be borne upon it if by it is meant that (j) is the same as (b); and it is not so, because it is clear that what is intended is that whatever (j) is true of, (b) is true of, and it is permissible for things that differ in concept to be true of a single essence. So what (j) is true of is called the subject's essence, and the concept of (j) is called the description of the subject and its title, because by it the essence of (j) which is the true subject of judgment is known, just as a book is known by its title. We say: In predication there must be a mental difference between its two terms, otherwise no predication between them would be conceivable; and also they must be united in existence according to external reality, whether actual or hypothetical, because two things that differ in external existence - actual or hypothetical - it is impossible for one to be predicated of the other as identical, necessarily, whether there is another connection between them or not. So the meaning of predication is the union of two mentally distinct things in external existence, actual or hypothetical - as has been verified in its proper place. And that is because the title is a universal, so when it is related to the quiddity of the individuals to which it applies, it must be one of three divisions - as has passed in the five universals. The title may be identical to the essence, as in our saying 'Every human is an animal': for the reality of human is the same as the quiddity of Zayd and Amr and Bakr and other individuals of it; or it may be part of it, as in our saying 'Every animal is sensitive': for the judgment therein is also upon Zayd and Amr and others of its individuals, and the reality of 'animal' is only part of them; or it may be external to it, as in our saying 'Every walker is an animal': for the judgment therein is also upon Zayd and Amr and others of its individuals, and the concept of 'walker' is external to their quiddities.

The Referent of the Subject's Essence in a Proposition
Author analyzes proposition into assertions and subject referent based on universal type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two assertions in a proposition and determine the referent of the subject's essence based on whether the subject term is a species or a genus.

Core Idea
The subject term's reference expands from individuals to species when it is a genus.

In logic, every proposition contains two fundamental assertions: the assertion of the subject's own description (عقد الوضع) and the assertion of the predicate (عقد الحمل). Together they reveal three elements: the subject's essence, the truth of its description, and the truth of the predicate's description. But what does 'subject's essence' actually refer to? That depends on the type of universal the subject term is — species or genus. This lesson unpacks that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two assertions in a proposition
The first assertion (عقد الوضع) is the restrictive description of the subject: e.g., 'human is rational'. The second (عقد الحمل) is the predicative attribution: e.g., 'human is an animal'. Notice that both involve the subject's essence.
عقد الوضع is a specific post-classical term; it is the composition that defines what the subject is.
2
Locate the three things derived from the assertions
From the two assertions we get: (1) the subject's essence itself (ذات الموضوع), (2) the truth of the subject's description upon it (صدق وصفه عليه), and (3) the truth of the predicate's description upon it (صدق وصف المحمول عليه).
These three things are the building blocks for evaluating truth in a proposition.
3
Determine whether the subject term is a species or a genus
If the subject is a species (نوع) or its equivalents (differentia, property), then the subject's essence refers only to individual persons (e.g., Zayd, Amr). If it is a genus (جنس) or its equivalents (accident general), it refers to both individual persons and species natures (e.g., human, horse).
The text mentions 'what is equivalent' to species/genus: differentia and property for species; accident general for genus.
4
Apply the rule to the given examples
For 'Every human is rational' — human is a species, so the judgment applies only to individual humans. For 'Every animal is sensitive' — animal is a genus, so the judgment applies to both individual animals and the species under animal (human, horse, etc.).
The phrase 'individual persons' here refers to individual instances of the universal.
5
Test with a new proposition from fiqh or kalam
Take 'Every intoxicant is haram'. Is 'intoxicant' a genus? Yes, because it covers species like wine, beer, etc. So the judgment applies to both individual intoxicants (a specific cup) and the species of intoxicants (wine as a type).
This illustrates how the logical concept applies in Islamic legal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المحصورات الأربع
فمحصل مفهوم القضيّة يرجع إلى عقدين : عقد الوضع - وهـو اتصاف ذات الموضوع بوصفه - وعقد الحمل - وهو اتصاف ذات الموضوع بوصف المحمول ؛ والأوّل تركيب تقييدي، والثاني تركيب خبري.
فهنا ثلاثة أشياء : ذات الموضوع، وصدق وصفه عليه، وصدق وصف المحمول عليه.
أما ذات الموضوع فليس المراد به أفراد (ج) مطلقاً، بل الافراد الشخصية - إن كان (ج) نوعاً أو ما يساويه من الفصل والخاصة - والأفراد الشخصية والنوعية معاً - إن كان (ج) جنساً أو ما يساويه من العرض العام.
فإذا قلنا : «كلّ إنسان - أو كل ناطق، أو كل ضاحك - كذا»، فالحكم ليس إلا على زيد وعمرو وبكر وغيرهم من أفراده الشخصية. وإذا قلنا : «كلّ حيوان - أوكل ماش - كذا فالحكم على زيد وعمرو وغيرهما من أشخاص الحيوان، وعلى الطبائع النوعية من الإنسان والفرس وغيرهما. ومن هاهنا نسمعهم يقولون : حمل بعض الكليات على بعض إنما هو على النوع وأفراده».
The four quantified propositions. So the gist of the concept of the proposition reduces to two assertions: the assertion of position (subject's description) - which is the qualification of the subject's essence by its description - and the assertion of predication - which is the qualification of the subject's essence by the predicate's description. The first is a restrictive composition, and the second is a predicative composition. So here are three things: the subject's essence, the truth of its description upon it, and the truth of the predicate's description upon it. As for the subject's essence, it is not meant by it the individuals of (j) absolutely, but rather the individual persons - if (j) is a species or what is equivalent to it from the differentia and property - and both individual persons and specific natures together - if (j) is a genus or what is equivalent to it from the accident general. So if we say 'Every human - or every rational, or every laughing - is such and such', the judgment is only upon Zayd and Amr and Bakr and others of its individual persons. And if we say 'Every animal - or every walker - is such and such', the judgment is upon Zayd and Amr and others of the individuals of animal, and upon the specific natures of human and horse and others. And from here we hear them saying: 'The predication of some universals upon others is only upon the species and its individuals.'
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is a definitional analysis of the proposition (qadiyya). The author reduces the proposition to two fundamental assertions: the assertion of the subject's own description (عقد الوضع), which is the attribution of the subject's description to its essence (this is a restrictive composition, e.g., 'human' is that which is rational), and the assertion of the predicate (عقد الحمل), which is the attribution of the predicate's description to the same subject's essence (this is a predicative composition, e.g., 'human is an animal'). These two assertions reveal three things: the subject's essence itself, the truth (sidq) of the subject's description upon it, and the truth of the predicate's description upon it. The author then clarifies what 'subject's essence' refers to. He states that it is not simply the individuals of the subject term in an unrestricted sense. Rather, if the subject term is a species (or its equivalents: differentia or property), then the subject's essence refers only to the individual persons (e.g., Zayd, Amr) that instantiate that species. If the subject term is a genus (or its equivalents: accident general), then the subject's essence refers to both the individual persons and the specific natures (e.g., human, horse) that fall under that genus. Examples illustrate: when we say 'every human is ...', the judgment is only on Zayd, Amr, etc. When we say 'every animal is ...', the judgment is on both the individual animals (Zayd, Amr) and the species such as human and horse. The passage concludes with a reported dictum: 'The predication of some universals upon others is only upon the species and its individuals.' This highlights the nuanced reference of the subject term in logical propositions.

Key Takeaway
The referent of the subject's essence in a proposition depends on whether the subject term is a species (only individuals) or a genus (individuals and species natures).

The Two Contracts of a Proposition and Subject Scope
Author explains the two contracts and subject scope in quantified propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two contracts in any categorical proposition and determine the scope of the subject term based on its logical type.

Core Idea
Every proposition has two contracts: binding (subject to its own attribute) and predication (subject to the predicate's attribute).

In this passage, the author unpacks the internal structure of a categorical proposition, revealing that it consists of two distinct contracts: one that binds the subject to its own attribute, and another that attributes the predicate to the subject. He then explains that the scope of the subject term—whether it refers only to individual persons or also to specific natures—depends on whether the subject is a species, differentia, property, or a genus or general accident. Mastering this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting and constructing propositions in Islamic logic and its applications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two contracts
Look at the proposition and note that it involves two relational layers: the subject's essence being described by its own attribute (contract of binding) and by the predicate's attribute (contract of predication). For example, in 'Every human is rational', 'human' is described by its own definition (rational) in the binding contract, and by the predicate 'rational' in the predicative contract.
The contract of binding is a restrictive composition, the contract of predication is a predicative composition.
2
Distinguish the three elements
The author says there are three things: the subject's essence, the truth of its description (subject term applying), and the truth of the predicate's description. In 'Every human is rational', the subject's essence is the individuals (Zayd, 'Amr), the subject term 'human' must truly describe them, and the predicate 'rational' must truly describe them.
3
Determine the subject term's type
If the subject term is a species, differentia, or property (e.g., 'human', 'rational', 'laughing'), the judgment applies only to individual persons. If it is a genus or general accident (e.g., 'animal', 'walker'), the judgment applies to both individual persons and specific natures (like the species human and horse).
The passage uses the variable 'ج' for the subject term; 'نوعاً' means species, 'جنساً' means genus.
4
Apply to a new example
Given the proposition 'Every animal is mortal', identify that 'animal' is a genus, so the judgment 'mortal' applies to individuals (Zayd, 'Amr) and to specific natures (human, horse). Contrast with 'Every human is mortal' where 'human' is a species, so the judgment applies only to individual persons.
This distinction is vital for accurate reasoning in Islamic theology, e.g., when arguing about the attributes of Allah or the nature of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المحصورات الأربع
زید و عمرو وغيرهما من الأفراد، وحقيقة «الحيوان» إنما هي جزء لها ؛ وقد يكون خارجا عنها كقولنا كل ماش حيوان، فإن الحكم فيه أيضاً على زيد وعمرو وغيرهما من أفراده، ومفهوم «الماشي» خارج عن ماهياتها.
فمحصل مفهوم القضيّة يرجع إلى عقدين : عقد الوضع - وهـو اتصاف ذات الموضوع بوصفه - وعقد الحمل - وهو اتصاف ذات الموضوع بوصف المحمول ؛ والأوّل تركيب تقييدي، والثاني تركيب خبري.
فهنا ثلاثة أشياء : ذات الموضوع، وصدق وصفه عليه، وصدق وصف المحمول عليه.
أما ذات الموضوع فليس المراد به أفراد (ج) مطلقاً، بل الافراد الشخصية - إن كان (ج) نوعاً أو ما يساويه من الفصل والخاصة - والأفراد الشخصية والنوعية معاً - إن كان (ج) جنساً أو ما يساويه من العرض العام.
فإذا قلنا : «كلّ إنسان - أو كل ناطق، أو كل ضاحك - كذا»، فالحكم ليس إلا على زيد وعمرو وبكر وغيرهم من أفراده الشخصية. وإذا قلنا : «كلّ حيوان - أوكل ماش - كذا فالحكم على زيد وعمرو وغيرهما من أشخاص الحيوان، وعلى الطبائع النوعية من الإنسان والفرس وغيرهما. ومن هاهنا نسمعهم يقولون : حمل بعض الكليات على بعض إنما هو على النوع وأفراده».
The four quantified propositions. Zayd, 'Amr, and other individuals; the reality of 'animal' is only a part of them; and it may be external to them, as when we say 'every walker is an animal', for the judgment in it is also upon Zayd, 'Amr, and other individuals of it, and the concept of 'walker' is external to their essences. Thus the summarized concept of the proposition reduces to two contracts: the contract of binding - which is the subject's essence being described by its attribute - and the contract of predication - which is the subject's essence being described by the attribute of the predicate; the first is a restrictive composition, and the second is a predicative composition. So here are three things: the subject's essence, the truth of its description [by the subject term] upon it, and the truth of the predicate's description upon it. As for the subject's essence, what is meant by it is not the individuals of (ج) absolutely, but rather the individual persons - if (ج) is a species or what equals it from the differentia and property - and both the individual persons and specific natures together - if (ج) is a genus or what equals it from the general accident. Thus when we say: 'Every human - or every rational, or every laughing - is such-and-such', the judgment is only upon Zayd, 'Amr, Bakr, and other individual persons of it. And when we say: 'Every animal - or every walker - is such-and-such', the judgment is upon Zayd, 'Amr, and other individuals of animal, and upon the specific natures like human and horse and others. And from here we hear them saying: 'The predication of some universals upon others is only upon the species and its individuals.'
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage defines the structure of categorical propositions and clarifies the scope of the subject term in quantified propositions. The author begins by referencing the four quantified propositions and then provides examples using Zayd and 'Amr. He explains that the reality of a universal like 'animal' may be part of the individuals or external to them, depending on the predicate. Then he introduces the two contracts: the contract of binding (عقد الوضع), which is the subject's essence being described by its own attribute (e.g., 'human' is described as 'rational'), and the contract of predication (عقد الحمل), which is the subject being described by the predicate's attribute. The first is a restrictive composition (تركيب تقييدي) because it restricts the subject to a specific attribute; the second is a predicative composition (تركيب خبري) because it reports a relation. From these, three aspects emerge: the subject's essence itself, the truth of the subject's description upon it (i.e., that the subject term actually applies to the subject), and the truth of the predicate's description upon it. The author then addresses the scope of the subject term's referent. He clarifies that when the subject term (ج) is a species, differentia, or property, its judgment falls only on individual persons (e.g., 'every human' refers to Zayd, 'Amr, etc.). When the subject term is a genus or general accident, the judgment applies to both individual persons and specific natures (e.g., 'every animal' covers Zayd, 'Amr, and also the species human and horse). This is why scholars say that predication of some universals upon others is limited to the species and its individuals, not to the genus as a whole.

عقد الحمل

الأفراد الشخصية

Key Takeaway
Every proposition has two contracts (binding and predication), and the scope of the subject term depends on whether it is a species/differentia/property (individuals only) or a genus/general accident (individuals and specific natures).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Limiting Judgment to Individuals and the Repetition Argument
Author presents the limitation view and repetition argument with al-Sharif's commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why including the specific nature alongside individuals in a universal judgment leads to repetition and evaluate the counterargument about nature's specific rulings.

Core Idea
Including the specific nature with individuals in one proposition creates repetition.

This lesson examines a key debate in Islamic logic about the scope of universal judgments. Some scholars argue that propositions should only be about individual entities, not about the specific nature, because the nature only exists within its instances. We will explore their reasoning and the objection about nature having its own rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The passage reports that some eminent scholars restrict judgment absolutely to individual persons, i.e., universal predicates apply only to individuals, not to the specific nature (al-tabi'a al-naw'iyya).
This is their position.
2
Understand the reasoning
The specific nature does not independently have the predicate; only individuals do. The nature has no existence except within its individuals, so any predicate attributed to it is actually attributed because of an individual.
The nature is not an independent entity.
3
Analyze the repetition argument
If you include both the nature and its individuals in a single proposition, the predicate's establishment for all individuals already entails it for the nature, so adding the nature is redundant.
This is the core logical point.
4
Consider the counterargument
One might object that the nature has its own specific rulings (e.g., 'human is universal'), so including it adds meaning. But the response is that in a single proposition, the ruling must be common, so repetition remains.
Al-Sharif defends the original position.
5
Evaluate the strength
Assess which side is more convincing given the aim of logical analysis. The author finds the restriction close to verification.
Your own evaluation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الأفاضل من قصر الحكم مطلقاً على الأفراد الشخصية، وهو قريب إلى التحقيق; لأن اتصاف الطبيعة النوعية بالمحمول ليس بالاستقلال، بل لاتصاف شخص من أشخاصها به، إذ لا وجود لها إلا في ضمن شخص من أشخاصها). وأما صدق وصف الموضوع على ذاته فبالإمكان عند الفارابي، حتى أن المراد ب(ج) عنده ما أمكن أن يصدق عليه (ج)، سواء كان ثابتا له بالفعل أو مسلوباً عنه دائماً، بعد أن كان ممكن الثبوت له. وبالفعل عند الشيخ، أي ما يصدق عليه (ج) بالفعل – سواء كان
(۱) فلو اعتبر الطبيعة النوعية مع الأشخاص كان ذلك بحسب المعنى تكراراً، لأنه لما اعتبر ثبوت المحمول لجميع الأشخاص فقد اندرج فيه ثبوته للطبيعة النوعية فيلزم التكرار. لا يقال : إنما يلزم التكرار إذا لم يكن للطبيعة النوعية حكم يختص بها، وذلك ممنوع، إذ لا يلزم من عدم وجودها إلا في ضمن أشخاصها أن لا يكون لها أحكام مخصوصة بها، فإن طبيعة الإنسان كلّيّة وعامة - إلى غير ذلك من الأحوال التي لا تشاركها فيها أشخاصها. لانا نقول: الكلام في اعتبار الطبيعة مع الأشخاص في قضية واحدة، فلابد أن يكون الحكم الذي يكون فيها مشتركاً بينهما، فهاهنا - أعني في الأحكام المشتركة - يلزم التكرار (شريف). (۲) قيل إنما عدل الشيخ عن مذهب الفارابي واعتبر مع الإمكان الثبوت بالفعل، لأنّ الاقتصار على مجرد الإمكان مخالف للعرف واللغة، فإن الأسود إذا أطلق لم يفهم منه عرفاً ولغةً شيء لم يتصف بالسواد أزلاً وأبداً - وإن أمكن اتصافه به (شریف)
Among the eminent scholars is one who restricted the judgment absolutely to individual persons, and this is close to verification; because the attribution of the specific nature to the predicate is not independent, but rather due to the attribution of a person from its persons to it, since it has no existence except within a person of its persons. As for the truth of the description of the subject upon its essence, it is by possibility according to al-Farabi, such that what is meant by (ج) according to him is that which it is possible to describe as (ج), whether it is actually established for it or perpetually negated from it, as long as it was possible for it to be established. And by actuality according to al-Shaykh [Ibn Sina], i.e., that which is actually described as (ج) – whether it is... (1) For if one considered the specific nature together with the persons, that would be, in terms of meaning, a repetition, because when the establishment of the predicate for all persons is considered, its establishment for the specific nature is included in it, so repetition follows. It cannot be said: Repetition would only follow if the specific nature did not have a ruling specific to it, and that is denied, for the fact that it has no existence except within its persons does not necessitate that it has no rulings specific to it; for the nature of 'human' is universal and general – and other attributes that its persons do not share with it. But we say: The discussion is about considering the nature together with the persons in a single proposition, so the ruling must be common between them. Here – I mean in the common rulings – repetition follows (al-Sharif). (2) It is said that al-Shaykh only deviated from al-Farabi's doctrine and required actual possibility because restricting to mere possibility is contrary to custom and language, for when 'black' is uttered, conventionally and linguistically nothing that has not been characterized by blackness ever is understood from it – even if it were possible for it to be characterized (al-Sharif).
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage addresses two related issues. First, the author reports a view from some eminent scholars who restrict the judgment in quantified propositions solely to individual persons, not including specific natures. They argue that the attribution of a predicate to a specific nature is not independent but depends on its individuals, since the nature only exists within individuals. The author finds this view close to verification. Second, the passage discusses the condition of the subject term's description: must it be actually true or only possibly true? Al-Farabi holds that it is enough for the subject to be possibly described by the term, even if it is never actually so described. For example, if the subject term is 'black', it suffices that something could possibly be black, even if it is never black. Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh) disagrees and requires actual truth: the subject must actually be described by the term. The footnote by al-Sharif elaborates on the first issue: combining the specific nature with individuals leads to repetition because the predicate's establishment for individuals already covers the nature. The objection that the nature has its own specific rulings is rejected because in a single proposition the ruling is common, so repetition follows. The second footnote explains Ibn Sina's rationale: mere possibility contradicts linguistic custom. For example, if we say 'black', we do not conventionally understand something that is never black. Thus Ibn Sina's position aligns with usage.

التكرار
الأفراد الشخصية

D) Because the judgment must be limited to individuals only

Including the specific nature alongside its individuals in one proposition is redundant because the predicate for individuals already entails the nature.

The Farabi-Ibn Sina Dispute on Subject Term Description
Author presents the dispute on the condition of subject description with al-Sharif's explanatory footnote.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-Farabi's and Ibn Sina's conditions for the subject term in a proposition and identify the linguistic argument supporting Ibn Sina.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina requires that the subject term actually describes its subject; al-Farabi only requires possibility.

A central issue in propositional logic is whether the subject term must be actually true of the subject or merely possibly true. Al-Farabi and Ibn Sina held opposing views, with Ibn Sina citing linguistic custom as his basis. This lesson unpacks their positions and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Al-Farabi holds that the subject term (ج) only needs to be possibly applicable to the subject, even if never actually true. Ibn Sina requires that it be actually true of the subject.
The variable (ج) represents any subject term.
2
Understand al-Farabi's view
For al-Farabi, what is meant by (ج) is that which it is possible to describe as (ج), whether actual or perpetually negated, as long as it was possible.
Example: 'black' can apply to something that could be black even if never black.
3
Understand Ibn Sina's view
Ibn Sina requires that (ج) actually describes the subject, i.e., the subject is actually characterized by the term.
Example: 'black' only applies to something actually black.
4
Analyze Ibn Sina's rationale
Al-Sharif explains that Ibn Sina deviated because mere possibility contradicts linguistic custom. For instance, when 'black' is uttered, we do not conventionally understand something that never was black.
Language usage grounds logic.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the proposition 'The burning bush is a theophany'. According to Ibn Sina, the subject must actually be a theophany; according to al-Farabi, it is enough that it could be.
Think about epistemological implications.

Al-Farabi vs Ibn Sina on Subject Truth
Author compares al-Farabi's and Ibn Sina's views on the truth condition of the subject term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-Farabi's and Ibn Sina's conditions for the truth of the subject term in universal propositions.

Core Idea
Rulings apply to actual individuals, not merely possible ones — Ibn Sina's view is closer to custom.

In Islamic logic, the truth of a universal proposition depends on whether the subject term truly applies to its referents. Two major figures, al-Farabi and Ibn Sina, disagree on this condition: al-Farabi allows merely possible subjects, while Ibn Sina insists on actual existence at some time. This passage compares their views and weighs them against customary language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
What does 'truth of the description of the subject' mean? It is whether the subject term truly describes its instances. For example, for 'Every black is ...' to be true, what must be true about being black?
2
Extract al-Farabi's condition
Al-Farabi requires only possibility (imkan): the subject term could possibly apply to an individual, even if it never actually does. Thus 'Every black...' includes all possible black things, like the Romans (who are white but could be black).
This is a very broad scope.
3
Extract Ibn Sina's condition
Ibn Sina requires actuality (fi'l): the subject term must actually apply to an individual at some time (past, present, or future). So 'Every black...' excludes the Romans because they are never actually black.
This is narrower and matches common usage.
4
Apply the example
Take the proposition 'Every black is a color.' Under al-Farabi, this includes all possible black things (even nonexistent). Under Ibn Sina, it only includes actual black things. Which seems more natural?
The author says Ibn Sina's view is closer to custom ('urf).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الأفاضل من قصر الحكم مطلقاً على الأفراد الشخصية، وهو قريب إلى التحقيق؛ لأن اتصاف الطبيعة النوعية بالمحمول ليس بالاستقلال، بل لاتصاف شخص من أشخاصها به، إذ لا وجود لها إلا في ضمن شخص من أشخاصها). وأما صدق وصف الموضوع على ذاته فبالإمكان عند الفارابي، حتى أن المراد ب(ج) عنده ما أمكن أن يصدق عليه (ج)، سواء كان ثابتـا لـه بالفعل أو مسلوباً عنه دائماً، بعد أن كان ممكن الثبوت له. (۲) وبالفعل عند الشيخ، أي ما يصدق عليه (ج) بالفعل – سواء كان ذلك الصدق في الماضي أو الحاضر أو المستقبل - حتى لا يدخل فيه ما لا يكون (ج) دائماً. فإذا قلنا : كل أسود كذا» يتناول الحكم كل ما أمكن أن يكون أسود - حتي الروميين مثلاً - على مذهب الفارابي لإمكان اتصافهم بالسواد، وعلى مذهب الشيخ لا يتناولهم الحكم لعدم اتصافهم بالسواد في وقت ما. ومذهب الشيخ أقرب إلى العرف.
And among the eminent scholars, there is one who restricts the judgment absolutely to individual instances, and this is close to verification; because the attribution of the specific nature to the predicate is not independent, but rather due to the attribution of an individual among its individuals to it, since it has no existence except within an individual of its individuals. As for the truth of the description of the subject on its essence, it is by possibility according to al-Farabi, such that what is meant by (J) according to him is what could possibly be true of (J), whether it is actually established for it or always negated from it, after it was possible to be established for it. (2) And by actuality according to al-Shaykh (Ibn Sina), i.e., what (J) is actually true of — whether that truth is in the past, present, or future — so that it does not include that which is not (J) always. So if we say: 'Every black is such-and-such,' the judgment encompasses everything that could possibly be black — even the Romans, for example — according to al-Farabi's school, due to the possibility of their being black; and according to al-Shaykh's school, the judgment does not encompass them because they are not black at any time. And al-Shaykh's school is closer to custom.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a comparative discussion of two views on the truth condition of the subject term in universal propositions. The author begins by mentioning a scholar (al-Fadil?) who restricts the judgment solely to individual instances, which is close to verification because the specific nature's attribution is not independent. He then moves to the core issue: the truth of the description of the subject (e.g., 'black') on its essence. Al-Farabi holds that possibility (imkan) suffices — the subject could be black, even if it never actually is. Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh) insists on actuality (bil fi'l) — the subject must actually have the property at some time. The author gives an example: 'Every black is such-and-such.' According to al-Farabi, this includes even the Romans because they could possibly be black. According to Ibn Sina, it does not include them because they are not black at any time (presumably in the context of the example). The author concludes that Ibn Sina's view is closer to customary usage. This discussion clarifies the scope of universal affirmative propositions: whether they range over all possible individuals or only actual ones.

Key Takeaway
The truth condition of the subject in a universal proposition can be possibility (al-Farabi) or actuality (Ibn Sina), with the latter being more aligned with common usage.

Haqiqiyya vs Kharijiyya Universal Propositions
Author defines haqiqiyya and kharijiyya universal affirmatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between haqiqiyya and kharijiyya universal affirmative propositions and identify which is used in scientific discourse.

Core Idea
Haqiqiyya propositions range over all possible individuals; kharijiyya only over actual existents.

In Islamic logic, universal affirmative propositions like 'Every J is B' can be interpreted in two ways: one that considers all possible individuals of J, even if they never exist, and one that considers only those that actually exist. This distinction is crucial in scientific propositions, where we often reason about general natures regardless of actual instantiation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantifier
In 'Every J is B', the universal quantifier can be interpreted over actual instances or over all possible instances. This passage clarifies two interpretations.
2
Understand 'haqiqa' (reality)
The term haqiqiyya means considering the nature of J itself, not limited to existing individuals. The proposition is true if every possible J would be B.
The author says haqiqiyya is 'as if it is the reality of the proposition used in the sciences'.
3
Understand 'kharij' (external)
Kharijiyya restricts to Js that actually exist in the external world. The proposition is true if every actual J is B.
External is defined as 'external to the senses' (al-kharij 'an al-masha'ir).
4
Apply the distinction with examples
For example, 'Every anqaa is a bird' is haqiqiyya because anqaa is mythical; the proposition is still meaningful. For actual birds, 'Every raven is black' is kharijiyya but could be evaluated on actual ravens.
Anqaa is a legendary bird from Arabic lore.
5
Recognize scientific usage
Haqiqiyya is the standard in sciences because scientific laws apply to all possible instances of a type, not just observed ones.
The author explicitly states haqiqiyya is used in the sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما صدق وصف المحمول على ذات الموضوع فقد يكون بالضرورة (۱) وبالإمكان وبالفعل وبالدوام - على ماسيجيء في بحث الموجهات. وإذا تقررت هذه الأصول فنقول : قولنا : «كلّ ج ب» يعتبر تارة بحسب الحقيقة، وتسمّى حينئذ «حقيقية» كأنها حقيقة القضية المستعملة في العلوم، وأخرى بحسب الخارج، وتسمّى «خارجية» والمراد بالخارج الخارج عن المشاعر أما الأوّل فنعني به كلّ مالو وجد كان (ج) من الأفراد الممكنة فهو بحيث لو وجد كان (ب) فالحكم فيه ليس مقصوراً على ماله وجود في الخارج فقط، بل على كل ماقدر وجوده - سواء كان موجوداً في الخارج أو معدوماً - فـ «ج» إن لم يكن موجوداً فالحكم فيه على أفراده المقدرة للوجود - كقولنا : كلّ عنقاء طائر » - وإن كان موجوداً فالحكم ليس (۱) المشاعر هي القوى الدراكة، جمع «مشعر» - بفتح الميم أو كسرها - أي موضع الشعور أو آلته (شريف).
And as for the truth of the description of the predicate on the essence of the subject, it may be by necessity (1) or by possibility or by actuality or by perpetuity — as will come in the discussion of modal propositions. And when these foundations are established, we say: Our statement 'Every J is B' is considered sometimes according to reality (haqiqa) and is then called 'haqiqiyya' — as if it is the reality of the proposition used in the sciences — and sometimes according to external existence (kharij) and is called 'kharijiyya'. And by 'external existence' is meant being external to the senses. As for the first type (haqiqiyya), we mean by it every possible individual such that if it existed it would be J, and it is such that if it existed it would be B. So the judgment in it is not restricted only to what has existence externally, but rather to everything whose existence is conceived — whether it exists externally or is nonexistent. So if J is not existent, the judgment is on its hypothetical individuals — like our statement: 'Every anqaa is a bird' — and if it is existent, the judgment is not... (1) 'al-masha'ir' are the perceptive faculties, plural of 'mash'ar' — with fatha or kasra on the mim — i.e., the locus of sensation or its instrument (Sharif).
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage defines two types of universal affirmative propositions. The author first notes that the truth of a predicate on the subject can be necessary, possible, actual, or perpetual (modalities to be discussed later). After establishing these principles, he introduces the division: 'Every J is B' can be considered either haqiqiyya (according to reality) or kharijiyya (according to external existence). Haqiqiyya is the type used in the sciences; it considers all possible individuals of J, whether they exist in the external world or not. For example, 'Every anqaa (a mythical bird) is a bird' is true for all possible anqaa even if none actually exist. Kharijiyya, on the other hand, restricts the judgment to actually existing individuals. The author defines al-kharij as 'external to the senses,' meaning actual reality. The passage ends with a footnote explaining that al-masha'ir are the perceptive faculties. This classification clarifies the scope of universal quantifiers in logic.

Key Terms
حقيقية
خارجية
المشاعر

B) Haqiqiyya considers all possible individuals; kharijiyya considers only actual ones.
C) Haqiqiyya is used in poetry; kharijiyya in science.
D) They are synonymous.

Key Takeaway
Haqiqiyya universal affirmatives consider all possible individuals, whereas kharijiyya restrict to actual existents; scientific propositions are haqiqiyya.

Haqiqiyya vs Kharijiyya Universal Propositions
Author distinguishes haqiqiyya and kharijiyya propositions with condition of possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between haqiqiyya and kharijiyya universal propositions and identify the role of the possibility condition in haqiqiyya.

Core Idea
A haqiqiyya proposition applies to all possible individuals, not just those that actually exist.

In this passage, the author introduces a crucial distinction in universal propositions: those that consider all possible individuals (haqiqiyya) versus those restricted to actual existents (kharijiyya). This distinction is fundamental for understanding how universal statements are used in Islamic sciences, especially in definitions and proofs. You will learn the logical condition that makes haqiqiyya propositions universally true.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Locate the terms 'حقيقية' and 'خارجية' in the Arabic. Note that both are forms of 'كل ج ب' but differ in scope.
The text explicitly says 'تارة بحسب الحقيقة' and 'أخرى بحسب الخارج'.
2
Extract the scope of each type
For haqiqiyya, the judgment applies to all possible individuals of the subject, whether existent or not. For kharijiyya, it applies only to those actually existing at some time. Find the examples: phoenix for non-existent, human for existent.
The author says 'فالحكم فيه ليس مقصوراً على ماله وجود في الخارج فقط'.
3
Recognize the condition of possibility
Understand why the author imposes 'الإمكان' for haqiqiyya. Without this condition, we could imagine a J that is not B, making the universal false.
The text argues 'لأنه لو أطلقت لم تصدق كلية أصلا'.
4
Compare the schools of al-Farabi and Ibn Sina
Note that al-Farabi considers possibility of the descriptive attribute applying, while Ibn Sina requires actual application. This affects whether the possibility condition is needed.
The commentary (Sharif) clarifies when the condition is necessary.
