المحصورات الأربع. ذلك الصدق في الماضي أو الحاضر أو المستقبل - حتى لا يدخل فيه ما لا يكون (ج) دائماً. فإذا قلنا : كل أسود كذا» يتناول الحكم كل ما أمكن أن يكون أسود - حتي الروميين مثلاً - على مذهب الفارابي لإمكان اتصافهم بالسواد، وعلى مذهب الشيخ لا يتناولهم الحكم لعدم اتصافهم بالسواد في وقت ما. ومذهب الشيخ أقرب إلى العرف. وأما صدق وصف المحمول على ذات الموضوع فقد يكون بالضرورة وبالإمكان وبالفعل وبالدوام - على ماسيجيء في بحث الموجهات. وإذا تقررت هذه الأصول فنقول : قولنا : «كلّ ج ب» يعتبر تارة بحسب الحقيقة، وتسمّى حينئذ «حقيقية» كأنها حقيقة القضية المستعملة في العلوم، وأخرى بحسب الخارج، وتسمّى «خارجية» والمراد بالخارج الخارج عن المشاعر أما الأوّل فنعني به كلّ مالو وجد كان (ج) من الأفراد الممكنة فهو بحيث لو وجد كان (ب) فالحكم فيه ليس مقصوراً على ماله وجود في الخارج فقط، بل على كل ماقدر وجوده - سواء كان موجوداً في الخارج أو معدوماً - فـ «ج» إن لم يكن موجوداً فالحكم فيه على أفراده المقدرة للوجود - كقولنا : كلّ عنقاء طائر » - وإن كان موجوداً فالحكم ليس مقصوراً على أفراده الموجودة - بل عليها وعلى أفراده المقدرة الوجود أيضاً - كقولنا : «كل إنسان حيوان». وإنما قيد الأفراد بالإمكان لأنه لو أطلقت لم تصدق كلية أصلا : أما الموجبة : فلأنه إذا قيل : كلّ ج ب » بهذا الاعتبار، فنقول : ليس كذلك لأن (ج) الذي ليس (ب) لووجد كان (ج) وليس (ب)، فبعض مالو وجد كان (ج) فهو بحيث لو وجدكان ليس (ب)، وإنه يناقض «كل ج ب » بهذا الاعتبار. يعنى اعتبر المصنف إمكان وجود أفراد الموضوع في القضية الحقيقية، لأن الحكم فيها يتناول الأفراد المقدّرة في الخارج، ومن جملتها مالا يكون ممكن الوجود فيه، فلايكون الحكم فيها - سواء كان إيجابياً أو سلبياً - صادقاً عليه، فلا تصدق قضية كلية أصلاً، بل تصدق في كل مادة تفرض موجبة جزئية أو سالبة جزئية كما قررة. وهذا القيد - أعني إمكان وجود الأفراد - إنما يحتاج إليه إذا لم يعتبر إمكان صدق الوصف العنواني على ذات الموضوع بحسب نفس الأمر، بل يكتفى بمجرد فرض صدقه عليه أو إمكان فرض صدقه عليه، كما في صدق الكلّي على جزئياته، حتى إذا وقع الكلّي موضوعاً للقضية الكلية كان متناولاً الجميع أفراده التي هو كلي بالقياس إليها سواء أمكن صدقه عليها أو لا. وأما إذا اعتبر إمكان صدق الوصف العنواني على ذات الموضوع في نفس الأمر - كما هو مذهب الفارابي - أو اعتبر مع الإمكان الصدق بالفعل كما هو مذهب الشيخ - فلا حاجة إلى اعتبار إمكان وجود الأفراد والمحذور مندفع، فإن الإنسان الذي ليس بحيوان لا يصدق عليه الإنسان في نفس الأمر ، فلا يدخل في قولنا : «كل إنسان حيوان وكذا الإنسان الحجري لا يصدق عليه الإنسان في نفس الأمر، فلا يدخل في قولنا : لاشيء من الإنسان بحجر» (شريف).
The four quantified propositions. That truth in the past, present, or future - so that it does not include what is not (J) always. So if we say: 'Every black is such-and-such', the judgment encompasses everything that could possibly be black - even the Romans, for example - according to the school of al-Farabi, due to the possibility of their being described by blackness. And according to the school of al-Shaykh, the judgment does not encompass them due to their not being described by blackness at any time. And the school of al-Shaykh is closer to common usage. As for the truth of the predicate's description applying to the subject's essence, it may be by necessity, possibility, actuality, or perpetuity - as will come in the discussion of modal propositions. And when these principles are established, we say: Our statement 'Every J is B' is considered sometimes according to reality (haqiqiyya) and is then called 'haqiqiyya', as if it is the true proposition used in the sciences, and sometimes according to externality (kharijiyya) and is called 'kharijiyya', meaning by 'external' that which is external to the senses. As for the first, we mean by it: Every individual that, if it existed, is (J) from among possible individuals, so that if it existed it would be (B). Thus the judgment in it is not restricted to what has existence in the external only, but rather to everything whose existence is conceived - whether it exists externally or is non-existent. So if (J) does not exist, the judgment applies to its individuals conceived for existence - like our statement 'Every phoenix is a bird'. And if it does exist, the judgment is not restricted to its existing individuals, but rather to them and also to its individuals conceived for existence - like our statement 'Every human is an animal'. And the individuals are only restricted by possibility because if left unrestricted, no universal could be true at all. As for the affirmative: because if it is said 'Every J is B' in this sense, we say: It is not so, because (J) that is not (B), if it existed, would be (J) and not (B). So some of what, if it existed, is (J), is such that if it existed, it is not (B), and this contradicts 'Every J is B' in this sense. [Commentary:] That is, the author considered the possibility of the existence of the individuals of the subject in the haqiqiyya proposition, because the judgment in it encompasses the individuals conceived in the external, and among them are those whose existence is not possible in it, so the judgment - whether affirmative or negative - would not be true of it, so no universal proposition would be true at all. Rather, it is true only in every material that is supposed as a particular affirmative or particular negative, as established. And this condition - namely the possibility of existence of the individuals - is only needed if the possibility of the truth of the descriptive attribute (al-wasf al-‘unwani) on the subject's essence according to the actual state of affairs is not considered, but rather merely its supposition or the possibility of its supposition is sufficient, as in the truth of the universal on its particulars, so that if the universal is the subject of a universal proposition, it encompasses all its individuals to which it is universal, whether its truth applies to them or not. But if the possibility of the truth of the descriptive attribute on the subject's essence in the actual state of affairs is considered - as is the school of al-Farabi - or if actual truth along with possibility is considered, as is the school of al-Shaykh - then there is no need to consider the possibility of the existence of the individuals, and the difficulty is avoided. For the human that is not an animal - 'human' is not true of it in the actual state of affairs, so it does not enter into our statement 'Every human is an animal'. Likewise, the stone human - 'human' is not true of it in the actual state of affairs, so it does not enter into our statement 'No human is a stone' (Sharif).
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes two types of universal affirmative propositions: haqiqiyya (real) and kharijiyya (external), and discusses the necessary condition of possibility for the haqiqiyya. The author begins by noting that truth in propositions concerns past, present, or future, but not what is never the case. Then, using the example 'Every black is such-and-such', he explains that according to al-Farabi, the judgment covers everything that could possibly be black (e.g., Romans, even if not actually black), while according to Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh), it covers only those actually described by blackness at some time. The author then explains that the truth of a predicate's description of the subject's essence can be necessary, possible, actual, or perpetual, as will be detailed later in modal propositions. He then states that the statement 'Every J is B' can be considered in two ways: haqiqiyya, which is the true proposition used in the sciences, and kharijiyya, which considers actual external existence. The haqiqiyya proposition's judgment applies to all possible individuals of J, whether existent or not, as in 'Every phoenix is a bird' (where 'phoenix' has no actual existence) or 'Every human is an animal' (where it covers both actual and possible humans). The author then argues that without the condition of possibility, no universal affirmative would be true, because if we did not restrict individuals to possible ones, then we could conceive a J that is not B, which would contradict the universal claim. The commentary from Sharif clarifies that the author added the condition of possibility to avoid this problem, and that this condition is only needed if we do not consider the actual truth of the descriptive attribute (i.e., we are not assuming that the subject term actually applies). If we do consider actual truth (as in Ibn Sina's view) or possibility of truth (as in al-Farabi's view), then the condition of possibility of existence is unnecessary because the subject term itself guarantees that only actual or possible bearers are considered. The example given: a human that is not an animal is not actually human, so it is excluded from 'Every human is an animal'. Thus, the passage carefully sets out the truth conditions for universal propositions and explains the differences between major logicians on this point.

Key Terms
حقيقية (haqiqiyya): A universal proposition whose judgment applies to all possible individuals of the subject, whether existing externally or not. [Note: In later logical works, the term haqiqiyya is sometimes used interchangeably with 'natural' propositions, but here it is contrasted with kharijiyya.]
خارجية (kharijiyya): A universal proposition whose judgment is restricted to individuals existing externally at some time. [Note: This term is less common in later texts; some later logicians drop the distinction entirely.]
الإمكان (al-imkan): The condition that individuals of the subject must be possible in existence for the universal proposition to be true. [Note: Here imkan is a condition for haqiqiyya propositions; in modal logic, imkan is one of the modal qualifiers.]
الوصف العنواني (al-wasf al-‘unwani): The descriptive attribute that defines the subject term, such as 'human' or 'black'. [Note: This term is specific to this logical discussion; not widely used elsewhere.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the text, what is the key difference between a haqiqiyya and a kharijiyya universal proposition?
A) Haqiqiyya only considers actual individuals; kharijiyya considers possible individuals.
B) Haqiqiyya considers all possible individuals; kharijiyya only considers actual external individuals.
C) Haqiqiyya is used only in theology; kharijiyya is used in logic.
D) There is no difference; they are synonyms.

Key Takeaway
The truth of a haqiqiyya universal affirmative depends on the condition that the subject's individuals are possible, while a kharijiyya restricts to actual existents.

Condition of Possibility in Universal Propositions
Commentator explains the necessity of possibility condition and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why universal propositions in Islamic logic require the possibility of individuals, and apply this condition to evaluate the truth of a universal claim.

Core Idea
Universal propositions must range only over possible individuals; otherwise they become false.

This passage delves into a key condition for universal propositions: the individuals covered must be possible in existence. Without this condition, any universal claim could be falsified by assuming impossible individuals. We will unpack the reasoning, the objection, and the resolution, which hinges on the nature of the truth of universals over their individuals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and its motivation
The commentator states that universal propositions like 'Every human is an animal' apply to both existing and estimated (possible) individuals. Without the condition of possibility (imkan), no universal would be true because impossible individuals could be assumed that contradict the claim.
The condition is needed only when the truth of the descriptive attribute (e.g., 'human') is taken by mere assumption, not as actual.
2
Analyze the affirmative case
For an affirmative universal 'Every J is B', if we consider a J that is not B but is possible, it would contradict the universal. However, if we restrict J to possible individuals, such impossible individuals are excluded. The passage uses the example: a human who is not an animal cannot be a possible individual, so the universal holds.
The contradiction arises because the impossible individual, if assumed, would be an individual of J by assumption.
3
Analyze the negative case
For a negative universal 'No J is B', similarly, a J that is B would contradict if it were possible. Restricting to possible individuals avoids this.
The same reasoning applies symmetrically.
4
Examine the objection and response
An objection argues that a non-human human is not truly an individual of 'human' because the universal is not true of it in actuality. The commentator responds that in this framework, truth of universals over individuals is based on mere assumption, not actual reality. So an assumed non-human human is still an individual of 'human' by assumption.
This distinguishes the approach from al-Farabi and Ibn Sina, who consider actual truth and thus don't need the condition.

Source Text

وإنما قيد الأفراد بالإمكان لأنه لو أطلقت لم تصدق كلية أصلا : أما الموجبة : فلأنه إذا قيل : كلّ ج ب » بهذا الاعتبار، فنقول : ليس كذلك لأن (ج) الذي ليس (ب) لووجد كان (ج) وليس (ب)، فبعض مالو وجد كان (ج) فهو بحيث لو وجدكان ليس (ب)، وإنه يناقض «كل ج ب » بهذا الاعتبار.
۱) يعنى اعتبر المصنف إمكان وجود أفراد الموضوع في القضية الحقيقية، لأن الحكم فيها يتناول الأفراد المقدّرة في الخارج، ومن جملتها مالا يكون ممكن الوجود فيه، فلايكون الحكم فيها - سواء كان إيجابياً أو سلبياً - صادقاً عليه، فلا تصدق قضية كلية أصلاً، بل تصدق في كل مادة تفرض موجبة جزئية أو سالبة جزئية كما قررة.
وهذا القيد - أعني إمكان وجود الأفراد - إنما يحتاج إليه إذا لم يعتبر إمكان صدق الوصف العنواني على ذات الموضوع بحسب نفس الأمر، بل يكتفى بمجرد فرض صدقه عليه أو إمكان فرض صدقه عليه، كما في صدق الكلّي على جزئياته، حتى إذا وقع الكلّي موضوعاً للقضية الكلية كان متناولاً الجميع أفراده التي هو كلي بالقياس إليها سواء أمكن صدقه عليها أو لا. وأما إذا اعتبر إمكان صدق الوصف العنواني على ذات الموضوع في نفس الأمر - كما هو مذهب الفارابي - أو اعتبر مع الإمكان الصدق بالفعل كما هو مذهب الشيخ - فلا حاجة إلى اعتبار إمكان وجود الأفراد والمحذور مندفع، فإن الإنسان الذي ليس بحيوان لا يصدق عليه الإنسان في نفس الأمر ، فلايدخل في قولنا : «كل إنسان حيوان وكذا الإنسان الحجري لا يصدق عليه الإنسان في نفس الأمر، فلا يدخل في قولنا : لاشيء من الإنسان بحجر» (شريف).
لا يقال : هب أنّ (ج) الذي ليس (ب) لو وجد كان (ج) وليس (ب) ولكن لا نسلم أنه يصدق حينئذ بعض ما لو وجد كان (ج) فهو بحيث لو وجد كان (ج) وليس (ب) » فإنّ الحكم في القضية إنما هو على أفراد (ج)، ومن الجائز أن لايكون (ج) - الذي ليس (ب)- من أفراد (ج)؛ فإنّا إذا قلنا : «كلّ إنسان حيوان فالإنسان الذي ليس بحيوان ليس من أفراد الإنسان، لأنّ الكلّي يصدق على أفراده، والإنسان ليس بصادق على الإنسان الذي ليس بحيوان.
لانا نقول: قد سبقت الإشارة في مطلع باب الكليات إلى أن صدق الكلي على أفراده ليس بمعتبر بحسب نفس الأمر، بل بحسب مجرد الفرض ؛ فإذا فرض إنسان ليس بحيوان فقد فرض أنه إنسان، فيكون من أفراده.
وأما السالبة فلأنه إذا قيل : لاشيء من ج ب » فنقول : إنه كاذب، لأنّ (ج) الذي هو (ب) لو وجدكان (ج) و (ب)، فبعض مالووجد كان (ج) فهو بحيث لو وجدكان (ب)، وهو يناقض قولنا : «لاشيء مما لو وجد كان (ج) فهو بحيث لو وجد كان (ب)»؛ ولما قيد الموضوع بالإمكان اندفع الاعتراض، لأنّ (ج) الذي ليس (ب) في الإيجاب و(ج) الذي (ب) في السلب وإن كان فرداً لـ(ج) لكن يجوز أن يكون ممتنع الوجود في الخارج، فلايصدق «بعض مالو وجـد كـان (ج) من الأفراد الممكنة فهو بحيث لو وجد كان ليس (ب)» ، ولا «بعض مالو وجد كان (ج) من الأفراد الممكنة فهو بحيث لو وجدكان (ب)»؛ فلا يلزم كذب الكليتين.
Restricted to its existing individuals – rather, on them and on its individuals whose existence is estimated as well – like our statement: 'Every human is an animal.' And the individuals were restricted with possibility because if they were unrestricted, no universal would be true at all. As for the affirmative: because if it is said 'Every J is B' with this consideration, we say: it is not so because J that is not B, if it existed, would be J and not B. So some of what if it existed would be J is such that if it existed it would be not B, and that contradicts 'Every J is B' with this consideration. (1) Meaning: the author considered the possibility of the existence of the individuals of the subject in the real proposition, because the judgment in it encompasses the individuals estimated in existence, and among them are those whose existence is not possible in the external world. So the judgment in it – whether affirmative or negative – is not true of it. So no universal proposition is true at all, but rather a particular affirmative or particular negative is true in every assumed matter, as was established. And this condition – namely the possibility of the existence of individuals – is only needed if the possibility of the truth of the descriptive attribute on the essence of the subject in themselves is not considered, but rather one is satisfied with mere assumption of its truth or possibility of assuming its truth, as in the truth of the universal about its particulars. So when the universal occurs as subject of a universal proposition, it encompasses all its individuals in relation to which it is a universal, whether its truth is possible for them or not. But if the possibility of the truth of the descriptive attribute on the essence of the subject in themselves is considered – as is the doctrine of al-Farabi – or actual truth is considered along with possibility as is the doctrine of the Chief (Ibn Sina) – then there is no need to consider the possibility of the existence of individuals, and the problem is resolved. For a human who is not an animal – 'human' is not true of him in themselves, so he does not enter into our statement 'Every human is an animal'; and likewise the stone human – 'human' is not true of him in themselves, so he does not enter into our statement 'No human is a stone.' (al-Sharif) It cannot be said: Suppose that J that is not B, if it existed, would be J and not B, but we do not grant that then some of what if it existed would be J is such that if it existed would be J and not B – for the judgment in the proposition is only on the individuals of J, and it is possible that J that is not B is not among the individuals of J. For if we say 'Every human is an animal,' the human that is not an animal is not an individual of human, because the universal is true of its individuals, and 'human' is not true of a human who is not an animal. To this we say: reference was previously made at the beginning of the chapter on universals that the truth of the universal about its individuals is not considered in themselves, but rather according to mere assumption. So if a human who is not an animal is assumed, it is assumed that he is a human, so he is among its individuals. As for the negative: because if it is said 'No J is B,' we say: it is false, because J that is B, if it existed, would be J and B. So some of what if it existed would be J is such that if it existed would be B, and that contradicts our statement 'Nothing of what if it existed would be J is such that if it existed would be B.' And when the subject is restricted with possibility, the objection is repelled, because J that is not B in the affirmative and J that is B in the negative, although it is an individual of J, it is possible that its existence is impossible in the external world. So it is not true that 'some of what if it existed would be J from the possible individuals is such that if it existed would be not B,' nor 'some of what if it existed would be J from the possible individuals is such that if it existed would be B.' Thus the falsity of the two universal propositions does not follow.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is an expanded commentary (sharh) on the condition of possibility (imkan) for individuals in universal propositions, specifically in what are called 'real propositions' (al-qadhiya al-haqiqiyya). The commentator begins by stating that a universal proposition like 'Every human is an animal' should be understood as covering not only actually existing individuals but also those whose existence is estimated (muqaddara). The key point is that the subject term 'human' in such a proposition applies to any individual that could be human, regardless of actual existence. However, if we did not restrict the individuals to those that are possible in existence, no universal proposition could be true. The commentator explains why: for an affirmative universal 'Every J is B', if we consider an impossible individual like a J that is not B (e.g., a non-human human), if it existed it would be J and not B, contradicting the universal. So to avoid contradiction, we must only consider possible individuals. The commentator then notes that this condition of possibility is only needed in an approach where the truth of the descriptive attribute (like 'human') on the essence is taken merely by assumption, not as actually true in reality. In contrast, al-Farabi and Ibn Sina (the Chief) considered actual truth or possibility of truth, making the condition unnecessary. The passage then addresses an objection: one might argue that a non-human human is not truly an individual of 'human' because the universal 'human' does not apply to it in itself. The commentator replies that earlier it was established that the truth of a universal about its individuals is based on mere assumption, not actual reality. So if we assume a non-human human, it is assumed to be human, thus it is an individual. Similarly for negative universals, the condition of possibility prevents falsity by excluding impossible individuals. In summary, this passage clarifies the logical necessity of restricting the subject of universal propositions to possible individuals when dealing with hypothetical or assumed existence, a key distinction in Islamic logic.

D) Because it simplifies the logic.

Key Takeaway
Universal propositions in Islamic logic require the subject to be restricted to possible individuals to prevent falsification by impossible hypotheticals, a condition especially needed when the truth of universals over individuals is based on mere assumption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Subject's Individuals in Real Propositions
Author refines the extension of universals in real propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a hypothetical individual is included in the scope of a universal in a real proposition and explain the roles of supposition (farad) and possibility (imkan).

Core Idea
In real propositions, universals include even merely possible individuals — truth is by supposition, not actual existence.

The author addresses a subtle objection: if the subject of a real proposition is 'what if it existed would be J', does a J that is not B count as an individual of J? The answer hinges on two key concepts from the chapter on universals: supposition (farad) and possibility (imkan). Understanding this distinction is essential to correctly evaluate universal propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector claims that a J that is not B might not be an individual of J, because the universal is true only of its actual individuals. The example 'Every human is an animal' is given: a human that is not an animal is not a true individual of 'human'.
Note the universal affirmative form: Every J is B, with the real proposition conditional interpretation.
2
Understand the author's first response
The author recalls a principle from the beginning of the universals chapter: the truth of a universal over its individuals is based on mere supposition (farad), not actual existence. So if you suppose a human that is not an animal, that supposed entity is an individual of 'human'.
This principle allows the universal to cover hypothetical individuals.
3
Analyze the negative case
For the universal negative 'No J is B', a similar problem arises: a J that is B would contradict the statement. The author invokes the restriction to possible existence (imkan): any J that is B but impossible in external existence is not considered, so the universal remains true.
The real proposition only quantifies over possible individuals (al-afrad al-mumkina).
4
Apply the concepts
In practice, to test whether a universal holds as a real proposition, assume any individual that could exist (possible) and check if it satisfies the predicate. If a hypothetical individual would break the rule, it must be impossible, not merely non-existent.
This is used in theological and legal arguments.

Source Text

It should not be said: Suppose that (J) which is not (B) if it existed would be (J) and not (B), but we do not concede that there is then some of what if it existed would be (J), it is such that if it existed would be (J) and not (B). For the judgment in the proposition is only upon the individuals of (J), and it is possible that (J) which is not (B) is not an individual of (J). For if we say 'Every human is an animal,' then the human that is not an animal is not an individual of human, because the universal is true of its individuals, and 'human' is not true of the human that is not an animal.
We say: The indication has preceded in the beginning of the chapter on universals that the truth of the universal over its individuals is not considered according to actual reality, but according to mere supposition; so if one supposes a human that is not an animal, one has supposed it to be human, so it is among its individuals.
As for the negative, if it is said 'No J is B,' we say it is false, because (J) which is (B) if it existed would be (J) and (B), so some of what if it existed would be (J) is such that if it existed would be (B), and that contradicts our statement 'Nothing of what if it existed would be (J) is such that if it existed would be (B).' And when the subject was restricted by possibility, the objection is repelled, because (J) which is not (B) in the affirmative and (J) which is (B) in the negative, even if they are individuals of (J), it is possible that they are impossible in external existence, so it is not true that 'some of what if it existed would be (J) from possible individuals is such that if it existed would not be (B)' nor 'some of what if it existed would be (J) from possible individuals is such that if it existed would be (B)'; so the falsity of both universal propositions does not follow.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer (naqd wa jawab) concerning the real proposition (al-qadiyya al-haqiqiyya). The objection is that in the universal affirmative 'Every J is B', the subject term 'J' is interpreted as 'what if it existed would be J', and the predicate as 'what if it existed would be B'. The objector claims that a J that is not B may not be an individual of J because the universal is true only of its actual individuals. The example given is 'Every human is an animal': a human that is not an animal is not a true individual of human. The author responds by citing a principle from the beginning of the chapter on universals: the truth of a universal over its individuals is based on mere supposition (farad), not actual existence. So if one supposes a human that is not an animal, that supposed human is an individual of human. Thus the objection fails. For the universal negative 'No J is B', a similar objection arises: a J that is B would contradict the statement, but it might be an individual of J. The author answers that by restricting the subject to possible existence (imkan), the objection is repelled because those problematic individuals might be impossible in external existence, so they are not considered in the real proposition which only quantifies over possible individuals. Hence the universal propositions are not falsified.

Key Takeaway
In real propositions, universals extend to all merely possible individuals through supposition, and the restriction to possible existence prevents impossible counterexamples from undermining the universal.

Refuting the Implication Interpretation of Conditional in Real Propositions
Author critiques the interpretation of conditional phrases in real propositions as implications and offers an alternative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why conditional phrases in real propositions do not indicate logical implication and how they serve as generalization devices.

Core Idea
Conditionals in real propositions are not implications but tools to include hypothetical individuals.

In classical logic, real propositions (qadaya haqiqiyya) are defined using conditional phrases like 'if it existed it would be J'. Some logicians, including the author of al-Kashaf, interpreted these conditionals as necessary implications (luzum). However, this passage shows that such an interpretation is mistaken. The conditional is merely a device to include hypothetical individuals in the judgment, not to express a logical connection. This lesson will guide you through the critique and the correct understanding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional elements
Notice that the real proposition uses 'if it existed it would be J' for the subject term and 'if it existed it would be B' for the predicate term. These appear as conditional statements.
2
Distinguish between implication and coincidence
The text gives two types of conditionals: by implication (luzum) like 'if sun risen then day exists', and by coincidence (ittifaq) like 'if man rational then donkey brays'. The conditionals in real propositions could be either, not necessarily implication.
3
Recognize al-Kashaf's error
Al-Kashaf and his followers reduced all such conditionals to implication, claiming the real proposition means a logical connection between subject and predicate.
4
Apply the commentator's correction
According to the gloss, the real proposition is categorical (hamli), not conditional. The conditional phrasing is only to generalize over both actual and hypothetical individuals. For example, 'Every J is B' normally applies only to actual J's; the conditional reminds that hypothetical J's are included too.
The example of 'if sun is risen then day exists' is true even at night (hypothetically), showing it does not require actuality.
5
Handle the objection about the predicate side
If only generalization were needed, why include the conditional in the predicate? The answer: in inverted propositions (munharifa) where the quantifier is attached to the predicate, both subject and predicate need the conditional to cover hypotheticals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما اعتبر في عقد الوضع الاتصال - وهو قولنا : «لو وجد كان (ج)» - وكذا في عقد الحمل - وهو قولنا : «لو وجد كان (ب)»- والاتصال قديكون بطريق اللزوم كقولنا إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود وقد يكون بطريق الاتفاق كقولنا : إن كان الإنسان ناطقاً فالحمار ناهق» فسره صاحب الكشف ومن تابعه باللزوم، فقالوا : معنى قولنا : «كل ما
(1) هذا بحسب الظاهر من العبارة صحيح، فإن قولك «لووجد كان ج» متصلة، وكذا قولك «لووجدكان ب متصلة أخري. وأما بحسب المعنى : فينبغي أن لا يقصد هناك اتصال قطعاً، لأن هذه العبارة تفسير للقضية الحملية، وقد عرفت أن عقد الوضع فيها تركيب تقييدي، فكيف يتصور أن يكون معناه متصلة، وأن عقد الحمل فيها تركيب خبري، لكنه حملي لا اتصالي ; فليس في مفهوم القضية الحقيقية معنى الاتصال أصلاً، فكيف يفسر بمعنى متصلين ؟ بل يجب أن يحمل عبارة الشرط على قصد التعميم في أفراد الموضوع بحيث يندرج فيها الأفراد المحققة والمقدرة، فإنّك إذا قلت : «كل ج ب » يتبادر ، أن الحكم على كل ماهو (ج) في الخارج محققاً، فأورد كلمة الشرط في التفسير تنبيهاً على دخول الأفراد المقدّرة أيضاً في الحكم، فإن كلمة الشرط تستعمل في المحققات والمقدّرات، كقولك في النهار : «إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود»، وكقولك في الليل : إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود».
فإن قلت : فعلى هذا يكفي إيراد الشرط في جانب الموضوع وبلغو إيراده في جانب المحمول، لأن المقصود منه المفهوم لا الأفراد.
قلت : قد يقصد بالمحمول الأفراد إذا كانت القضية منحرفة، وهي أن يكون السور مذكوراً في جانب المحمول، سواء ذكر في جانب الموضوع، أو لا ، فإيراد الشرط في المحمول ينفعك في المنحرفات (شريف).
And when the conditional was considered in the subject term — which is our statement 'if it existed it would be (J)' — and likewise in the predicate term — which is our statement 'if it existed it would be (B)' — and the conditional may be by way of implication, like our statement 'if the sun is risen then day exists,' and may be by way of coincidence, like our statement 'if man is rational then the donkey is braying.' The author of al-Kashaf and his followers interpreted it as implication, and they said: the meaning of our statement 'every (J) is (B)' ... 
(1) This is correct according to the apparent wording, for your statement 'if it existed it would be (J)' is a conditional, and likewise your statement 'if it existed it would be (B)' is another conditional. But according to meaning: it should not be intended there as a conditional at all, because this statement is an interpretation of the categorical proposition, and you have known that the subject term in it is a restrictive composition, so how can it be imagined that its meaning is a conditional? And that the predicate term is a predicative composition, but it is categorical, not conditional. So there is no meaning of conditionality at all in the concept of the real proposition, so how can it be interpreted as two conditionals? Rather, the conditional phrasing must be taken as intending generalization over the individuals of the subject such that it includes both actual and hypothetical individuals. For if you say 'Every J is B,' it is understood that the judgment is on every J that is actually in external existence, so the conditional word was brought in the interpretation as a reminder of the inclusion of hypothetical individuals as well in the judgment, for the conditional word is used for both actuals and hypotheticats, like your statement in daytime: 'if the sun is risen then day exists,' and like your statement at night: 'if the sun is risen then day exists.'
If you say: According to this, it would be sufficient to bring the conditional in the subject side and it would be useless to bring it in the predicate side, because the intended is the concept, not the individuals.
I say: The individuals may be intended by the predicate if the proposition is inverted (munharifa), which is that the quantifier is mentioned on the predicate side, whether it is mentioned on the subject side or not, so bringing the conditional in the predicate benefits you in inverted propositions (al-Sharif).
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the interpretation of the real proposition. The main text notes that the real proposition involves conditional phrases in both the subject term ('if it existed it would be J') and the predicate term ('if it existed it would be B'). These conditionals can be of two types: implication (luzum) like 'if the sun is risen then day exists', or coincidence (ittifaq) like 'if man is rational then the donkey is braying'. The author of al-Kashaf and his followers interpreted these conditionals as implications. The gloss (likely by al-Sharif) corrects this: apparently, the wording suggests two conditionals, but in reality the real proposition is a categorical (hamli) proposition, not a conditional. The subject term is a restrictive composition, and the predicate is a predicative composition; thus the conditional phrases are not meant as logical conditionals but merely as a device to generalize over both actual and hypothetical individuals. The example: 'Every J is B' normally refers only to actual J's, so the conditional word is added to remind that hypothetical individuals are also included. The conditional word is used for both actual and hypothetical contexts, as seen in the example 'if the sun is risen then day exists' which is true even when the sun is not risen (hypothetically). Then an objection (firqa) is raised: if the purpose is only generalization, why include the conditional in the predicate side? The answer (qult) is that in inverted propositions (munharifa) where the quantifier is attached to the predicate, the predicate side also needs to include hypothetical individuals, so the conditional is necessary there.

Key Takeaway
The conditional phrase in the real proposition does not indicate logical implication but is a device to extend the judgment to hypothetical individuals, preserving the proposition's categorical nature.

Outside and Real Propositions: Definitions and Timing
Definition and clarification of outside and real propositions with timing examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between outside (kharijiyya) and real (haqiqiyya) propositions and explain why the subject's description need not hold at the time of judgment.

Core Idea
Outside propositions judge actual existents; real propositions judge possible ones; timing of description is irrelevant.

In Islamic logic, propositions are classified based on the existential status of their subjects. This lesson unpacks the definitions of 'outside' and 'real' propositions from the source passage and clarifies a common confusion: whether the subject must be described by its predicate at the very moment of judgment. The answer is no, and understanding why is essential for accurate logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions
Find where the passage defines 'outside' (kharijiyya) and 'real' (haqiqiyya) propositions. What does each require regarding the existence of the subject's individuals?
Outside requires actual external existence; real requires only possibility.
2
Understand the timing condition
The passage uses the example 'every writer is laughing' to show that the subject's description (writer) does not need to hold at the same time as the predicate (laughing). Why is this allowed? Trace the explanation that the judgment is on the essence, not the description.
The sleeper/awake example makes the point vividly.
3
Apply with an Islamic example
Consider the proposition 'Every person who performed Hajj is rewarded.' Is this outside or real? At what times does the subject have the property of having performed Hajj and being rewarded? Does simultaneity matter?
Reward may come after Hajj; the essence is the person who performed Hajj.
4
Test the distinction
Take 'Every angel is obedient.' Can this be an outside proposition? Why or why not? (Consider whether all angels exist externally.)
Angels exist, so outside is possible if we assume actual existence.

وأما الثاني فيراد به كلّ (ج) في الخارج فهو (ب) في الخارج، والحكم فيه على الموجود في الخارج، سواء كان اتصافه ب(ج) حال الحكم أو قبله أو بعده، لأن مالم يوجد في الخارج أزلاً وأبداً يستحيل أن يكون (ب) في الخارج. وإنما قال : «سواء كان حال الحكم أو قبله أو بعده» دفعاً لتوهم من ظن أن معنى (ج) (ب) هو اتصال الجيم بالبائية حال كونه موصوفاً بالجيمية، فإن الحكم فيه ليس على وصف الجيم حتى يجب تحققه في الخارج حال تحقق الحكم - بل على ذات الجيم - فلا يستدعي الحكم إلا وجوده؛ وأما اتصافة بالجيمية فلا يجب تحققه حال تحقق الحكم، فإذا قلنا : «كل كاتب ضاحك فليس من شروط كون ذات الكاتب موضوعاً أن يكون كاتباً في وقت كونه موصوفاً بالضحك، بل يكفي في ذلك أن يكون موصوفاً بالكاتبية في وقت ما حتى يصدق قولنا : «كل نائم مستيقظ»، وإن كان اتصاف ذات النائم بالوصفين إنما هو في وقتين. لا يقال : هاهنا قضايا لا يمكن أخذها بأحد الاعتبارين، وهي التي المراد «كل ما صدق عليه (ج) في الخارج» تعين الحكم على الموجود الخارجي تحقيقاً فقط، لأن ما لم يوجد أصلا لم يصدق عليه (ج) في الخارج (شريف). (١) أي دفع بماذكره ذلك التوهم لكونه باطلا، لأن الحكم ليس على وصف الجيم الخ (شریف). (٢) يعنى أن مثل قولنا : «كل ممتنع معدوم قضيّة لا يمكن أخذها خارجية - وهو ظاهر، إذ ليس أفراد الموضوع موجودة في الخارج محققاً - ولا حقيقية - إذ لا يمكن وجود أفراده في الخارج، وقد اعتبر في الحقيقية إمكان وجود الأفراد كما مر. وأجاب بأن المقصود ضبط القضايا المستعملة في العلوم في الأغلب، و ما ذكر تم تما يستعمل نادراً، فلم يلتفتوا إليه، إذلم يمكنهم إدراجه في القواعد بسهولة. ومنهم من جعل أمثال هذه القضايا ذهنيّة، فقال : معنى قولك : «كل
As for the second, it means: every (j) in the external world is (b) in the external world, and the judgment therein applies to what exists externally, whether its description with (j) is at the time of judgment, before it, or after it, because that which does not exist externally at all, eternally, is impossible to be (b) externally. He said "whether at the time of judgment, before it, or after it" to repel the misconception of one who thinks that the meaning of (j) (b) is the conjunction of (j) with (b) at the time it is described with (j); for the judgment therein is not upon the description of (j) such that it must be realized externally at the time of judgment—rather it is upon the essence of (j)—so the judgment does not require anything but its existence; as for its description with (j), its realization at the time of judgment is not necessary. So when we say "Every writer is laughing," it is not a condition for the essence of the writer to be the subject that he be a writer at the time he is described with laughter; rather it suffices that he be described with writerhood at some time, so that our statement "Every sleeper is awake" holds, even if the description of the sleeper's essence with the two descriptions is at two times. It should not be said: There are propositions that cannot be taken under either consideration, which are those where what is meant is "everything that (j) applies to in the external world" is limited to judgment upon existing external things only because what never existed did not have (j) apply to it externally. (Sharif) That is, he repelled that misconception by what he mentioned because it is false, for the judgment is not upon the description of (j) etc. (Sharif) That is, such as our statement "Every impossible is non-existent" is a proposition that cannot be taken as external—this is obvious, since the individuals of the subject are not actually existent externally—nor as real—since the existence of its individuals in the external world is impossible, and possibility of existence was considered in the real proposition as mentioned. And he answered that the aim is to capture the propositions used in the sciences most of the time, and what was mentioned is rarely used, so they did not pay attention to it, since they could not easily include it in the rules. And among them are those who made such propositions mental, saying: The meaning of your statement "Every"
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the classification of categorical propositions into 'outside' (kharijiyya) and 'real' (haqiqiyya). It begins by defining the outside proposition: it asserts that every (j) that exists in the external world is (b) in the external world. The judgment here applies to what actually exists externally, regardless of whether the subject's description with (j) holds at the time of judgment, before it, or after it. The commentator clarifies this to prevent a misunderstanding: some might think that the proposition requires the subject to be described with (j) at the very moment of the judgment (e.g., that 'every writer is laughing' requires the writer to be writing while laughing). The commentator rejects this by explaining that the judgment is on the essence of (j), not on its description. It suffices that the subject has the description (j) at some time, as in 'every sleeper is awake'—a person is not sleeping and awake at the same time, but the truth of the proposition depends on the person being a sleeper at one time and awake at another. The commentary then addresses an objection: there are propositions like 'every impossible is non-existent' that cannot be classified as either outside or real. An outside proposition requires actual existence of individuals, but the impossible has no individuals. A real proposition requires possibility of existence, but the impossible cannot exist. The author's response is that the classification focuses on commonly used propositions in the sciences; rare cases like this are ignored or handled separately. Some logicians treat such propositions as 'mental' (dhihniyya), where the judgment is about mental existence. This passage exemplifies the precision required in logical classification and shows how exceptions are managed within the tradition.

الحقيقية
الذهنية

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the proposition 'Every sleeper is awake' true even though no one is simultaneously sleeping and awake?
A) Because the world is in constant change.
B) Because the proposition is about mental existence only.
C) Because the judgment applies to the essence of the subject, not its description at the moment of judgment.
D) Because 'sleeper' and 'awake' are both true of the same person at different times.

Key Takeaway
Outside propositions require actual existence, real propositions require only possibility; in both, the subject's description need not coincide temporally with the predicate.

Propositions
intermediate
10 min
Handling Impossible Subjects: The Mental Proposition
Addressing propositions with impossible subjects and proposing mental propositions as a solution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify propositions whose subjects are impossible and explain the solution of mental (dhihniyya) propositions.

Core Idea
Propositions about impossible subjects (like 'Every impossible is non-existent') fit neither outside nor real; logicians created mental propositions for them.

Not all categorical propositions fit neatly into the outside/real classification. Some subjects, like 'impossible' or 'partner to Allah,' cannot exist at all. This lesson examines the problem posed by such propositions and the solution offered by logicians: treating them as 'mental' (dhihniyya), where the judgment is about mental existence rather than external reality.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the problem
Find the proposition 'Every impossible is non-existent' in the passage. Why does the author say it cannot be an outside proposition? Why can it not be a real proposition?
Outside requires actual existence; real requires possibility of existence. Impossible subjects have neither.
2
Understand the author's response
The author notes that the classification was designed for commonly used propositions; rare cases are ignored. Why might this response be considered a practical rather than a theoretical solution?
It sidesteps the problem by appealing to frequency.
3
Explore the proposed solution
Some logicians treat such propositions as 'mental' (dhihniyya). What does it mean for a proposition to be about mental existence? How does this allow the proposition to be true?
Mental existence means the subject exists in the mind; the predicate can then be affirmed or denied of that mental entity.
4
Apply with an Islamic example
Consider 'Every partner to Allah is non-existent.' Explain why it cannot be outside or real, and how it could be treated as a mental proposition. Is the proposition true?
The mental proposition saves to affirm the non-existence of a logically impossible entity.

Propositions
intermediate
10 min
Three Types of Propositions: Haqiqiyya, Kharijiyya, and Dhihniyya
Author classifies propositions into three types and addresses objections about impossible subjects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of propositions (haqiqiyya, kharijiyya, dhihniyya) and correctly classify any proposition according to its subject's mode of existence.

Core Idea
Propositions are classified by the existence mode of their subjects: essential, external, or mental.

In this passage, the author addresses an objection to the standard two-type classification of propositions (essential and external). Impossible subjects like 'partner of God' seem to fit neither. He resolves this by introducing a third category—mental propositions—and reframes the classification based on the three aspects of things: essential concomitants, external properties, and mental properties. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for evaluating the scope of judgments in scientific and logical discourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem with impossible subjects
The objection states that propositions like 'Every partner of God is impossible' cannot be classified as haqiqiyya (because the subject cannot even potentially exist) or kharijiyya (because no external individuals exist). This challenges the sufficiency of the two-type classification.
2
Understand the mental proposition solution
Some scholars reinterpret such propositions as dhihniyya (mental): the judgment applies only to mental individuals. For example, 'Every impossible is non-existent' means 'whatever is mentally conceived as impossible is mentally conceived as non-existent'.
3
Learn the threefold classification and examples
The three types: haqiqiyya (covers all actual and potential external individuals, e.g., geometric truths), kharijiyya (covers only actual external individuals, e.g., natural science statements), dhihniyya (covers only mental individuals, e.g., logical statements like 'every universal is predicated of many').
Notice that haqiqiyya includes potential individuals, while kharijiyya does not.
4
Apply the deeper ontological basis
The author's 'better view' grounds the classification in three aspects of things: lawazim al-mahiyyat (essential concomitants), properties specific to external existence, and properties specific to mental existence. Each corresponds to one proposition type, clarifying why the classification is exhaustive.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : هاهنا قضايا لا يمكن أخذها بأحد الاعتبارين، وهي التي موضوعاتها ممتنعة، كقولنا : شريك الباري ممتنع، وكل ممتنع فهو معدوم والفن يجب أن يكون قواعده عامة. لانا نقول : القوم لايزعمون انحصار جميع القضايا في الحقيقية و الخارجية، بل زعمهم أن القضايا المستعملة في العلوم مأخوذة في الأغلب بأحد الاعتبارين، فلهذا وضعوهما واستخرجوا أحكامهما لينتفعوا بذلك في العلوم ؛ وأما القضايا التي لا يمكن أخذها بأحد هذين الاعتبارين فمنهم من جعل أمثال هذه القضايا ذهنيّة، فقال : معنى قولك : «كل ممتنع معدوم» أن كل ما يصدق عليه في الذهن أنه ممتنع في الخارج يصدق عليه في الذهن أنه معدوم في الخارج، فجعل القضايا ثلاثة أقسام : حقيقية يتناول الحكم فيها جميع الأفراد الخارجية المحققة والمقدرة. وخارجية يتناول فيها الأفراد الخارجية المحققة فقط. وذهنيّة يتناول الأفراد الموجودة في الذهن فقط. فالأولى أن يقال : أحوال الأشياء على ثلاثة أقسام : قسم يتناول الأفراد الذهنية والخارجية المحققة والمقدرة - وهذا القسم يسمى لوازم الماهيات كالزوجية للأربعة والفردية للثلاثة وتساوى الزوايا الثلاث لقائمتين للمثلث - وقسم يختص بالموجود الخارجي كالحركة والسكون والإضاءة والإحراق، وقسم يختص بالموجود الذهني كالكلية والذاتية والجنسية وغيرها. فينبغي أن يعتبر ثلاث قضايا : إحداها أن يكون الحكم فيها على جميع أفراد الموضوع - ذهنياً كان أو خارجياً محققاً كان أو مقدّراً، كالقضايا الهندسية والحسابية - وتسمى هذه «حقيقية». وثانيتها أن يكون الحكم فيها مخصوصا بالأفراد الخارجية مطلقاً محققاً أو مقدراً، كالقضايا الطبيعية، وتسمى هذه «قضية خارجية». وثالثها أن يكون الحكم فيها مخصوصا بالأفراد الذهنية، وتسمى «قضية ذهنيّة كالقضايا المستعملة في المنطق.
One cannot say: There exist propositions that cannot be taken by either of the two considerations, namely those whose subjects are impossible, like our saying: 'The partner of God is impossible, and every impossible is non-existent' – and the art must have general rules. For we say: The scholars do not claim that all propositions are confined to the essential and the external, but rather their claim is that the propositions used in the sciences are mostly taken by one of the two considerations; therefore they established them and derived their rules to benefit from that in the sciences. As for propositions that cannot be taken by either of these two considerations, some of them have made such propositions mental, saying: The meaning of your saying 'Every impossible is non-existent' is that everything that is mentally conceived as impossible in the external world is mentally conceived as non-existent in the external world. Thus they made propositions into three types: Essential, where the judgment encompasses all external individuals, actual and potential; External, where it encompasses only actual external individuals; and Mental, where it encompasses only individuals existing in the mind. The better view is to say: The states of things are of three types: a type that encompasses mental and external individuals, actual and potential – this type is called the necessary concomitants of essences, like evenness for four, oddness for three, and the equality of the three angles of a triangle to two right angles; a type specific to external existence, like motion, stillness, illumination, and burning; and a type specific to mental existence, like universality, essentialness, genericity, and others. Thus three propositions should be considered: first, where the judgment applies to all individuals of the subject – mental or external, actual or potential – like geometrical and arithmetical propositions – these are called 'essential'. Second, where the judgment is specific to external individuals absolutely, actual or potential, like natural propositions – these are called 'external proposition'. Third, where the judgment is specific to mental individuals, called 'mental proposition', like the propositions used in logic.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is a commentary that expands on the classification of propositions into the well-known two types (essential and external) by addressing an objection and then introducing a third type (mental). The objection states that there are propositions whose subjects cannot be taken as either essential or external because their subjects are impossible – for example, 'Every partner of God is impossible' or 'Every impossible is non-existent'. Impossible subjects do not exist in the external world, so they cannot be external; and since they cannot even potentially exist, the essential type (which includes potential existents) also fails. The art of logic needs general rules, so such propositions must be accounted for. The author responds by noting that logicians do not claim all propositions fit into the two types; rather, most scientific propositions do. For exceptions, some scholars add a third category: mental propositions, where the judgment applies to mental individuals only. Thus they reinterpret 'Every impossible is non-existent' as: whatever is mentally conceived as impossible is mentally conceived as non-existent. This leads to a threefold division: essential (haqiqiyya) – covering all actual and potential external individuals; external (kharijiyya) – covering only actual external individuals; and mental (dhihniyya) – covering only mental individuals. The author then offers a more systematic classification based on the three aspects of things: (1) essential concomitants (lawazim al-mahiyyat) that apply to mental and external actual and potential individuals, like mathematical properties; (2) properties specific to external existence, like motion and heat; (3) properties specific to mental existence, like universality and categorical-ness. Accordingly, three types of propositions correspond: essential propositions (e.g., geometric and arithmetic truths), external propositions (e.g., natural science statements), and mental propositions (e.g., logical statements). The example of geometric propositions illustrates how essential propositions cover even potential cases: the property of triangles having angles summing to 180° holds whether or not the triangle actually exists. This expansion clarifies the scope of each proposition type and defends the logicians' practice.

خارجية
ذهنية

Key Takeaway
The classification of propositions depends on the mode of existence of their subjects: essential (all actual/potential), external (only actual), or mental (only mental).

Propositions
intermediate
10 min
The Three Types of Propositions: Real, External, and Mental
Author classifies propositions into real, external, and mental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real, external, and mental propositions and classify a given proposition into the correct type based on its scope.

Core Idea
Propositions are classified by the individuals they cover: real covers all possibilities, external covers actual existents, mental covers only mental concepts.

In the Islamic logical tradition, propositions are not all treated alike. Logicians distinguish three types based on whether the judgment applies to all possible individuals of the subject, only those existing externally, or only those in the mind. This classification is essential for understanding the scope of claims in different sciences like mathematics, physics, and logic itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
Find in the Arabic text the terms 'حقيقية', 'خارجية', and 'ذهنية'. Recognize that these are the three types of propositions discussed.
2
Determine the scope of each type
For each type, note what individuals are covered: Real (حقيقية) covers all actual and possible external individuals as well as mental individuals; External (خارجية) covers only actual external individuals; Mental (ذهنية) covers only individuals existing in the mind.
Real propositions do not require the subject to exist externally.
3
Match examples to types
The passage gives examples: geometrical and arithmetical propositions are real; natural science propositions are external; logical propositions are mental. Understand why each fits its type.
4
Apply the classification to new propositions
Practice by taking a proposition from an Islamic science and deciding its type based on whether the judgment depends on actual existence or not.
For example, 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' in fiqh is real because it covers all intoxicants, even those not yet existing.

لانا نقول : القوم لايزعمون انحصار جميع القضايا في الحقيقية و الخارجية، بل زعمهم أن القضايا المستعملة في العلوم مأخوذة في الأغلب بأحد الاعتبارين، فلهذا وضعوهما واستخرجوا أحكامهما لينتفعوا بذلك في العلوم ؛ وأما القضايا التي لا يمكن أخذها بأحد هذين الاعتبارين ممتنع معدوم» أن كل ما يصدق عليه في الذهن أنه ممتنع في الخارج يصدق عليه في الذهن أنه معدوم في الخارج»، فجعل القضايا ثلاثة أقسام : حقيقية يتناول الحكم فيها جميع الأفراد الخارجية المحققة والمقدرة. وخارجية يتناول فيها الأفراد الخارجية المحققة فقط. وذهنيّة يتناول الأفراد الموجودة في الذهن فقط. فالأولى أن يقال : أحوال الأشياء على ثلاثة أقسام : قسم يتناول الأفراد الذهنية والخارجية المحققة والمقدرة - وهذا القسم يسمى لوازم الماهيات كالزوجية للأربعة والفردية للثلاثة وتساوى الزوايا الثلاث لقائمتين للمثلث - وقسم يختص بالموجود الخارجي كالحركة والسكون والإضاءة والإحراق، وقسم يختص بالموجود الذهني كالكلية والذاتية والجنسية وغيرها. فينبغي أن يعتبر ثلاث قضايا : إحداها أن يكون الحكم فيها على جميع أفراد الموضوع - ذهنياً كان أو خارجياً محققاً كان أو مقدّراً، كالقضايا الهندسية والحسابية - وتسمى هذه «حقيقية». وثانيتها أن يكون الحكم فيها مخصوصا بالأفراد الخارجية مطلقاً محققاً أو مقدراً، كالقضايا الطبيعية، وتسمى هذه «قضية خارجية». وثالثها أن يكون الحكم فيها مخصوصا بالأفراد الذهنية، وتسمى «قضية ذهنيّة كالقضايا المستعملة في المنطق (شريف).
For we say: The [logicians] do not claim that all propositions are confined to the real and the external, rather their claim is that the propositions used in the sciences are taken mostly according to one of the two considerations, so they set them up and derived their rulings to benefit by that in the sciences. As for propositions that cannot be taken according to either of these two considerations, they are impossible and non-existent. [They said:] 'Every thing that is mentally judged to be impossible in external reality is mentally judged to be non-existent in external reality.' So they made propositions three divisions: Real: where the judgment includes all actual and possible external individuals. External: where it includes only actual external individuals. And Mental: where it includes only individuals existing in the mind. So it is better to say: The states of things are three divisions: a division that includes mental, actual external, and possible external individuals—and this division is called the necessary concomitants of essences, like evenness for four, oddness for three, and the equality of the three angles to two right angles for the triangle—and a division specific to external existents, like motion, rest, illumination, and burning—and a division specific to mental existents, like universality, essentiality, genericity, and others. So three propositions should be considered: First, that the judgment in them is upon all individuals of the subject—whether mental or external, actual or possible—like geometrical and arithmetical propositions—and these are called 'real'. Second, that the judgment in them is particular to external individuals absolutely, actual or possible—like natural propositions—and these are called 'external proposition'. Third, that the judgment in them is particular to mental individuals, and these are called 'mental proposition', like the propositions used in logic. (Sharif)
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the classification of propositions. The author begins by clarifying that logicians do not restrict all propositions to only two types (real and external); rather, in the sciences, propositions are usually taken according to one of these two perspectives. Then he presents the threefold classification: real (haqiqiyya) which covers all actual and possible external individuals; external (kharijiyya) covering only actual external individuals; and mental (dhihniyya) covering only mental individuals. The commentary then suggests a better formulation: the states of things are three kinds—those that are necessary concomitants of essences (such as mathematical properties), those specific to external existents (like physical properties), and those specific to mental existents (like logical properties). Correspondingly, three types of propositions are defined. Real propositions are used in mathematics and geometry, external in natural science, and mental in logic. The Sharif comment reinforces that mental propositions are those used in logic itself. The key point is that real propositions do not require the actual existence of the subject in external reality, while external propositions do.

ذهنية

The three types of propositions—real, external, and mental—are defined by the scope of individuals they cover, with real being the broadest and mental the narrowest.

Real vs External Propositions: Scope of Judgment
Author illustrates the difference between real and external propositions using examples of squares and shapes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a real (haqiqiyya) and an external (kharijiyya) proposition by analyzing the existence condition of the subject and the scope of the judgment.

Core Idea
Real propositions consider possible individuals; external propositions only actual ones.

In this passage, the author clarifies a crucial distinction in propositional logic: whether a proposition's truth depends on the actual existence of its subject. Using simple geometric examples, he shows how the same sentence can be true under one consideration and false under another. Understanding this difference is essential for correctly applying universal judgments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two proposition types
Note the definitions: a real proposition (haqiqiyya) does not require external existence of its subject; it covers both actual and possible individuals. An external proposition (kharijiyya) requires external existence and restricts judgment to actual individuals.
The key difference is the role of possibility.
2
Analyze the first example
Consider the sentence 'Every square is a shape' under the assumption that no squares exist externally. According to the real consideration, it is true because it covers possible squares. According to the external consideration, it is false because there are no actual squares.
The truth value flips based on the consideration.
3
Analyze the second example
Now assume that only squares exist externally (no other shapes). Then 'Every shape is a square' is true externally (since all actual shapes are squares) but false really (because possible non-square shapes are not excluded).
This shows the real proposition is more inclusive.
4
Generalize the rule
When the subject does not exist externally, only the real proposition can be true. When the subject does exist, both can be true but under different conditions: the external restricts to actual individuals, the real includes possible ones.
This distinction is critical for applying universal rules in fiqh and kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلم يعرف بعد أحكامها، وتعميم القواعد إنما هو بقدر الطاقة الإنسانية.
[٤٨ - النسبة بين القضية الحقيقية والخارجية]
قال: والفرق بين الاعتبارين ،ظاهر، فإنّه لولم يوجد شيء من المربعات في الخارج يصح أن يقال: كل مربع شكل بالاعتبار الأول دون الثاني، ولو لم يوجد شيء من الأشكال في الخارج إلا المربع يصح أن يقال: «كل شكل مربع بالاعتبار الثاني - دون الأول.
أقول: قد ظهر لك تمابيناه أن الحقيقية لا تستدعي وجود الموضوع في الخارج، بل يجوز أن يكون موجوداً في الخارج وأن لا يكون، وإذا كان موجوداً في الخارج فالحكم فيها لا يكون مقصوراً على الأفراد الخارجية، بل يتناولها والأفراد المقدّرة الوجود؛ بخلاف الخارجية فإنها تستدعي وجود الموضوع في الخارج، فالحكم فيها مقصور على الأفراد الخارجية، فالموضوع إن لم يكن موجوداً فقد تصدق القضية باعتبار الحقيقة دون الخارج، كما إذالم يكن شيء من المربعات موجوداً في الخارج، يصدق بحسب الحقيقة كل مربع شكل» - أي «كل مالو وجد كان مربعاً فهو بحيث لووجد كان شكلاً ولا يصدق بحسب الخارج، لعدم وجود المربع في الخارج على ما هو المفروض.
وإن كان الموضوع موجوداً لا يخلو : إما أن يكون الحكم مقصوراً على الأفراد الخارجية، أو متناولاً لها وللأفراد المقدّرة؛ فإن كان مقصوراً على الأفراد الخارجيّة تصدق الكلّية الخارجية - دون الكلية الحقيقية - كما إذا انحصر الأشكال في الخارج في المربع، فيصدق «كل شكل مربع»
So their rulings are not yet known, and generalization of the rules is only to the extent of human capacity. [48 - The relationship between the real and external proposition] He said: The difference between the two considerations is clear. For if none of the squares exist in external reality, it is correct to say: 'Every square is a shape' according to the first consideration but not the second. And if nothing of the shapes exists in external reality except the square, it is correct to say: 'Every shape is a square' according to the second consideration but not the first. I say: It has become clear to you from what we explained that the real proposition does not require the existence of the subject in external reality; rather, it may exist externally or not. And if it exists externally, the judgment in it is not restricted to external individuals but includes them and the possible individuals. In contrast, the external proposition requires the existence of the subject in external reality, so the judgment is restricted to external individuals. Thus, if the subject does not exist, the proposition may be true according to the real consideration but not the external, as when none of the squares exist externally, it is true according to reality: 'Every square is a shape'—i.e., 'Every thing that if existed would be a square is such that if it existed it would be a shape'—and it is not true externally due to the non-existence of the square externally, as assumed. And if the subject exists, it is not free: either the judgment is restricted to external individuals, or it includes them and the possible individuals. If it is restricted to external individuals, the universal external proposition is true—but not the universal real proposition—as when shapes are confined externally to the square, then 'Every shape is a square' is true.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the difference between real (haqiqiyya) and external (kharijiyya) propositions. The author begins by noting that the rulings of mental propositions are not yet known and that generalization is limited. Then the matn states that the difference is clear with an example: if no squares exist externally, the real proposition 'Every square is a shape' is true (since it considers possibility), but the external proposition is false because there are no individuals. Conversely, if only squares exist externally, the external proposition 'Every shape is a square' is true (since all existing shapes are squares), but the real proposition is false because it would also consider possible shapes that are not squares. The commentary expands: the real proposition does not require external existence; it includes both actual and possible individuals. The external proposition requires external existence and restricts the judgment to actual individuals. The commentary then explains the two cases: non-existence of the subject and existence of the subject, showing when each type of universal is true.

Real vs. External Universal Propositions
Author explains the difference with examples of squares and shapes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish haqiqiyya from kharijiyya propositions and determine which type applies in a given existential scenario.

Core Idea
Real propositions need no external existence; external propositions are confined to what actually exists.

In Islamic logic, universal propositions carry different existential commitments. The haqiqiyya proposition is true even if its subject has no actual instances, while the kharijiyya proposition requires actual existence. This distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting universal claims in fiqh and kalam, where rulings often depend on whether the subject must exist or merely be conceivable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Considerations
Notice the author contrasts two 'considerations' (iʿtibārān): the real (haqiqī) and the external (khārijī). The real focuses on the essence regardless of existence, while the external focuses on actual existents.
The terms are introduced at the beginning: 'al-iʿtibār al-awwal' = real, 'al-iʿtibār al-thānī' = external.
2
Test Non-Existence Scenario
If no squares exist externally, 'Every square is a shape' is true as a real proposition but false as an external one. Understand why: the real proposition is conditional ('if existed, would be a shape'), while the external requires actual squares.
This shows that real propositions do not require subject existence.
3
Test Existence with Confined Judgment
If all existing shapes are squares, 'Every shape is a square' is true externally but false really. The real proposition is false because one can conceive a shape that is not a square (e.g., triangle). The external is confined to actual individuals.
This illustrates when the judgment is restricted to external individuals only.
4
Test Existence with Inclusive Judgment
Using 'Every human is an animal', both real and external are true because humans actually exist and the judgment applies to all possible humans as well. This shows the real proposition includes supposed individuals.
When both types are true, the real proposition is broader in scope.
5
Determine the Relationship
The author concludes that the two types have a relationship of 'particularity and generality from one aspect' (ʿumūm wa khuṣūṣ min wajh). This means they overlap in some cases (both true) and differ in others (one true, one false).
This relationship is key for evaluating universal propositions in Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: والفرق بين الاعتبارين ظاهر، فإنه لو لم يوجد شيء من المربعات في الخارج يصح أن يقال: كل مربع شكل بالاعتبار الأول دون الثاني، ولو لم يوجد شيء من الأشكال في الخارج إلا المربع يصح أن يقال: «كل شكل مربع بالاعتبار الثاني دون الأول. أقول: قد ظهر لك تمابيناه أن الحقيقية لا تستدعي وجود الموضوع في الخارج، بل يجوز أن يكون موجوداً في الخارج وأن لا يكون، وإذا كان موجوداً في الخارج فالحكم فيها لا يكون مقصوراً على الأفراد الخارجية، بل يتناولها والأفراد المقدّرة الوجود؛ بخلاف الخارجية فإنها تستدعي وجود الموضوع في الخارج، فالحكم فيها مقصور على الأفراد الخارجية، فالموضوع إن لم يكن موجوداً فقد تصدق القضية باعتبار الحقيقة دون الخارج، كما إذالم يكن شيء من المربعات موجوداً في الخارج، يصدق بحسب الحقيقة كل مربع شكل» - أي «كل مالو وجد كان مربعاً فهو بحيث لووجد كان شكلاً ولا يصدق بحسب الخارج، لعدم وجود المربع في الخارج على ما هو المفروض. وإن كان الموضوع موجوداً لا يخلو : إما أن يكون الحكم مقصوراً على الأفراد الخارجية، أو متناولاً لها وللأفراد المقدّرة؛ فإن كان مقصوراً على الأفراد الخارجيّة تصدق الكلّية الخارجية - دون الكلية الحقيقية - كما إذا انحصر الأشكال في الخارج في المربع، فيصدق «كل شكل مربع» بحسب الخارج - وهو ظاهر - ولا يصدق بحسب الحقيقة - أي لا يصدق «كل مالو وجد كان شكلا فهو بحيث لو وجد كان مربعاً» لصدق قولنا : بعض مالو وجد كان شكلاً فهو بحيث لو وجد كان ليس بمربع». وإن كان الحكم متناولاً لجميع الأفراد المحققة والمقدرة، فتصدق الكليتان معاً ، كقولنا : «كلّ إنسان حيوان»، فإذن يكون بينهما خصوص وعموم من وجه
He said: The difference between the two considerations is apparent, because if nothing of the squares existed externally, it would be correct to say 'Every square is a shape' according to the first consideration and not the second. And if nothing of the shapes existed externally except the square, it would be correct to say 'Every shape is a square' according to the second consideration and not the first. I say: It has become clear to you from what we explained that the real [proposition] does not require the existence of the subject externally, but it is permissible that it exists externally or not. And if it exists externally, the judgment in it is not confined to external individuals, but encompasses them and the supposed individuals; unlike the external [proposition], for it requires the existence of the subject externally, so the judgment in it is confined to external individuals. Thus, if the subject does not exist, the proposition may be true according to the real consideration and not the external, as when none of the squares exist externally: 'Every square is a shape' is true according to reality—i.e., 'Whatever were to exist as a square, it would be such that were it to exist it would be a shape'—and it is not true according to the external, due to the non-existence of the square externally according to the assumed situation. And if the subject exists, it is not free (from two cases): either the judgment is confined to the external individuals, or it encompasses them and the supposed individuals. If it is confined to external individuals, the external universal is true—not the real universal—as when the shapes externally are limited to the square, so 'Every shape is a square' is true externally—that is apparent—and it is not true according to reality—i.e., 'Whatever were to exist as a shape, it would be such that were it to exist it would be a square' is not true because of the truth of our statement: 'Some of that which were to exist as a shape is such that were it to exist it would not be a square.' And if the judgment encompasses all actual and supposed individuals, then both universals are true together, as in our statement: 'Every human is an animal.' Thus there is a relationship of particularity and generality from one aspect between them.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage defines and contrasts the two types of universal propositions: haqiqiyya (real) and kharijiyya (external). The author begins by giving two scenarios to illustrate the difference. The first consideration is the real (haqiqiyya) and the second is the external (kharijiyya). The commentary then explains that a real proposition does not require actual existence of its subject in the external world; it can be true even if no individual exists, as long as the conditional relation holds. An external proposition requires actual existence. The author then breaks down the possible cases when the subject exists: either the judgment is restricted to external individuals (then only the external universal is true) or it includes both external and supposed individuals (then both universals are true). The example of squares and shapes is used to demonstrate the first case where the external universal 'Every shape is a square' is true but the real universal is false because one can conceive of a shape that is not a square. The example 'Every human is an animal' shows both universals true because humans actually exist and the judgment applies to all possible humans. The passage concludes that the two types have a relationship of 'particularity and generality from one aspect' (عموم وخصوص من وجه), meaning they overlap in some cases and differ in others.

Key Terms
القضية الحقيقية
القضية الخارجية

Key Takeaway
Real universals are conditional on existence, while external universals are existential.
