Two Senses of Truth in Logic: Predication vs. Fact
Author extends analysis to remaining quantified propositions and distinguishes two senses of truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between truth as predication (sidq hamli) and truth as fact (sidq wuqu'i) in Islamic logic, and apply the correct sense when analyzing quantified propositions.

Core Idea
Truth in logic is not one thing: it means predication for terms, but correspondence for propositions.

In the previous lesson, you learned about the universal affirmative proposition. Now the author asks you to extend that analysis to the other three quantified propositions. But there is a critical twist: the concept of 'truth' (sidq) that governs generality and particularity among simple terms is different from the truth that governs propositions. This lesson will clarify that distinction, which is essential for correctly understanding relations among propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two contexts for truth
The passage distinguishes truth when applied to simple terms vs. when applied to full propositions. Look for the prepositions: 'ala (upon) for predication, fi (in) for factual truth.
Prepositions are key: 'truth upon' = predication; 'truth in' = fact.
2
Recognize that propositions are not predicated
The author states that a proposition like 'Zaid is standing' cannot be predicated of anything. Why? Because a proposition is a complete statement, not a term.
3
Understand the consequence for generality and particularity
Since propositions cannot be predicated, generality and particularity among propositions must be based on their truth conditions (actual realization), not on predication.
4
Apply the distinction to equivalent propositions
Equivalent propositions are those where the truth of each in itself necessitates the truth of the other. This is a truth-conditional relation, not a predication relation.
The footnote reinforces this: truth in the sense of actualization uses 'fi'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: على هذا فقس المحصورات الباقية. القول: لما عرفت مفهوم الموجبة الكلية أمكنك أن تعرف مفهوم باقي المحصورات بالقياس عليه، فإن الحكم في الموجبة الجزئية على بعض ما عليه الحكم في الموجبة الكلية، فالأمور المعتبرة قمة بحسب الكل معتبرة (1) العموم والخصوص في المفردات وما في حكمها من المركبات التقييدية إنما هو بحسب الصدق - أعني الحمل على الشيء كمامر - وأما في القضايا : فلا يتصور صدقها بمعنى حملها على شيء، لأن القضية - كقولنا «زيد قائم - لا يحمل على شيء مفرد ولا على قضيّة أخرى، فالعموم والخصوص وسائر النسب المذكورة فيما سبق إنما يعتبر في القضايا بحسب صدقها – أي تحققها في الواقع فالقضيتان المساويتان هما اللتان يكون صدق كل واحدة منهما في نفس الأمر مستلزماً لصدق الأخرى فيها، وكذا القياس في سائر النسب؛ والصدق بمعنى الحمل يستعمل بـ«على»، فيقال : الكاتب صادق على الإنسان» - أي محمول عليه - والصدق بمعنى التحقق والوجود يستعمل بـ«في»، فيقال : صدقت هذه القضية في الواقع» (شريف).
He said: Based on this, analogize the remaining quantified propositions. The statement: Since you have understood the concept of the universal affirmative, you can understand the concept of the remaining quantified propositions by analogy with it. For the judgment in the particular affirmative is a part of what the judgment is in the universal affirmative, so the matters considered with respect to the whole are considered (1) Generality and particularity among simple terms and governed compound expressions are according to truth—meaning predication as before—but in propositions, their truth is not conceived in the sense of predicating them of something, because a proposition—like our saying 'Zaid is standing'—is not predicated of any simple thing nor of another proposition. Thus generality and particularity and the other relations mentioned previously are considered in propositions according to their truth—i.e., their actual realization. So the two equivalent propositions are those for which the truth of each in itself necessitates the truth of the other; and likewise the analogy for the other relations. And truth in the sense of predication is used with 'upon', so it is said: 'The writer is true upon the human'—i.e., predicated of it. And truth in the sense of actualization and existence is used with 'in', so it is said: 'This proposition is true in reality' (Sharif).
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage continues the lesson by instructing the student to analogize the other quantified propositions (particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative) from the universal affirmative. The author then adds a critical note about the term 'truth' (صدق). In logic, when dealing with simple terms (like 'human') or compound restrictive expressions, generality and particularity are based on truth as predication—i.e., one term is predicated of another. However, in propositions (like 'Zaid is standing'), truth cannot be predication because a proposition is not predicated of anything. Instead, truth here means actual correspondence with reality. The footnote from Sharif clarifies that two propositions are equivalent if the truth of one necessarily entails the truth of the other in actual fact. The same analogy applies to other relations like contrariety, subcontrariety, etc. The distinction between two meanings of truth: truth as predication (using the preposition 'ala) and truth as fact (using the preposition fi), is emphasized to avoid confusion. This passage guards against conflating predication of terms with truth of propositions.

Key Takeaway
Truth in logic has two meanings: predication for terms and correspondence for propositions; you must use the correct sense when analyzing quantified propositions.

Distinguishing Real and External Quantifiers in Categorical Propositions
Author explains the logical relationship between real and external affirmative and negative propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a categorical proposition is interpreted as real or external and determine the logical relationships between them.

Core Idea
Real quantifiers include possible individuals; external quantifiers only existing ones.

In Islamic logic, propositions can be interpreted in two ways: real (haqiqi) concerning all possible individuals, and external (khariji) concerning only existing ones. This distinction affects how we compare universal and particular propositions. In this lesson, we will explore the logical relationships between real and external affirmatives and negatives, using a key principle: the negation of the more specific is more general than the negation of the more general.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations
Notice that the author distinguishes between real (haqiqi) and external (khariji) interpretations for both affirmative and negative propositions. The real interpretation considers all possible individuals (including non-existent), while the external considers only those that actually exist.
2
Recognize the relation between real and external particular affirmatives
The author states that the real particular affirmative (some possible individuals) is absolutely more general than the external particular affirmative (some existing individuals). This is because every external individual is also a real individual, but not vice versa.
3
Apply the principle of negation to universal negatives
Using the principle 'the negation of the more specific is more general than the negation of the more general', we deduce that the external universal negative (No existing individual is P) is more general than the real universal negative (No possible individual is P). Why? Because the external particular (more specific) has a more general negation.
4
Analyze the relation between particular negatives
The author says between the two particular negatives there is partial opposition (mubāyana juz'iyya). This means they overlap but neither is wholly more general. Try to construct an example to see why.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هاهنا بحسب البعض، ومعنى السالبة الكلية رفع الإيجاب عن كل واحد واحد، والسالبة الجزئية رفع الإيجاب عن بعض الأحاد، فكما اعتبرت الموجبة الكلية بحسب الحقيقة والخارج، كذلك تعتبر المحصورات الأخر بالاعتبارين. وقد تقدم الفرق بين الكليتين، وأما الفرق بين الجزئيتين : فهو أن الجزئية الحقيقيّة أعم مطلقاً من الخارجيّة، لأنّ الإيجاب على بعض أفراد الخارجية إيجاب على بعض أفراد الحقيقية مطلقاً - بدون العكس -. وعلى هذا تكون السالبة الكلّيّة الخارجية أعم من السالبة الكلية الحقيقية، لأن نقيض الأخص أعم من نقيض الأعم مطلقاً، وبين السالبتين الجزئيتين مباينة جزئية، وذلك ظاهر.
Here according to some, and the meaning of the universal negative is the removal of affirmation from each individual, and the particular negative is the removal of affirmation from some individuals. Just as the universal affirmative was considered according to reality and externality, likewise the other quantified propositions are considered according to the two considerations. The difference between the two universals has preceded. As for the difference between the two particulars: it is that the real particular is absolutely more general than the external particular, because affirmation of some individuals of the external is affirmation of some individuals of the real absolutely, without the converse. And accordingly, the external universal negative is more general than the real universal negative, because the negation of the more specific is more general than the negation of the more general universally. And between the two particular negatives there is partial opposition, and that is clear.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage defines and compares the logical relations between quantified propositions (affirmative and negative, universal and particular) under two different interpretations: the 'real' (ḥaqīqī) and the 'external' (khārijī). The author begins by stating that the universal negative means the removal of affirmation from every single individual, and the particular negative means removal from some individuals. Just as the universal affirmative was considered in both the real and external senses, so too are the other quantified propositions considered in both senses. The difference between the two types of universals has already been discussed. Now the author explains the difference between the two types of particulars: the real particular is absolutely more general than the external particular, because affirming something of some external individuals necessarily entails affirming it of some real individuals, but not vice versa. Then he deduces that the external universal negative is more general than the real universal negative, using the principle that the negation of the more specific is more general than the negation of the more general. Finally, he states that between the two particular negatives (real and external) there is partial opposition (mubāyana juzʾiyya), meaning they intersect but neither is wholly more general. The explanation relies on the prior understanding of the relations between universal propositions. An example: 'Every human is mortal' in the real sense means all possible humans; in the external sense it means all existing humans. The real universal is more general. The negation of that universal—'Some human is not mortal'—shows the relation.

Classifying Propositions: Maʿdūla vs. Simple
Author classifies propositions into maʿdūla and muḥaṣṣala/basīṭa based on negation particle inclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any categorical proposition as maʿdūla, muḥaṣṣala, or basīṭa based on the position of the negation particle.

Core Idea
The position of the negation particle determines whether a proposition is maʿdūla or simple.

In logical analysis, it is crucial to distinguish between propositions where the negation is part of the term and those where it negates the copula. This distinction affects how we evaluate truth conditions. In this lesson, we learn the classification of propositions into maʿdūla (with internal negation) and muḥaṣṣala/basīṭa (without).

Guided Path
1
Identify the negation particle
Find the word 'laysa', 'ghayr', 'lā', or any other particle of negation (ḥarf al-salb) in the proposition.
Negation particles include 'lā', 'laysa', 'ghayr', 'mā', etc.
2
Determine if the negation is part of a term
Check if the negation particle is attached to or compounds with the subject or the predicate, or both, forming a single term (e.g., 'lā-ḥayy' meaning 'not-living').
If the negation is separate and negates the predicate as a whole (e.g., 'Zayd laysa kātiban'), it is not part of the term.
3
Classify the proposition
If the negation is part of a term, the proposition is maʿdūla (further specifying subject, predicate, or both). If the negation is not part of either term, classify as muḥaṣṣala if affirmative, or basīṭa if negative.
Muḥaṣṣala is affirmative without internal negation; basīṭa is negative without internal negation.
4
Practice with Islamic examples
Apply the rule to propositions from Islamic sciences, such as 'The not-believer is a disbeliever' (maʿdūla) and 'The believer is not a disbeliever' (basīṭa).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: البحث الثالث في العدول والتحصيل. حرف السلب إن كان جزء من الموضوع - كقولنا: «اللاحي جماد» أو من المحمول - كقولنا: «الجماد لاعالم» أو منهما جميعاً، سميت القضية «معدولة» موجبة كانت أو سالبة - وإن لم يكن جزء لشيء منهما سميت «محصلة» إن كانت موجبة و «بسيطة» إن كانت سالبة. القول: القضية أما معدولة أو محصلة: لأن حرف السلب إما أن يكون جزء لشيء من الموضوع والمحمول أو لا يكون، فإن كان جزء - إما من الموضوع كقولنا: «اللاحي جماد» أو من المحمول كقولنا: «الجماد لاعالم» أو منهما جميعاً كقولنا «اللاحي لاعالم» سميت القضية «معدولة» موجبة كانت أو سالبة. أما الأولى: فمعدولة الموضوع. وأما الثانية: فمعدولة المحمول. وأما الثالثة: فمعدولة الطرفين. وإنما سميت «معدولة» لأنّ حروف السلب - كـ«ليس» و «غير» و «لا»- إنما وضعت في الأصل للسلب والرفع، فإذا جعل مع غيره كشيء
He said: The third investigation is on 'negative subject/predicate' (al-ʿudūl) and 'simple' (al-taḥṣīl). If the particle of negation is part of the subject—as in our saying: 'The not-living is inanimate'—or part of the predicate—as in 'The inanimate is not-knowing'—or part of both, the proposition is called 'maʿdūla', whether it is affirmative or negative. And if it is not part of either, it is called 'muḥaṣṣala' if affirmative and 'basīṭa' if negative. The statement: The proposition is either maʿdūla or muḥaṣṣala, because the particle of negation is either part of something from the subject and predicate or not. If it is part—either from the subject as in 'The not-living is inanimate' or from the predicate as in 'The inanimate is not-knowing' or from both as in 'The not-living is not-knowing'—the proposition is called 'maʿdūla', whether affirmative or negative. The first is maʿdūlat al-mawḍūʿ, the second is maʿdūlat al-maḥmūl, and the third is maʿdūlat al-ṭarafayn. And it is called 'maʿdūla' because the particles of negation—like 'laysa', 'ghayr', and 'lā'—were originally placed for negation and removal, so when they are made part of something else, it is as if something...
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is a definitional passage from the third investigation of the text, dealing with 'al-ʿudūl wa al-taḥṣīl' (negative subject/predicate and simple propositions). The author defines a proposition as 'maʿdūla' (literally 'turned aside') if the negation particle is part of the subject or the predicate or both. He gives three examples: (1) subject negation: 'The not-living is inanimate' where 'lā' is attached to 'ḥayy' (living) to form 'lā-ḥayy' (not-living); (2) predicate negation: 'The inanimate is not-knowing' where 'lā' is attached to 'ʿālim' (knowing); (3) both: 'The not-living is not-knowing'. If the negation particle is not part of either, then the proposition is called 'muḥaṣṣala' if affirmative, and 'basīṭa' if negative. For example, 'Zayd is writing' is muḥaṣṣala, while 'Zayd is not writing' is basīṭa (because the negation is external to the predicate). The author explains the rationale for the name 'maʿdūla': originally, words like 'laysa', 'ghayr', and 'lā' are used for negation, but when they are combined with another word to form a single term, the proposition is 'turned aside' from its simple form. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the core definitions are complete.

Modified and Simple Propositions: The Role of the Negation Particle
Author defines modified and simple propositions based on negation particle status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a categorical proposition is modified or simple by analysing the position of the negation particle in its subject and predicate terms.

Core Idea
A modified proposition embeds negation into its terms; a simple proposition keeps the negation separate.

In this lesson, we classify categorical propositions based on the status of the negation particle. Is it part of the subject or predicate term, or is it an external operator? This distinction may seem technical, but it affects how we understand the logical form of statements, especially in juristic and theological reasoning where terms like 'non-prohibited' or 'not-caused' appear.

Guided Path
1
Identify the negation particle
Look for words like لا, ليس, غير in the proposition. These are the primary negation particles in Arabic.
The particle may appear as part of a compound term or as a separate operator.
2
Determine if the particle is part of a term
Check whether the negation particle is attached to the subject or predicate to form a single term (e.g., لا-حي, لا-عالم). If it is, the term is 'modified' (معدول).
If the particle is separate (e.g., زيد ليس بكاتب), it is not part of the term.
3
Classify as modified or simple
If the negation particle is part of the subject or predicate (or both), the proposition is modified. Otherwise, it is simple.
This classification is independent of the proposition's quality (affirmative/negative).
4
Subclassify modified propositions
Determine whether the modification is in the subject only, predicate only, or both. This gives three subtypes: modified subject, modified predicate, modified in both.
Examples: 'اللاحي جماد' (subject modified), 'الجماد لاعالم' (predicate modified), 'اللاحي لاعالم' (both modified).
5
Distinguish quality from term modification
The quality of a modified proposition is still determined by the copula (positive or negative), not by the embedded negation in the terms.
A negative copula combined with a modified term can be tricky; but the definition of modified does not change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثالث في العدول والتحصيل. القول : القضية أما معدولة أو محصلة : لأن حرف السلب إما أن يكون جزء لشيء من الموضوع والمحمول أو لا يكون، فإن كان جزء - إما من الموضوع كقولنا : «اللاحي جماد» أو من المحمول كقولنا : الجماد لاعالم أو منهما جميعاً كقولنا «اللاحي لاعالم» سميت القضية «معدولة» موجبة كانت أو سالبة. أما الأولى : فمعدولة الموضوع. وأما الثانية : فمعدولة المحمول. وأما الثالثة : فمعدولة الطرفين. وإنما سميت معدولة» لأنّ حروف السلب - كـ«ليس» و «غیر» و «لا»- إنما وضعت في الأصل للسلب والرفع، فإذا جعل مع غيره كشيء واحد يثبت له شيء أو هو لشيء آخر، أو يسلب عنه أو هو عن شيء آخر : فقد عدل به عن موضوعه الأصلي إلى غيره. وإنما أورد للأولى والثانية مثالاً دون الثالثة : لأنه قد علم من المثال الأول الموضوع المعدول، ومن المثال الثاني المحمول المعدول، فقد علم مثال معدولة الطرفين بجمعهما معا. وإن لم يكن حرف السلب جزء لشيء من الموضوع والمحمول سميت القضية «محصلة» - سواء كانت موجبة أو سالبة - كقولنا : «زيد كاتب» و «زيد ليس بكاتب». ووجه التسمية أن حرف السلب إذا لم يكن جزء من طرفيها فكل واحد من الطرفين وجودي محصل ؛ وربما يخصص اسم المحصلة بالموجبة، و تسمى السالبة بسيطة»، لأنّ البسيط مالاجزء له، وحرف السلب وإن كان موجوداً فيها إلا أنه ليس جزء من طرفيها. وإنما لم يذكر لهما مثالاً لأنّ جميع الأمثلة المذكورة في المباحث السابقة تصلح أن تكون مثالاً لهما.
The third investigation: On modification and simplicity. The statement: The proposition is either modified or simple. Because the negation particle is either part of something from the subject and predicate or not. If it is part — either from the subject, as in our saying 'the not-living is inanimate', or from the predicate, as in our saying 'the inanimate is not-knowing', or from both together, as in our saying 'the not-living is not-knowing' — the proposition is called 'modified', whether it is affirmative or negative. As for the first: it is modified in subject. As for the second: it is modified in predicate. As for the third: it is modified in both terms. And it is called 'modified' because the negation particles — like 'laysa', 'ghayr', and 'la' — were originally placed for negation and removal. So when they are made with something else as a single thing to which something is affirmed or which is for something else, or to which something is negated or which is from something else, then they have been diverted from their original function to something else. And he only gave examples for the first and second without the third because from the first example the modified subject is known, and from the second example the modified predicate is known, so the example of the modified in both terms is known by combining them. And if the negation particle is not part of something from the subject and predicate, the proposition is called 'simple' — whether affirmative or negative — as in our saying 'Zayd is a writer' and 'Zayd is not a writer'. And the reason for the name is that if the negation particle is not part of its terms, then each of the two terms is a positive obtained thing. And sometimes the name 'simple' is specified for the affirmative, and the negative is called 'absolute' because the absolute is that which has no part, and the negation particle, although present in it, is not part of its terms. And he did not give examples for them because all the examples mentioned in the previous investigations suffice as examples for them.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage defines the classification of categorical propositions into modified (mudawwala) and simple (muhassala). The author begins by stating the division: a proposition is either modified or simple. The criterion is whether the negation particle (such as 'laysa', 'ghayr', 'la') is a part of the subject term, the predicate term, or both. If it is, the proposition is called 'modified' regardless of its quality (affirmative or negative). Three subcases are given: (1) modified subject, e.g., 'al-la-hayyu jamad' (the not-living is inanimate) where 'la' is part of the subject; (2) modified predicate, e.g., 'al-jamadu la-'alim' (the inanimate is not-knowing) where 'la' is part of the predicate; (3) both terms modified, e.g., 'al-la-hayyu la-'alim' (the not-living is not-knowing). The author explains the etymology: these particles are originally used for negation, but when incorporated into a term, they are 'diverted' (udila) from their original function. He notes that examples for the third case are not given separately because combining the first two suffices. If the negation particle is not part of either term, the proposition is 'simple' (muhassala), whether affirmative or negative, e.g., 'Zayd is a writer' (affirmative) and 'Zayd is not a writer' (negative). The term 'muhassala' is sometimes reserved for the affirmative, while the negative is called 'basita' (absolute) because the negation particle, though present in the sentence, is not a constituent of the terms. The author concludes that examples for simple propositions are abundant in previous sections.

Propositions

5 min
Criterion for Affirmation and Negation in Propositions
Author explains that affirmation/negation depends on the relation, not on the nature of the terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a proposition is affirmative or negative based on the relation between its terms, regardless of whether the terms themselves contain negation.

Core Idea
Affirmation and negation depend on the copula, not the terms.

In logic, the quality of a proposition (affirmative or negative) is not always obvious from the words used. A proposition like 'Everything that is not living is not knowing' looks negative because both terms contain 'not', but it is actually affirmative. This lesson explains the correct criterion: look at the relation (nisba) between subject and predicate, not at the terms themselves. The source passage from Qutbi's commentary clarifies this crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate
Find the subject term (mawdu') and the predicate term (mahmul) in the proposition. For example, in 'كل ما ليس بحي فهو لا عالم', the subject is 'ما ليس بحي' (what is not living) and the predicate is 'لا عالم' (not knowing).
2
Determine the type of relation (nisba)
Ask: Does the proposition affirm the predicate of the subject, or deny it? In the example, the predicate 'لا عالم' is affirmed of the subject 'ما ليس بحي' – the proposition says that whatever is not living is not knowing. This is an affirmative relation (nisba thubutiyya).
Ignore any negation inside the terms; focus on the copula.
3
Classify the proposition accordingly
If the relation is affirmative, the proposition is affirmative (ijab) even if terms are privative. If the relation is negative, the proposition is negative (salb) even if terms are positive. Confirm with the second example: 'لا شيء من المتحرك بساكن' denies the predicate 'ساكن' of the subject 'متحرك', so it is negative despite both terms being positive.
This method prevents confusion in analyzing modified propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: والاعتبار بايجاب القضية وسلبها بالنسبة الثبوتية أو السلبية - لا بطرفي القضية - فإن قولنا: كلّ ماليس بحيّ فهو لا عالم» موجبة مع أن طرفيها عدميان، وقولنا: لاشيء من المتحرك بساكن سالبة مع أن طرفيها وجوديان.
He said: The consideration for the affirmation and negation of a proposition is the affirmative or negative relation — not the two terms of the proposition. For our saying 'Everything that is not living is not knowing' is affirmative even though its two terms are privative, and our saying 'Nothing of that which moves is at rest' is negative even though its two terms are positive.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage establishes the criterion for determining whether a proposition is affirmative (ijab) or negative (salb). The author states that the judgment is based on the relation (nisba) between the subject and predicate—whether it is affirmative (thubutiyya) or negative (salbiyya)—and not on the nature of the terms themselves (tarafay al-qadiyya). Two examples illustrate the principle. First: 'Everything that is not living is not knowing' (كل ما ليس بحي فهو لا عالم) is an affirmative proposition even though both the subject ('not living') and predicate ('not knowing') are privative (i.e., contain negation). This is because the relation between them is affirmative: the predicate is affirmed of the subject. Second: 'Nothing of that which moves is at rest' (لا شيء من المتحرك بساكن) is a negative proposition even though both terms ('that which moves' and 'at rest') are positive (existential). This is because the relation is negative: motion is denied of rest. The point is that the quality of a proposition is determined by the copula, not by the presence or absence of negation in the terms. This distinction prevents confusion when analyzing modified propositions.

Key Terms
اعتبار
نسبة ثبوتية
نسبة سلبية
طرفا القضية

C) 'Everything that is not living is not knowing' is affirmative.
D) 'Nothing that moves is at rest' is affirmative.

Key Takeaway
The quality of a proposition is determined by the affirmative or negative relation between subject and predicate, not by the presence of negation in the terms themselves.

Mu'addalah Propositions: Negation in the Term
Definition of mu'addalah proposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a mu'addalah proposition and distinguish it from muhassalah.

Core Idea
When negation becomes part of a term, the proposition is diverted from its original subject.

In this passage, the author defines mu'addalah (diverted) propositions. He explains how the presence of the negation particle 'la' as a part of the subject or predicate diverts the proposition from its original meaning. Understanding this helps in correctly classifying propositions in logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Locate the negation particle
Find the word 'la' (لا) or 'laysa' (ليس) in the proposition. Is it attached to a term like 'la insan' (لا إنسان) or is it a separate predicate negator?
If the negation is a separate word negating the whole predicate, it is not part of the term.
2
Check if negation is part of subject or predicate
If the negation is part of the subject (e.g., 'لا إنسان كاتب' = a non-human is a writer) or part of the predicate (e.g., 'زيد لا كاتب' = Zayd is a non-writer), then it is mu'addalah.
If negation is separate (e.g., 'زيد ليس بكاتب'), it is not mu'addalah.
3
Identify which term is diverted
Determine whether the negation modifies the subject (first type), the predicate (second type), or both (third type). The passage says examples of the first two suffice to understand the third.
4
Formulate the original positive term
Remove the negation from the term to see what the original subject or predicate would be. For 'non-human', the original is 'human'. The proposition is 'diverted' from that.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحد يثبت له شيء أوهو لشيء آخر، أو يسلب عنه أو هو عن شيء آخر : فقد عدل به عن موضوعه الأصلي إلى غيره. وإنما أورد للأولى والثانية مثالاً دون الثالثة : لأنه قد علم من المثال الأول الموضوع المعدول، ومن المثال الثاني المحمول المعدول، فقد علم مثال معدولة الطرفين بجمعهما معا. وإن لم يكن حرف السلب جزء لشيء من الموضوع والمحمول سميت القضية «محصلة» - سواء كانت موجبة أو سالبة - كقولنا : «زيد كاتب» و «زيد ليس بكاتب». ووجه التسمية أن حرف السلب إذا لم يكن جزء من طرفيها فكل واحد من الطرفين وجودي محصل؛ وربما يخصص اسم المحصلة بالموجبة، و تسمى السالبة «بسيطة»، لأن البسيط ما لا جزء له، وحرف السلب وإن كان موجوداً فيها إلا أنه ليس جزء من طرفيها. وإنما لم يذكر لهما مثالاً لأن جميع الأمثلة المذكورة في المباحث السابقة تصلح أن تكون مثالاً لهما.
One to which something is affirmed or it is for another thing, or is negated from it or it is from something else: then it has been diverted from its original subject to something else. He only gave an example for the first and second, not the third, because from the first example the subject-modified [proposition] is known, and from the second the predicate-modified [proposition] is known, so the example of both-modified [proposition] is known by combining them together. If the negation particle is not part of anything of the subject and predicate, the proposition is called 'muhassalah' (actual) - whether it is affirmative or negative - like our saying 'Zayd is a writer' and 'Zayd is not a writer'. The reason for the name is that when the negation particle is not part of its two terms, then each of the two terms is an actual existent; and perhaps the name 'muhassalah' is specialized to the affirmative, and the negative is called 'simple' (basitah), because the simple is that which has no part, and the negation particle even if it exists in it, it is not part of its two terms. He did not give examples for them because all the examples mentioned in the previous discussions are suitable to be examples for them.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage defines two types of propositions: mu'addalah (diverted) and muhassalah (actual). It begins by explaining mu'addalah: a proposition where the negation particle is part of the subject or predicate (e.g., 'non-living' as subject) is called 'diverted' because its term is diverted from its original subject. The author (of the matn) only gave examples for two of the three types of mu'addalah (subject-modified and predicate-modified) but not for both-modified, because combining the two examples suffices. Then it defines muhassalah: a proposition where the negation particle is not part of either term. This includes both affirmative and negative, as in 'Zayd is a writer' (affirmative with no negative) and 'Zayd is not a writer' (negative but the subject and predicate are positive). The rationale for naming it muhassalah is that both terms are actual existents (not containing a negation particle). However, the term 'muhassalah' is sometimes restricted to affirmative, while the negative is called 'simple' (basitah) because it lacks a part (negation) in its terms, though the negation is present in the proposition as a whole. The author did not provide examples for these two because all earlier examples apply.

Key Terms
القضية المعدولة
المعدولة
حرف السلب

D) لا إنسان هو كاتب

Key Takeaway
A proposition is mu'addalah when the negation particle is an integral part of the subject or predicate term.

Muhassalah Propositions: Actual Terms Without Negation
Definition of muhassalah proposition and simple negative

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a muhassalah proposition and explain why it is called 'actual' or 'simple'.

Core Idea
A proposition is 'actual' when both subject and predicate are positive terms, even if the whole proposition is negative.

The author defines muhassalah as propositions where the negation particle is not part of either term. This includes both affirmative and negative forms. He also notes that some restrict 'muhassalah' to affirmatives, calling negatives 'simple'. Understanding this clarifies the classification of propositions in logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify if the proposition has a negation particle
Look for words like 'لا' or 'ليس'. If there is none, it is an affirmative muhassalah.
2
Check if the negation is part of the subject or predicate
If the negation is a separate word negating the whole predicate (e.g., 'ليس' in 'زيد ليس بكاتب') and not attached to a term, it is muhassalah (specifically a simple negative).
If the negation is attached to a term like 'لا إنسان', it is mu'addalah.
3
Classify as muhassalah or simple negative
If the proposition is affirmative and has no negation, it is muhassalah. If it is negative but negation is not part of the terms, it is a simple negative (salibah basitah), which the passage says is sometimes not called muhassalah.
The passage notes that some restrict 'muhassalah' to affirmatives only.
4
Understand the naming rationale
The term 'muhassalah' (actual) indicates that each term is a positive existent (not containing negation). The term 'simple' for the negative indicates the proposition lacks a negation part in its terms.

Affirmation and Negation Are About the Relation, Not the Terms
Author establishes that affirmation/negation depend on the relation, not on the terms themselves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether any proposition is affirmative or negative by inspecting whether the relation between subject and predicate is posited or removed, regardless of negation particles in the terms.

Core Idea
A proposition's quality depends on the relation, not on negative terms.

In this passage, al-Qutbi clarifies a common confusion: students think that a proposition containing 'not' in the subject or predicate is automatically negative. But the criterion is whether the relation (nisbah) between terms is affirmed or negated. We will learn to look at the internal relation rather than the words.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate
Find the two terms in the proposition. For example, in 'كل ماليس بحي فهو لا عالم', the subject is 'ماليس بحي' (non-living) and predicate is 'لا عالم' (non-knowing).
2
Determine the relation between them
Look at the linking word (e.g., 'هو' is, or the absence of 'ليس' or a negative copula). Is the relation affirmed or removed?
3
Check the presence of negation particles in terms
Note that the terms themselves may contain negation (e.g., 'لا عالم'). This is irrelevant for the quality of the proposition.
4
Apply the rule: affirmation = relation posited, negation = relation removed
If the relation is affirmed (positive copula), the proposition is affirmative even if terms are negative. If the relation is removed (negative copula or 'ليس'), the proposition is negative even if terms are positive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: والاعتبار بايجاب القضية وسلبها بالنسبة الثبوتية أو السلبية - لا بطرفي القضية - فإن قولنا: كلّ ماليس بحي فهو لا عالم موجبة مع أن طرفيها عدميان، وقولنا: لاشيء من المتحرك بساكن سالبة مع أن طرفيها وجوديان. القول: ربما يذهب الوهم إلى أن كل قضية تشتمل على حرف السلب تكون سالبة؛ ولما ذكر أن القضية المعدولة مشتملة على حرف ذلك قد تكون موجبة وقد تكون سالبة، ذكر معنى الإيجاب والسلب حتى يرتفع الاشتباه. فقد عرفت أن الإيجاب هو إيقاع النسبة، والسلب هو رفعها؛ فالعبرة في كون القضية موجبة وسالبة بإيقاع النسبة ورفعها – لا بطرفيها - فمتى كانت النسبة واقعة كانت القضيّة موجبة وإن كان طرفاها عدميين، كقولنا : «كل ماليس بحي فهو لا عالم» فإنّ الحكم فيها بثبوت اللاعالمية لكلّ ما صدق عليه أنه ليس بحي، فتكون موجبة وإن اشتمل طرفاها على حرف السلب. ومتى كانت النسبة مرفوعة فهي سالبة وإن كان طرفاها وجوديين كقولنا : «لاشيء من المتحرك بساكن»، فإن الحكم فيها بسلب الساكن عن كل ماصدق عليه المتحرّك فتكون سالبة، وإن لم يكن في شيء من طرفيها سلب، فليس الالتفات في الإيجاب والسلب إلى الأطراف بل إلى النسبة.
He said: Consideration is given to the affirmation and negation of the proposition based on the affirmative or negative relation - not to the two terms of the proposition. For our saying: 'Every non-living is non-knowing' is affirmative even though its two terms are negative, and our saying: 'No moving is at rest' is negative even though its two terms are positive. Statement: Perhaps the mind goes away to think that every proposition containing a negation particle is negative; and when he mentioned that the modified proposition contains a particle of that [negation] and may be affirmative or negative, he mentioned the meaning of affirmation and negation so that confusion is removed. You have known that affirmation is the positing of the relation, and negation is its removal. So the criterion in a proposition being affirmative or negative is the positing of the relation and its removal - not its two terms. Whenever the relation is posited, the proposition is affirmative, even if its two terms are negative, like our saying: 'Every non-living is non-knowing', for the judgment in it is the affirmation of non-knowingness to everything of which it is true that it is non-living, so it is affirmative, even if its two terms contain the negation particle. And whenever the relation is removed, it is negative, even if its two terms are positive, like our saying: 'No moving is at rest', for the judgment in it is the negation of rest from everything of which 'moving' is true, so it is negative, even if there is no negation in any of its two terms. So attention in affirmation and negation is not to the terms but to the relation.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage clarifies the criterion for determining whether a proposition is affirmative or negative. The author (al-Qutbi) explains that the common mistake is to think that any proposition containing a negation particle is negative. However, the criterion is not the terms but the relation: if the relation (i.e., the copula) is posited (positive), the proposition is affirmative; if the relation is removed (negated), it is negative. He gives two examples: 'Every non-living is non-knowing' is affirmative because it posits a relation (non-knowingness is affirmed of non-living), even though both subject and predicate contain negation particles. Conversely, 'No moving is at rest' is negative because it denies the relation (says 'moving is not at rest'), even though both terms are positive. The passage emphasizes that the negation particle in the subject or predicate does not affect the quality of the proposition; only the external negation (or absence thereof) of the relation matters. This is a key point for understanding the division of propositions.

Key Terms
الإيجاب
السلب
النسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The quality of a proposition is determined by the relation (nisbah), not by the presence of negation particles in the terms.

Scope Difference Between Simple Negative and Modified Affirmative
Author compares truth conditions of two proposition types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a simple negative and a modified affirmative proposition by identifying which is true when the subject does not exist.

Core Idea
A simple negative is true when the subject does not exist, but a modified affirmative is not.

In Islamic logic, it is crucial to understand how propositions behave when their subjects are non-existent. This passage clarifies the scope difference between two proposition types that may look similar but have different truth conditions. Mastering this distinction will help you avoid errors in reasoning about non-existent entities, which often appear in theological and jurisprudential discussions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two proposition types
Re-read the Arabic: 'السالبة البسيطة' (simple negative) and 'الموجبة المعدولة المحمول' (modified affirmative of the predicate). Note their structures: the simple negative uses 'ليس' separately, while the modified affirmative incorporates the negation into the predicate.
Example: 'زيد ليس بكاتب' vs 'زيد هو لا كاتب'.
2
Understand truth when subject exists
When the subject exists (e.g., 'زيد'), both propositions are logically equivalent in truth value. They are both either true or false together. The only difference is linguistic phrasing.
If Zayd is not a writer, both are true; if he is a writer, both are false.
3
Apply the case of a non-existent subject
Consider a subject that does not exist, like 'العدم' (non-existence). The simple negative 'العدم ليس بموجود' is true because it simply denies existence. But the modified affirmative 'العدم هو لا موجود' is false because it affirms something of a non-existent subject.
The passage states: السلب عند عدم الموضوع دون الإيجاب.
4
Draw the scope conclusion
Because the simple negative is true in all cases where the subject does not exist (and also when subject exists), it is more general (أعم) than the modified affirmative, which is only true when the subject exists.
The modified affirmative requires a subject that is 'موجود محقق' or 'مقدّر'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسالبة البسيطة أعمّ من الموجبة المعدولة المحمول لصدق السلب عند عدم الموضوع دون الإيجاب، فإنّ الإيجاب لايصلح إلا على موجود محقق كما في الخارجية الموضوع - أو مقدّر - كما في الحقيقية الموضوع - أما إذا كان الموضوع موجوداً فإنهما متلازمتان، والفرق بينهما في اللفظ:
The simple negative is more general than the modified affirmative of the predicate, because the negative is true when the subject does not exist, while the affirmative is not; for the affirmative is only sound for an actually existent subject - as in the external subject - or assumed - as in the true subject. However, if the subject exists, then they are equivalent, and the difference between them is in wording.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage discusses the logical relationship between two types of propositions: the simple negative (salibah basitah) and the modified affirmative of the predicate (mujabah mu'addalah al-mahmul). The simple negative is a negative proposition where the negation particle is not part of the terms (e.g., 'Zayd is not a writer'). The modified affirmative of the predicate is an affirmative proposition where the negation particle is part of the predicate (e.g., 'Zayd is non-knowing'). The author argues that the simple negative is more comprehensive (a'mm) in scope, meaning it is true in more cases. The reason is that if the subject does not exist (e.g., 'the non-existent'), the simple negative is true (since nothing is affirmed of it), but the modified affirmative is false because it requires the subject to exist either in external reality or in the mind (as a true subject). However, if the subject exists, both propositions are equivalent in truth value: they are true in exactly the same cases. The only difference then is linguistic: one uses a negative form, the other uses a modified term. This is a precise logical distinction regarding existential import.

Key Terms
السالبة البسيطة
الموجبة المعدولة المحمول
الخارجية والحقيقية الموضوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Criterion for Affirmative and Negative Propositions
Author explains that affirmation/negation is determined by the copula, not the terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether any categorical proposition in an Islamic text is affirmative or negative by examining its copula, regardless of negative particles in its terms.

Core Idea
Affirmation or negation is about the copula, not the words.

A common mistake is to assume that a proposition like 'Every non-living is non-knowing' is negative because it contains 'non-'. But in logic, the quality depends solely on whether the copula (nisba) is affirmed or denied. This lesson will sharpen your ability to read propositions accurately in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definitions
Start by internalizing the definitions: affirmation (ijab) = positing the relation (nisba) between subject and predicate; negation (salb) = removing that relation. These are the foundation.
2
Locate the copula in a proposition
In Arabic categorical propositions, the copula is often implied by the structure (e.g., 'هو' in 'فهو لا عالم') or by the quantifier+negation combination. Identify whether the proposition asserts or denies the predicate of the subject.
3
Ignore negative terms
Check if the subject or predicate contains a negative particle like 'لا' or 'غير'. Even if both terms are negative, the proposition may still be affirmative if it posits the relation. Do not let the terms deceive you.
Example: 'كل ماليس بحي فهو لا عالم' has negative terms but affirms 'non-knowing' of 'non-living'.
4
Test with contrasting examples
Apply the criterion to the given examples: the first is affirmative (positing relation) despite negative terms; the second is negative (removing relation) despite positive terms. This reinforces that the copula is decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول : ربما يذهب الوهم إلى أن كل قضية تشتمل على حرف السلب تكون سالبة؛ ولما ذكر أن القضية المعدولة مشتملة على حرف ذلك قد تكون موجبة وقد تكون سالبة، ذكر معنى الإيجاب والسلب حتى يرتفع الاشتباه. فقد عرفت أن الإيجاب هو إيقاع النسبة، والسلب هو رفعها؛ فالعبرة في كون القضية موجبة وسالبة بإيقاع النسبة ورفعها – لا بطرفيها - فمتى كانت النسبة واقعة كانت القضيّة موجبة وإن كان طرفاها عدميين، كقولنا : «كل ماليس بحي فهو لا عالم» فإنّ الحكم فيها بثبوت اللاعالمية لكلّ ما صدق عليه أنه ليس بحي، فتكون موجبة وإن اشتمل طرفاها على حرف السلب. ومتى كانت النسبة مرفوعة فهي سالبة وإن كان طرفاها وجوديين كقولنا : «لاشيء من المتحرك بساكن»، فإن الحكم فيها بسلب الساكن عن كل ماصدق عليه المتحرّك فتكون سالبة، وإن لم يكن في شيء من طرفيها سلب، فليس الالتفات في الإيجاب والسلب إلى الأطراف بل إلى النسبة.
Statement: Perhaps there is a misconception that every proposition containing a negative particle is negative. And when it was mentioned that the modified proposition contains that particle and may be affirmative or negative, the meaning of affirmation and negation was mentioned to remove doubt. You have known that affirmation is the positing of the relation, and negation is its removal. Therefore, the criterion for a proposition being affirmative or negative is the positing or removal of the relation—not its two terms. Whenever the relation is posited, the proposition is affirmative, even if its two terms are non-existent, like our saying: 'Every non-living is non-knowing'—for the judgment in it is the affirmation of non-knowledge of everything that is described as non-living, so it is affirmative even though its two terms contain a negative particle. And whenever the relation is removed, it is negative, even if its two terms are existent, like our saying: 'No moving thing is stationary'—for the judgment in it is the negation of stationary from everything that is described as moving, so it is negative, even though there is no negation in any of its two terms. Thus the attention in affirmation and negation is not to the terms but to the relation.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage defines the criterion for distinguishing affirmative and negative categorical propositions. The author first identifies a common misconception: that any proposition containing a negative particle is automatically negative. He then clarifies by reaffirming the definitions: affirmation (ijab) is the positing of the copulative relation (nisba), and negation (salb) is its removal. The decisive factor is whether the relation is posited or removed, irrespective of the terms. Two examples illustrate: the proposition 'Every non-living is non-knowing' (كل ماليس بحي فهو لا عالم) is affirmative because it posits the predicate 'non-knowing' of every subject that is 'non-living', even though both terms contain negative particles. Conversely, 'No moving thing is stationary' (لاشيء من المتحرك بساكن) is negative because it removes the predicate 'stationary' from every subject that is 'moving', even though neither term has a negative particle. Thus, the quality of the proposition depends solely on the copula, not the terms.

Key Terms
إيجاب
سلب
نسبة

B) Negative because it contains the negative particle 'لا'.
C) Negative because the copula removes the predicate from the subject.
D) Affirmative because it asserts a relation between moving and stationary.

Key Takeaway
The quality of a categorical proposition (affirmative or negative) is determined solely by the positing or removal of the copulative relation, regardless of the terms.

Simple Negative vs. Modified Affirmative: Generality and Equivalence
Author compares simple negative and modified affirmative, explains their relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify simple negative and modified affirmative propositions and determine their logical relationship based on subject existence.

Core Idea
The simple negative is more general because it includes cases where the subject does not exist.

In this passage, the author compares two types of propositions: the simple negative (e.g., 'Zayd is not a writer') and the modified affirmative (e.g., 'Zayd is non-writing'). He explains that the simple negative is more general because it holds even when the subject doesn't exist, while the modified affirmative requires the subject to exist. However, when the subject exists, they are equivalent. This distinction is crucial in logic for understanding how negation and modification work.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Look at the wording: if the predicate is directly negated (e.g., 'laysa') it is a simple negative; if the predicate is modified with a negative prefix (e.g., 'ghayr') and the copula is affirmative, it is a modified affirmative.
In tripartite propositions, the order of copula and negative particle determines the type.
2
Check subject existence
Determine if the subject exists in reality (external) or is taken as existent (real). The simple negative is true even if the subject does not exist; the modified affirmative requires existence.
3
Apply the generality rule
If the subject does not exist, the simple negative is true and the modified affirmative is false, so the simple negative is more general.
4
Equivalence when subject exists
If the subject exists, both propositions are equivalent in truth value – they are true under the same conditions.
This is a key logical relation: they are not identical but equivalent when the subject is instantiated.
5
Distinguish tripartite vs bipartite wording
In tripartite propositions (with explicit copula), the copula before the negative particle makes it affirmative; after makes it negative. In bipartite, conventions like 'ghayr' for modified affirmative and 'laysa' for simple negative are used.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٥٢ - النسبة بين القضية السالبة والمعدولة] قال : والسالبة البسيطة أعمّ من الموجبة المعدولة المحمول لصدق السلب عند عدم الموضوع دون الإيجاب، فإنّ الإيجاب لايصلح إلا على موجود محقق كما في الخارجية الموضوع - أو مقدّر - كما في الحقيقية الموضوع - أما إذا كان الموضوع موجوداً فإنهما متلازمتان، والفرق بينهما في اللفظ: أما في الثلاثية فالقضيّة موجبة إن قدّمت الرابطة على حرف السلب وسالبة إن أخرت عنها. وأما في الثنائية فبالنية أو بالاصطلاح على تخصيص لفظ «غير» أو «لا» بالإيجاب المعدول، ولفظ «ليس» بالسلب البسيط، أو بالعكس. القول : لقائل أن يقول العدول كما يكون في جانب المحمول كذلك يكون في جانب الموضوع - على مابينه - فحين ما شرع في الأحكام فلم خصص کلامه بالعدول في المحمول؟ ثم إن المحصلات والمعدولات المحمول كثيرة، فما الوجه في تخصيص السالبة البسيطة والموجبة المعدولة المحمول بالذكر ؟ فنقول : أما وجه التخصيص في الأوّل : فهو أن المعتبر في الفن من العدول ماجاء في جانب المحمول، وذلك لأنك قد حققت أن مناط الحكم ذات الموضوع ووصف المحمول، ولاخفاء في أن الحكم على الشيء بالأمور الوجودية يخالف الحكم عليه بالأمور العدمية، فاختلاف القضية بالعدول والتحصيل في المحمول يؤثر في مفهومها، بخلاف العدول 1) أي يوجب اختلاف مفهوم القضية مطلقاً (ن : قطعا)، فإن قولك : «زيد كاتب» قضية، وقولك : «زيد لا كاتب قضيّة أخرى يتخالف مفهوماهما في الحقيقة؛ وأما اختلاف العنوان بالعدول والتحصيل فلايوجب اختلافاً في مفهوم القضية، فإنه إذا كان لذات واحدة وصفان أحدهما وجودي كالجماد والآخر عدمي كاللاحي، وعبر عنها تارة بالوجودي وأخرى بالعدمي وحكم عليها في الحالين بحكم واحد : لم يحصل هناك قضيتان متخالفتان في المفهومية حقيقة (شریف).
[52 – The relation between the simple negative and the modified proposition] He said: The simple negative is more general than the modified affirmative of the predicate, because negation is true when the subject is non-existent, unlike affirmation. For affirmation is only valid for an actually existent subject – as in the external subject – or a presumed one – as in the real subject. But if the subject exists, then they are equivalent, and the difference between them is in wording: In tripartite propositions, the proposition is affirmative if the copula precedes the negative particle, and negative if it follows it. In bipartite propositions, it is by intention or convention, by specifying the word 'ghayr' or 'la' for the modified affirmative, and the word 'laysa' for the simple negative, or vice versa. Statement: Someone might say that modification occurs in the subject as well as in the predicate – as has been explained – so when he began the rulings, why did he restrict his discussion to modification in the predicate? Furthermore, there are many simple and modified predicates, so why specifically mention the simple negative and the modified affirmative of the predicate? We say: As for the reason for specification in the first: it is that what is considered in this art of modification is that which occurs in the predicate side, because you have verified that the basis of judgment is the subject's essence and the predicate's description. And it is clear that judging a thing by existential matters differs from judging it by non-existential matters. Therefore, modifying or not modifying the predicate in the proposition affects its concept, unlike modification in the subject (1) i.e., it necessarily causes a difference in the concept of the proposition (n: absolutely), for your saying 'Zayd is a writer' is one proposition, and your saying 'Zayd is not a writer' is another proposition whose concepts differ in reality. As for changing the title by modification or not, it does not cause a difference in the concept of the proposition, because if one essence has two descriptions, one existential like 'inanimate' and the other non-existential like 'non-living', and it is expressed sometimes by the existential and sometimes by the non-existential, and the same judgment is made on it in both cases, then there are not two propositions differing in concept in reality. (Sharif)
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage discusses the relationship between the simple negative proposition (saliba basita) and the modified affirmative proposition with a modified predicate (mujiba ma'dula al-mahmul). It begins by stating that the simple negative is more general (a'amm) than the modified affirmative because the simple negative holds true even when the subject does not exist, whereas affirmation requires the subject to exist (either actually or hypothetically). However, if the subject exists, they are equivalent, differing only in wording. The wording difference is explained: in tripartite propositions (those with an explicit copula), the proposition is affirmative if the copula precedes the negative particle (e.g., Zayd is not-writing but the copula 'is' comes first) and negative if the copula follows the negative particle. In bipartite propositions (without explicit copula), the distinction is made by convention, such as using 'ghayr' or 'la' for modified affirmative and 'laysa' for simple negative, or vice versa. Then an objection is raised: why focus on modification in the predicate when modification can also occur in the subject, and why specifically compare the simple negative and the modified affirmative? The answer justifies the specification: the art of logic primarily considers modification in the predicate because the basis of judgment (manat al-hukm) is the essence of the subject and the description of the predicate. Judging with existential matters (umur wujudiya) differs from judging with non-existential matters; thus, modifying the predicate changes the proposition's concept (mafhum). For example, 'Zayd is a writer' and 'Zayd is not a writer' are two different propositions with distinct concepts. In contrast, modifying the subject (e.g., referring to the same entity as 'inanimate' or 'non-living') does not change the concept because the same judgment applies. The passage concludes with a marginal note from Sharif affirming this point.

Key Terms
السالبة البسيطة
الموجبة المعدولة المحمول
عدول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when are the simple negative and modified affirmative equivalent?
A) When the subject is non-existent
B) When the subject exists
C) When the predicate is modified
D) When the wording is tripartite

Key Takeaway
The simple negative is more general than the modified affirmative, but they are logically equivalent when the subject exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Construct a simple negative and a modified affirmative from the subject 'السارق (the thief)' and predicate 'مقطوع اليد (hand-cut)'. Then determine which is more general.

Why Predicate Modification Matters: The Basis of Judgment
Author justifies focusing on predicate modification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why modifying the predicate changes a proposition's concept while modifying the subject does not.

Core Idea
The basis of judgment is the subject's essence and the predicate's description; altering the predicate alters the judgment.

The passage addresses a possible objection: why focus on predicate modification when subject modification also exists? The author argues that the basis of judgment (manat al-hukm) is the subject's essence and the predicate's description. Judging with existential descriptions differs from judging with non-existential ones. Changing the predicate thus changes the proposition's concept, whereas changing the subject's description (e.g., calling the same thing 'inanimate' or 'non-living') does not, because the same judgment applies.

Guided Path
1
Understand manat al-hukm
The basis of judgment is the combination of the subject's essence (dhat al-mawdu') and the predicate's description (wasf al-mahmul). This is what determines the truth conditions of the proposition.
2
Compare existential vs non-existential predicates
When the predicate is existential (e.g., 'writer') vs non-existential (e.g., 'non-writer'), the judgment differs because the predicate description is different.
This difference affects the concept (mafhum) of the proposition.
3
Test subject modification
Consider an entity with two descriptions: existential ('inanimate') and non-existential ('non-living'). If the same judgment is applied to both (e.g., 'it is a body'), the proposition remains the same in concept.
The passage gives this example to show that subject modification does not create a new proposition.
4
Draw the conclusion
Only predicate modification yields genuinely distinct propositions because it changes the basis of judgment. Subject modification may only change the wording without affecting the logical content.

5 min
Why Only Predicate Modification Matters in Logic
Author answers why only predicate modification is considered.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why only predicate modification is considered in categorical propositions and distinguish between subject and predicate modification.

Core Idea
Only predicate modification changes propositional meaning because judgment is about the subject's essence, not its description.

In studying categorical propositions, you learned about 'adl (modification) and tahsil (non-modification). The author has focused on modification in the predicate. But why not also consider modification in the subject? After all, you can say 'Non-writer is Zaid' just as well as 'Zaid is non-writer.' This lesson addresses that objection and clarifies the logical rationale behind the author's choice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the hypothetical questioner's claim: that modification occurs in both subject and predicate. The objection is that just as you can have 'Zaid is non-writer' (predicate modification), you can also have 'Non-writer is Zaid' (subject modification). So why focus only on the predicate?
2
Extract the author's key premise
The author states that the basis of judgment (manat al-hukm) is the essence of the subject and the attribute of the predicate. Judgment concerns the subject's essence (what it is in itself) and the predicate as an attribute attached to that essence.
This is a metaphysical claim about what propositions assert.
3
Contrast predicate vs subject modification
When modification occurs in the predicate, the attribute is changed from existential (positive) to privative (negative). Since the predicate is what is affirmed or denied of the subject, this change alters the proposition's meaning. For example, 'Zaid is writer' vs 'Zaid is non-writer' are opposite judgments.
4
Evaluate subject modification
When modification occurs in the description of the subject, it only changes the expression used to refer to the subject, not the essence being judged. The thing judged (the subject's essence) remains the same. For example, 'Writer is Zaid' and 'Non-writer is Zaid' both attribute something to the same essence (Zaid), though the description differs.
Thus subject modification does not affect the proposition's logical concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول : لقائل أن يقول العدول كما يكون في جانب المحمول كذلك يكون في جانب الموضوع - على مابينه - فحين ما شرع في الأحكام فلم خصص كلامه بالعدول في المحمول؟
فنقول : أما وجه التخصيص في الأوّل : فهو أن المعتبر في الفن من العدول ماجاء في جانب المحمول، وذلك لأنك قد حققت أن مناط الحكم ذات الموضوع ووصف المحمول، ولاخفاء في أن الحكم على الشيء بالأمور الوجودية يخالف الحكم عليه بالأمور العدمية، فاختلاف القضية بالعدول والتحصيل في المحمول يؤثر في مفهومها، بخلاف العدول والتحصيل في وصف الموضوع، فإنّه لا يؤثر في مفهوم القضية، لأن العدول والتحصيل إنما يكون في مفهوم الموضوع، وهو غير المحكوم عليه، لأن المحكوم عليه عبارة عن ذات الموضوع والحكم على الشيء لا يختلف باختلاف العبارات عنه.
Statement: A speaker may say: modification, just as it occurs in the side of the predicate, so too it occurs in the side of the subject — as has been explained. So when he embarked on the rulings, why did he restrict his speech to modification in the predicate?
We say: As for the reason for the specification in the first: it is that what is considered in the art of modification is what appears in the side of the predicate. That is because you have verified that the locus of judgment is the essence of the subject and the attribute of the predicate. It is no secret that judging a thing by existential matters differs from judging it by privative matters. Thus the difference of the proposition by modification and non-modification in the predicate affects its concept, unlike modification and non-modification in the description of the subject, for it does not affect the concept of the proposition, because modification and non-modification occur only in the concept of the subject, and the subject is not the thing judged, because the thing judged is the essence of the subject, and judgment about a thing does not differ with differing expressions about it.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange where a hypothetical questioner objects to the author's exclusive focus on predicate modification in categorical propositions. The objection states that just as modification occurs in the predicate, it also occurs in the subject, so why did the author restrict his discussion to the predicate? The author answers that in logic only modification in the predicate is considered significant. The reason is that the basis of the judgment (the ruling) is the essence of the subject (the thing being judged) and the attribute of the predicate (the thing being attributed). Judging a thing by existential (positive) attributes is different from judging it by privative (negative) attributes. Therefore, whether the proposition is modified or simple with respect to the predicate changes its conceptual meaning. In contrast, modification or non-modification in the description of the subject does not affect the proposition's concept because it only changes the expression (the wording) of the subject, while the actual thing being judged (the subject's essence) remains the same. Thus only predicate modification is relevant.

In categorical propositions, only modification in the predicate changes the logical meaning because the judgment is about the subject's essence and the predicate's attribute; subject alteration only affects expression, not the thing judged.

The Four Types of Categorical Propositions
Author enumerates the four types from predicate modification and clarifies which pairs are easily confused.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any categorical proposition as simple or modified, and affirmative or negative, by analyzing the placement of the negation particle.

Core Idea
Predicate modification quadruples the division of propositions into four distinct types.

In this passage, the author explains how considering modification in the predicate yields four kinds of categorical propositions. This classification is crucial for understanding the precise logical form of statements in Islamic logic, especially when analyzing arguments in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basis of division
Identify the two criteria: (a) Is the negation particle part of the predicate? (modified or simple) (b) Is the proposition affirmative or negative?
2
Learn the four combinations
List the four: simple affirmative, simple negative, modified affirmative, modified negative. Use the author's examples to anchor each type.
3
Memorise the examples
Commit to memory: simple affirmative (Zaid is a writer), simple negative (Zaid is not a writer), modified affirmative (Zaid is a non-writer), modified negative (Zaid is not a non-writer).
4
Practice classification
Given a proposition, determine whether it is simple/modified and affirmative/negative based on the negation particle's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما وجه التخصيص في الثاني فلأن اعتبار العدول والتحصيل في المحمول يربع القسمة، لأن حرف السلب إن كان جزء من المحمول فالقضية معدولة وإلا فمحصلة كيفما كان الموضوع، وأيا ما كان فهي إما موجبة أو سالبة : فهاهنا أربع قضايا :
موجبة محصلة، كقولنا : «زيد كاتب».
وسالبة محصلة، كقولنا : «زيد ليس بكاتب».
وموجبة معدولة، كقولنا : «زيد لاكاتب».
وسالبة معدولة كقولنا : «ليس زيد بلاكاتب».
ولا التباس بين قضيّتين من هذه القضايا إلا بين السالبة المحصلة والموجبة المعدولة.
أما بين الموجبة المحصلة والسالبة المحصلة : فلعدم حرف السلب في الموجبة ووجوده في السالبة.
وأما بين الموجبة المحصلة والموجبة المعدولة : فلوجود حرف السلب في المعدولة دون الموجبة المحصلة.
وأما بين الموجبة المحصلة والسالبة المعدولة : فلوجود حرفي السلب في السالبة المعدولة ، بخلاف الموجبة المحصلة.
As for the reason for the specification in the second: it is because considering modification and non-modification in the predicate quadruples the division. For if the negation particle is part of the predicate, then the proposition is modified; otherwise it is unmodified, however the subject is. And whichever it is, it is either affirmative or negative. So here are four propositions:
Simple affirmative, like our saying: 'Zaid is a writer.'
Simple negative, like our saying: 'Zaid is not a writer.'
Modified affirmative, like our saying: 'Zaid is a non-writer.'
Modified negative, like our saying: 'Zaid is not a non-writer.'
There is no confusion between any two of these propositions except between the simple negative and the modified affirmative.
As for between the simple affirmative and the simple negative: because of the absence of the negation particle in the affirmative and its presence in the negative.
As for between the simple affirmative and the modified affirmative: because of the presence of the negation particle in the modified affirmative, unlike the simple affirmative.
As for between the simple affirmative and the modified negative: because of the presence of two negation particles in the modified negative, unlike the simple affirmative.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and clarification of the four types of categorical propositions that result from considering modification (inclusion of negation) in the predicate. The author explains why he specifically mentioned only the simple negative and modified affirmative earlier: because predicate modification yields four possible combinations. The division works as follows: the negation particle either is part of the predicate (making it modified) or not (making it simple); and the proposition as a whole is either affirmative or negative. Thus we have: (1) Simple affirmative (no negation particle at all), e.g., 'Zaid is a writer.' (2) Simple negative (negation particle outside the predicate, i.e., 'not' applies to the whole copula), e.g., 'Zaid is not a writer.' (3) Modified affirmative (negation particle inside the predicate, making it a positive statement with a negated term), e.g., 'Zaid is a non-writer.' (4) Modified negative (negation both outside and inside, i.e., the predicate is negated via a negation particle and the copula is also negated), e.g., 'Zaid is not a non-writer.' The author then clarifies that among these four, the only pair that might be confused is the simple negative (2) and the modified affirmative (3), because both involve a single negation but placed differently. The other pairs are easily distinguished: simple affirmative vs simple negative by presence/absence of negation in the copula; simple affirmative vs modified affirmative by presence of negation in the predicate; simple affirmative vs modified negative by presence of two negations in the latter.

Key Terms
قضية محصلة
قضية معدولة
حرف السلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The four types of categorical propositions are simple affirmative, simple negative, modified affirmative, and modified negative, determined by predicate modification and affirmation/negation.

Distinguishing Simple Negative from Modified Affirmative
Author clarifies the only ambiguous pair among the four proposition types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the simple negative and modified affirmative are easily confused and how to distinguish them based on the position of the negation particle.

Core Idea
The only confusion among the four types is between simple negative and modified affirmative, both have one negation but placed differently.

The author highlights that among the four proposition types, only one pair is prone to confusion: the simple negative ('Zaid is not a writer') and the modified affirmative ('Zaid is a non-writer'). This lesson will help you see why they are different and how to avoid the confusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two propositions
Write out the simple negative (e.g., 'Zaid is not a writer') and the modified affirmative (e.g., 'Zaid is a non-writer') from the author's examples.
2
Note the negation placement
In simple negative, the negation is on the copula (the 'is not'); in modified affirmative, the negation is inside the predicate (the 'non-writer').
3
Translate into logical form
Simple negative: 'It is not the case that Zaid is a writer' (~Wz). Modified affirmative: 'Zaid is a non-writer' (Z has the property of non-writer: ¬W? Actually it's 'Z is non-writer' which is Wz? No: careful: 'non-writer' is a property, so it's 'Z is a non-writer' ~Wz? Actually 'Z is a non-writer' is often rendered as ¬W(z), but that is logically equivalent to ~Wz? But the distinction is important. The author's point is that they are not equivalent because in simple negative the negation is outside, in modified affirmative it is inside the predicate. So logical forms differ: simple negative ¬(Wz), modified affirmative (¬W)z? Better: predicate negation: Z has the property of not being a writer. In standard logic, both are ¬Wz but the author considers them syntactically distinct. Nonetheless, we follow the author's distinction.
4
Understand why they are confused
Both involve a single negation, but they are logically different: one denies the predicate, the other affirms a negated predicate.
5
Practice distinguishing
Given a proposition, decide whether it is simple negative or modified affirmative by checking if the negation particle is attached to the predicate or separate.
