Distinguishing Simple Negative from Affirmative Modified Propositions
Author delineates the confusion between simple negative and affirmative modified propositions and clarifies their difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a simple negative and an affirmative modified proposition by analyzing truth conditions and the role of subject existence.

Core Idea
The simple negative is more general: it is true even when the subject does not exist, while the affirmative modified requires the subject to exist.

The statement 'Zayd is not a writer' appears to be a simple negation, but it could also be interpreted as an affirmative proposition with a privative predicate: 'Zayd is non-writer'. The Islamic logicians carefully distinguished these two types to avoid confusion in theological reasoning, especially when discussing attributes of non-existent entities like the partner of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Count the negation signs
Both the simple negative and the affirmative modified have exactly one negation sign. This alone does not distinguish them, so further analysis is needed.
2
Examine the predicate form
In the affirmative modified, the predicate is a privation (e.g., 'non-writer'), whereas in the simple negative, the predicate is a positive term (e.g., 'writer'). The presence of a privative prefix (like 'non-') indicates a modified predicate.
3
Test truth conditions with a non-existent subject
Apply the proposition to a subject that does not exist, e.g., 'The partner of Allah.' The simple negative 'The partner of Allah is not seeing' is true, but the affirmative modified 'The partner of Allah is non-seeing' is false because affirmation of any predicate, even a privation, requires the subject to exist.
This test reveals the core difference: the simple negative does not assume existence, while the affirmative modified does.
4
Apply the generality rule
Whenever the affirmative modified is true, the simple negative is also true, but not vice versa. The simple negative is more general because it is true even when the subject is non-existent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمّا بين السالبة المحصلة والسالبة المعدولة : فلوجود حرفي السلب في السالبة المعدولة وحرف واحد في السالبة المحصلة. وأما بين الموجبة المعدولة والسالبة المعدولة : فلوجود حرف واحد في الإيجاب وحرفين في السلب. وأما السالبة المحصلة والموجبة المعدولة المحمول فبينهما التباس من حيث أن حرف السلب الموجود فيهما واحد، فإذا قيل : «زيـد لـيـس بكاتب فلا يعلم أنها موجبة معدولة أو سالبة بسيطة، فلهذا خصصهما بالذكر من بين القضايا ؛ والفرق بينهما معنوي ولفظي، أما المعنوي فهو أن السالبة البسيطة أعم من الموجبة المعدولة المحمول، لأنه متى صدقت الموجبة المعدولة المحمول صدقت السالبة البسيطة ولا ينعكس. أما الأول فلأنه متى ثبت اللاباء لـ(ج) يصدق سلب الباء عنه، فإنه لو لم يصدق سلب الباء عنه ثبت له الباء فيكون الباء واللاباء ثابتين له، وهو اجتماع النقيضين. وأما الثاني - وهو أنه لا يلزم من صدق السالبة البسيطة صدق الموجبة المعدولة المحمول - فلأن الإيجاب لا يصح على المعدوم، ضرورة أن ايجاب الشيء لغيره فرع على وجود المثبت له بخلاف السلب، فإنّ الإيجاب لما لم يصدق على المعدومات صح السلب عنها بالضرورة، فيجوز أن يكون الموضوع معدوماً، وحينئذ يصدق السلب البسيط (۱) سواء كان ذلك الشيء أمراً وجودياً أو عدميّاً، فإن ثبوت «اللاكتابة» لزيد فرع على وجوده، كما أن ثبوت الكتابة له كذلك (شريف). ولا يصدق الإيجاب المعدول؛ كما أنه يصدق قولنا : «شريك الباري ليس ببصير»، ولا يصدق شريك الباري غير بصير»، لأن معنى الأول سلب البصر عن شريك الباري، ولما كان الموضوع معدوماً صدق سلب كلّ مفهوم عنه، ومعنى الثاني أن عدم البصر ثابت لشريك الباري، فلابد أن يكون موجوداً في نفسه حتى يمكن ثبوت شيء له هو ممتنع الوجود. لا يقال : لو صدق السلب عند عدم الموضوع لم يكن بين الموجبة الكلية والسالبة الجزئية تناقض، لأنهما قديجتمعان على الصدق حينئذ، فإن من الجائز إثبات المحمول لجميع الأفراد الموجودة وسلبه عن بعض الأفراد المعدومة. لانا نقول : الحكم في السالبة على الأفراد الموجودة، كما أن الحكم في الموجبة على الأفراد الموجودة، إلا أن صدق السلب لا يتوقف على وجود الأفراد، وصدق الإيجاب يتوقف عليه، فإن معنى الموجبة للكلية أن (۱) وذلك لأن السلب رفع الإيجاب، فإذا كان الإيجاب متعلقاً بالأفراد الموجودة، الا كان رفعه أيضاً متعلقاً بها، فيكون الإيجاب والسلب واردين على الموجودات - أي يعتبر ذلك في مفهوم الموجبة والسالبة - لكن تحقق السالبة وصدقها لا يتوقف على وجودها، لأن محصلها انتفاء الشيء عن شيء - أي انتفاء المحمول عن ذات الموضوع - وذلك إما بأن يكون الموضوع موجودا وينتفي المحمول عنه، وإما بأن لا يوجد الموضوع، فينتفي عنه المحمول أيضاً قطعياً؛ ومحصل الموجبة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع، ولا يتصور ذلك إلا بأن يكون الموضوع موجوداً ثابتا له المحمول. وتلخيصه أن انتفاء شيء عن الموضوع قد يكون بانتفائه في نفسه وقد لا يكون. وأما ثبوت الشيء له فلا يمكن إلا بأن يكون موجوداً (شريف).
As for between the simple negative and the modified negative: because there are two letters of negation in the modified negative and one letter in the simple negative. And as for between the affirmative modified and the negative modified: because there is one letter in the affirmative and two in the negative. And as for the simple negative and the affirmative with modified predicate, there is confusion between them because the letter of negation present in them is one. So when it is said: 'Zayd is not a writer', it is not known whether it is an affirmative modified or a simple negative. For this reason, he specified them for mention among the propositions. The difference between them is semantic and verbal. As for the semantic: the simple negative is more general than the affirmative with modified predicate, because whenever the affirmative with modified predicate is true, the simple negative is true, and the converse does not hold. As for the first: because whenever 'la-ba'' is affirmed for (J), the negation of ba' from it is true; for if the negation of ba' from it were not true, then ba' would be affirmed for it, and thus ba' and la-ba' would be both established for it, which is the conjunction of contradictories. And as for the second – which is that the truth of the simple negative does not entail the truth of the affirmative with modified predicate – because affirmation is not valid for the non-existent, necessarily because affirming something for something else depends on the existence of that which is affirmed for, unlike negation. For since affirmation is not true of non-existents, negation of them is necessarily true. Thus it is possible that the subject is non-existent, and then the simple negative is true (1) whether that thing is existent or non-existent; for the establishment of 'non-writing' for Zayd depends on his existence, just as the establishment of writing for him does (Sharif). And the affirmative modified is not true; as it is true that 'The partner of Allah is not seeing', and it is not true that 'The partner of Allah is non-seeing', because the meaning of the first is the negation of sight from the partner of Allah, and since the subject is non-existent, the negation of every concept from it is true; and the meaning of the second is that non-sight is established for the partner of Allah, so it must be existent in itself so that the establishment of something for it is possible, which is impossible. It cannot be said: If the negative is true when the subject is non-existent, then there would be no contradiction between the universal affirmative and the particular negative, because they could both be true at that time. For it is possible to affirm the predicate for all existing individuals and negate it from some non-existent individuals. For we say: The judgment in the negative is about the existing individuals, just as the judgment in the affirmative is about the existing individuals, except that the truth of the negative does not depend on the existence of the individuals, while the truth of the affirmative does depend on it. For the meaning of the universal affirmative is that (1) and that is because negation is the removal of affirmation. So if affirmation pertains to existing individuals, then its removal also pertains to them. Thus affirmation and negation are applied to existents – that is, it is considered in the concept of the affirmative and the negative – but the realization and truth of the negative do not depend on their existence, because its purport is the negation of something from something – i.e., the negation of the predicate from the essence of the subject. That is either because the subject exists and the predicate is negated from it, or because the subject does not exist, so the predicate is also necessarily negated from it. And the purport of the affirmative is the establishment of the predicate for the subject, and that cannot be conceived unless the subject exists and the predicate is established for it. And its summary is that the negation of something from the subject may be due to its negation in itself or may not be. As for the establishment of something for it, it is only possible when it exists (Sharif).
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary (sharh) that clarifies the distinctions between different types of negative and modified propositions, particularly focusing on the confusion between the simple negative (saliba muhassala) and the affirmative with modified predicate (mujiba ma'dula al-mahmul). The author begins by enumerating the differences between three pairs: first, between the simple negative and the modified negative, where the modified negative contains two negation particles while the simple negative contains one; second, between the affirmative modified and the negative modified, where the affirmative has one negation particle (the modifier) and the negative has two; third, between the simple negative and the affirmative modified, which both have one negation particle, causing potential ambiguity. For example, the statement "Zayd is not a writer" could be interpreted either as a simple negative (negating the predicate 'writer' from Zayd) or as an affirmative modified (affirming the predicate 'non-writer' for Zayd). The author explains that the logician (presumably the author of al-Shamsiyya) specifically mentioned these two types to resolve this confusion. The difference is twofold: semantic (ma'nawi) and verbal (lafzi). Semantically, the simple negative is more general (a'amm) than the affirmative modified: whenever the affirmative modified is true, the simple negative is also true, but not vice versa. The proof: if the privation (la-ba') is established for something (J), then the negation of ba' from it is true; otherwise, ba' would be affirmed, leading to a contradiction (ba' and la-ba' together). Conversely, the truth of the simple negative does not imply the truth of the affirmative modified, because affirmation (positive attribution) is only valid for existing things; to affirm a property (even a privation) requires that the subject exists. For example, "The partner of Allah is not seeing" is true as a simple negative because the subject (partner of Allah) does not exist, so any predicate is negated from it. But "The partner of Allah is non-seeing" is false as an affirmative modified, because it would affirm 'non-seeing' of a non-existent subject, which is impossible without the subject's existence. The author then addresses an objection: if simple negatives can be true due to non-existence of the subject, then a universal affirmative (e.g., "All J are B") and a particular negative ("Some J are not B") could both be true when some individuals of J are non-existent, undermining the law of contradiction. The response: the judgment in both types of propositions is about the existing individuals only. The truth of a negative does not depend on the existence of the individuals, while the truth of an affirmative does. A negative's meaning is simply the negation of the predicate from the subject, which can happen either because the subject exists without the predicate or because the subject does not exist. In contrast, an affirmative requires the subject to exist for the predicate to be established. Thus, the universal affirmative and particular negative remain contradictory when evaluated over the domain of existing individuals, and the non-existence of some individuals does not affect this relationship.

Checkpoint
Consider the proposition: 'An angel that does not exist is not a messenger.' According to the distinction in the text, which type is it and why is the affirmative modified false?
A) It is a simple negative; the affirmative modified is false because the subject does not exist, and affirmation requires existence.
B) It is an affirmative modified; the simple negative would be false because the subject cannot have negation applied.
C) It is a simple negative; the affirmative modified is false because the subject exists but lacks the property.
D) It is an affirmative modified; the simple negative is false because negation of a non-existent subject is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The key difference is that the affirmative modified affirms a privation, requiring the subject's existence, while the simple negative only negates, and is true even if the subject does not exist.

Propositions
intermediate
8 min
Negative vs. Privative and Subject Existence
Author distinguishes negative from privative propositions and defends the existence requirement for affirmatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between simple negative and privative affirmative propositions and explain why affirmatives require subject existence while negatives do not.

Core Idea
Negation can be true of non-existents; affirmation cannot.

In this passage from the commentary on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya, the author clarifies a foundational distinction: simple negation (salb) does not require the subject to exist, while privative affirmation ('adl) does. He uses the theological example 'The partner of God is not seeing' vs. 'The partner of God is not-seeing (blind)' to illustrate the point. He then defends the standard logical doctrine that universal affirmatives require existence of their subjects, whereas particular negatives do not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two proposition types
Find in the Arabic the terms 'ليس ببصير' (simple negation) and 'غير بصير' (privative). Note the difference in meaning: the first negates the attribute, the second asserts the opposite attribute.
The first is true even if the subject does not exist; the second requires an existing subject to have that attribute.
2
Understand the subject existence condition
For the simple negative, the non-existence of the subject makes the negation true. For the privative, the subject must exist to have the attribute.
This is the core logical distinction being made.
3
Analyze the objection
The objector claims that if negation is true with non-existent subjects, then a universal affirmative and particular negative could both be true, breaking contradiction. Locate this objection in the text.
The objection is introduced with 'لا يقال'.
4
Trace the author's response
The author explains that universal affirmative judges only existing individuals, so its truth depends on existence. Particular negative can be true either by non-existence of all individuals or by some existing individuals lacking the predicate. Thus contradiction holds.
The key is that the affirmative and negative are about the same existing individuals, not about non-existent ones.
5
Examine the nuance about external vs real propositions
The author clarifies what 'existence of subject' means for different proposition types: external requires actual existence; real requires conceivability.
This avoids the objection that real propositions would never be true if existence meant external-only.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العدول والتحصيل
ولا يصدق الإيجاب المعدول؛ كما أنه يصدق قولنا : «شريك الباري ليس ببصير»، ولا يصدق شريك الباري غير بصير»، لأن معنى الأول سلب البصر عن شريك الباري، ولما كان الموضوع معدوماً صدق سلب كلّ مفهوم عنه، ومعنى الثاني أن عدم البصر ثابت لشريك الباري، فلابد أن يكون موجوداً في نفسه حتى يمكن ثبوت شيء له هو ممتنع الوجود.
لا يقال : لو صدق السلب عند عدم الموضوع لم يكن بين الموجبة الكلية والسالبة الجزئية تناقض، لأنهما قد يجتمعان على الصدق حينئذ، فإن من الجائز إثبات المحمول لجميع الأفراد الموجودة وسلبه عن بعض الأفراد المعدومة.
لانا نقول : الحكم في السالبة على الأفراد الموجودة"، كما أن الحكم في الموجبة على الأفراد الموجودة، إلا أن صدق السلب لا يتوقف على وجود الأفراد، وصدق الإيجاب يتوقف عليه، فإن معنى الموجبة للكلية أن جميع أفراد (ج) الموجودة يثبت له (ب)، ولاشك أنها إنما تصدق إذا كانت أفراد (ج) موجودة ؛ ومعنى السالبة أنه ليس كذلك : أي كل واحد من الأفراد الموجودة لـ(ج) ليس يثبت له (ب) ويصدق هذا المعنى تارة بأن لا يكون شيء من الأفراد موجوداً، وأخرى بأن تكون موجودة ويثبت اللاباء لها، وعند ذلك يتحقق التناقض جزماً.
وأما قوله : «لأنّ الإيجاب لا يصح إلا على موجود محقق كما في الخارجية الموضوع، أو مقدركما في الحقيقية الموضوع» فلادخل له في بيان الفرق، إذ يكفي فيه أن الإيجاب يستدعي وجود الموضوع دون السلب؛ وأما أن الموضوع موجود في الخارج محققاً أو مقدراً فلاحاجة إليه.
فكأنه جواب سؤال يذكر هاهنا ويقال : إن عنيتم بقولكم «الإيجاب يستدعي وجود الموضوع» أن الإيجاب يستدعي وجود الموضوع في الخارج : فلا تصدق الموجبة الحقيقية أصلاً، لأن الحكم فيها ليس مقصوراً على الموضوعات الموجوده في الخارج. و إن عنيتم به أن الإيجاب يستدعي مطلق الوجود : فالسالبة أيضاً تستدعي مطلق الوجود لأن المحكوم عليه لابد أن يكون متصوّراً بوجه ما، وإن كان الحكم بالسلب؛ فلا فرق بين الموجبة والسالبة في ذلك. فأجاب بأنّ كلامنا ليس إلا في القضية الخارجية والحقيقية - لا في مطلق القضية على ماسبقت الإشارة إليه - فالمراد بقولنا «الإيجاب يستدعي وجود الموضوع أن الموجبة إن كانت خارجية يجب أن يكون موضوعها موجوداً في الخارج محققاً، وإن كانت حقيقية يجب أن يكون
Modification and Accomplishment
And the modified affirmative is not true; just as our saying 'The partner of God is not seeing' is true, and 'The partner of God is not-seeing' is not true, because the meaning of the first is the negation of sight from the partner of God, and since the subject is non-existent, the negation of every concept from it is true. The meaning of the second is that the absence of sight is established for the partner of God, so it must be existent in itself so that something can be established for it, but it is impossible in existence.
It may not be said: If negation is true when the subject does not exist, then there would be no contradiction between the universal affirmative and the particular negative, because they could both be true at that time, for it is possible to affirm the predicate for all existing individuals and negate it for some non-existent individuals.
We say: The judgment in the negative is about existing individuals, just as the judgment in the affirmative is about existing individuals, except that the truth of the negation does not depend on the existence of the individuals, while the truth of the affirmation depends on it. For the meaning of the universal affirmative is that all existing individuals of (C) have (B) established for them, and undoubtedly it is only true if the individuals of (C) exist. The meaning of the negative is that it is not so: i.e., each one of the existing individuals of (C) does not have (B) established for it, and this meaning is true sometimes when none of the individuals exist, and other times when they exist and negativity is established for them, and in that case contradiction is definitely realized.
As for his statement: 'Because affirmation is only valid for an existing thing, either realized as in the external-subject proposition, or presumed as in the real-subject proposition,' it has no bearing on clarifying the difference, for it is sufficient that affirmation requires the existence of the subject while negation does not. As for whether the subject exists externally as realized or presumed, there is no need for it.
It is as if it is an answer to a question mentioned here. It is said: If you mean by your statement 'Affirmation requires the existence of the subject' that affirmation requires the existence of the subject externally, then the real affirmative would never be true, because the judgment in it is not restricted to subjects existing externally. And if you mean by it that affirmation requires absolute existence, then the negative also requires absolute existence because the subject of judgment must be conceived in some way, even if the judgment is by negation; so there is no difference between affirmative and negative in that regard. He answered that our discussion is only about the external and real propositions—not about absolute propositions as previously indicated—so the meaning of our statement 'Affirmation requires the existence of the subject' is that if the affirmative is external, its subject must exist externally as realized, and if it is real, its subject must be...
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya clarifies the crucial distinction between simple negative propositions and privative (or modified) affirmative propositions, and then defends the standard logical doctrine that affirmative propositions require the existence of their subject while negative propositions do not. The author begins with an example from Islamic theology: 'Sharik al-Bari (the partner of God) is not seeing' is true because the subject is non-existent, so negating sight is correct. But 'Sharik al-Bari is not-seeing' (i.e., blind) is false because it asserts a positive attribute (blindness) that requires an existing subject. This illustrates the difference between simple negation (salb) and privation ('adl). Next, an objection is raised: if negation can be true with a non-existent subject, then the universal affirmative (e.g., 'All existing Cs are B') and the particular negative (e.g., 'Some Cs are not B') could both be true simultaneously, which would break the law of contradiction. The author answers that the universal affirmative judges only existing individuals, and its truth depends on their existence. The particular negative, however, does not require existence: it is true either when there are existing individuals that lack B, or when there are no individuals at all. Thus, they cannot both be true under the same conditions—the affirmative requires existence, the negative does not—so contradiction is maintained. Finally, the author addresses a further nuance about the phrase 'affirmation requires existence of the subject.' Some might object that if 'existence' means external existence, then real (haqiqiyya) propositions (which judge possible individuals irrespective of actual existence) would never be true. The author clarifies that the requirement is relative: for external propositions, the subject must actually exist in the external world; for real propositions, the subject must be conceivable (muqaddar) as existing in the mind. Negation, in contrast, does not require even that much. Throughout, the author uses standard logical terminology and defends the positions of the matn with precision.

According to the author, what is the key difference between the propositions 'شريك الباري ليس ببصير' and 'شريك الباري غير بصير'?
A) The first is a simple negative that can be true even though the subject does not exist; the second is a privative affirmative that requires the subject to exist.
B) Both are equivalent in meaning and truth conditions.
C) The first requires the subject to exist, the second does not.
D) Neither can be true because the subject does not exist.

Key Takeaway
The key logical insight is that negation can be true of non-existent subjects, while affirmation always requires some mode of existence for the subject.
